ABSTRACT 
 
Purpose: The purpose of this thesis is to explore how Nordic Small and Medium 
Enterprises (SME) can deploy Open Innovation practices to address challenges of Smart 
City initiatives in Singapore. The author hopes to investigate the insufficiently researched 
area between Open Innovation and Smart Cities, by researching the Business Model 
Innovation of SMEs to Singapore. 
Method: This thesis applied an extensive survey of prior literature and an empirical 
study. The author chose seven case firms and four case intermediaries that use an 
innovation lab, to internationalize and address challenges of the Smart City initiatives in 
Singapore. Primary data were collected by semi-structured interviews in English at 
various locations in 2018. Furthermore, qualitative data was used, to conceptualize the 
topics of Open Innovation and Smart Cities by a Social Network analysis. 
Findings: In all, many similarities and differences to prior research could be identified. 
This thesis presents several conceptual connections between the deployment of Open 
Innovation and Smart Cities and contextualizes both topics. The Social Network Analysis 
shows many avenues and possibilities of how Nordic SMEs can deploy OI practices, to 
overcome the challenges of Singapore’s Smart City challenges. Empirical research also 
indicates that Nordic SMEs are engaging in various forms of OI practices that are aiming 
to facilitate their Business Model Innovation in Singapore and ultimately address the 
challenges of the Smart City initiatives in Singapore. 
Originality/ value: This thesis contributes to the fields of research in Open Innovation, 
Business Model Innovation, and Smart Cities, and advances our understanding by 
combining the topics. The findings provide explorative insights into the innovation work 
of SMEs, and the results of this thesis are used to and translated into practical 
managerial implications for businesses and policymakers. 
 
Keywords: Smart Cities, Open Innovation, Singapore, Nordics, Business Model 
Innovation, SME

1 INTRODUCTION 
In recent years, research in the field of Smart Cities (SC) has considerably increased 
due to the rise of global challenges that cities are facing all over the world, such as an 
increase of population and scarcity of resources (Alawadhi et al., 2012; Chandrasekar, 
Bajracharya, & O'Hare, 2016; Neirotti, De Marco, Cagliano, Mangano, & Scorrano, 
2014). Nowadays, governments have realized that the challenges of cities are changing 
rapidly. As we humans are in rising need of clean drinking water, as we create more 
waste, and are in demand for universal access to renewable energies, modern cities 
must start to think differently of how to solve such problems (Hollands, 2008). 
Current challenges require governments, cities, and its citizens to address the city’s 
difficulties in a different way (Hollands, 2008). New digital technologies are creating 
several opportunities for cities to meet such issues. For example, open data that is 
shared among citizens could improve parking situations in bigger cities, and data 
analytics could help to assist policymakers to devise long-term strategies. In pursuit of 
such innovative solutions, cities and governments are adopting an Open Innovation (OI) 
approach by including technologies into every aspect of its city operations, including: 
public transportation, IT connectivity, water and power supply, sanitation, solid waste 
management systems, citizen participation, E-governance, and urban mobility 
(Chandrasekar et al., 2016). Novel technologies, such as Big Data, or Internet of Things 
(IoT) are helping to redesign the shape of modern cities. 
As one of the key players in SCs, Singapore has been opening its markets for foreign 
innovation and invites externals stakeholders through governmental funding, innovation 
labs, and other incentives to take part in innovating the South East Asian country 
(„Smart-Nation“, 2018). However, it is still poorly understood how foreign companies can 
collaborate with SCs and how they can address the challenges of the cities by offering 
their solutions. This lack of attention is significant because knowing how to deploy 
innovations in a SC context will provide both solutions for cities and governments that 
can address the increased volume and complexity of challenges and new market 
opportunities for businesses that plan to internationalize their Business Models (BM). 
For decades, scholars and authors discussed the concept of innovation. Since Henry 
Chesbrough’s concept of OI in 2003, the approach of OI has become increasingly 
==PAGE:11==
Introduction 
10 
important for both theory and practice over the last few years. However, the discussion 
is under-examining the context of SCs, and how OI could contribute to improving the way 
cities works. Especially Small and Medium Enterprises (SME) could be interested in 
deploying their innovative solutions by means of Open Innovation for SCs, since these 
might be capable of embracing the fact that cities often do not even know which solutions 
to ask for and are more flexible in their product offering than bigger corporates (Enkel, 
Gassmann, & Chesbrough, 2009). Many policymakers are looking for innovation to 
become a SC and often look to best practices in other countries. Denmark is a leading 
player in CleanTech solutions and building innovations and is cooperating with 
Singapore to exchange solutions from country to country. This collaboration has benefits 
for all involved stakeholders. Start-ups are enabled to enter new markets while 
Singapore benefits from sustainable business solutions, which shape the face of the city.  
Thus, this thesis aims to contribute to debates regarding SCs, OI, and Business Model 
Innovation (BMI), by exploring OI theories within SCs at a firm level in the Singaporean 
CleanTech sector and conceptualizing them through a Social Network Analysis. 
Furthermore, this thesis provides a contextualized research of OI in the context of SCs, 
based on qualitative data from a case study of Nordic businesses in the CleanTech 
sector. 
1.1 
Problem Discussion and Research Question 
There is already extensive literature on the topics of OI, BMI, and SCs, as outlined in the 
theory part of this thesis ( Smart Cities and Open Innovation,  Business Model 
Innovation). The prior work shows relevance for scholars in different fields. However, the 
literature review reveals a lack of research on the topic of OI and SCs.  
The challenges from a SCs project perspective often force governments to collaborate 
with other firms and to open themselves up for innovation coming from external 
stakeholders (see Challenges of Smart City). Often, SMEs play an essential role in the 
innovation work of a SC, but they too have challenges, which go hand in hand with the 
deployment of OI (see Open Innovation challenges and best practices for SMEs). The 
combination of both topics and the restrictive challenges in both fields of research show 
many similarities as seen in Figure 1: Comparison of Smart City challenges and 
challenges of OI for SMEs. Both fields of research are related, and the restrictive 
challenges can be resolved as scholars suggest. 
Literature shows that it remains a big challenge for many companies to define their 
strategy when they decide to internationalize and how an enterprise delivers value to 
customers (Teece, 2010). Moreover, SMEs are often the sources of innovative solutions 
==PAGE:12==
Introduction 
11 
for solving urgent issues facing cities around the world. However, lack of strategic 
orientation and access to OI mechanisms limit their options to scale and extract more 
value from innovation and, ultimately, realize their full market potential. Additionally, the 
literature on OI for SMEs has only investigated their challenges and best practices. 
These approaches and concepts have not yet been researched in a specific context, 
such as the SC context of Singapore.  
In collaboration with start-ups originating from the Technical University of Denmark 
(DTU) based innovation lab “Smart City World Labs (SCWL)”1, this thesis will focus on 
three pillars which consist of a detailed analysis on Danish/ Nordic SMEs, OI challenges 
and best practices for SMEs, and SCs. 
The present study seeks to fill this gap and is, therefore, guided by the following research 
question (RQ): 
 
“How can Danish/ Nordic SMEs deploy Open Innovation practices to address 
challenges of Smart City initiatives in Singapore?”. 
 
The research question is to be answered through of a qualitative case study. The method 
of the qualitative analysis and its results are described and discussed in this thesis. 
Based on the findings of the qualitative case study, a Social Network Analysis will 
conceptualize the RQ. Grounded on the findings from the literature review, the Results 
and Discussion will investigate the research question further.

1.2 
Methodology, scope, and conditions 
This thesis gathered qualitative data through expert interviews of seven businesses and 
four intermediaries that are active in the deployment of OI practices for the SC initiatives 
in Singapore. The data for this thesis was gathered during the time frame of March to 
May 2018 (see chapter 4). Interviews are transcribed and taped on memos. The 
transcriptions are used to analyze and structure the interviews through a coding 
software, which enabled the description of the data, categorization according to theory 
and analyzation of the data to reflect on the frameworks used in this thesis. Furthermore, 
the qualitative data was used to conceptualize the topics of OI and SCs through a SNA. 
SMEs in this thesis are defined as all companies among start-ups and businesses of 
medium size. The terms venture, start-up, company, business, and SME are used 
interchangeably throughout this thesis. The term Nordics is defined as the Northern 
European countries, namely Denmark, Sweden, Norway, and Finland. 
In order to understand the concept of a SC, this thesis will outline a SC framework and 
explore the challenges that governments and city stakeholders are facing. Furthermore, 
this thesis defines OI and puts the concept and its advantages and barriers into the 
perspective of SMEs. 
Limitations of this thesis are to be seen in the short timeframe that is given due to the 
nature of this master thesis and the limited number of conducted interviews. 
Furthermore, this thesis focused on questioning interviewees out of Denmark and 
omitted a field trip to Singapore, where interesting contacts could have been interviewed,

which have expert insight into the SC initiatives of the city. Moreover, supplementary 
theories and fields of research could have been used to analyze the topic. 
An essential finding of this thesis highlights the importance of OI for the SC challenges 
in Singapore. This finding contributes to our understanding of how SMEs can use OI to 
deploy their innovations for SC challenges, and how OI can help to overcome barriers of 
SCs. Therefore, the theory aims to explain both concepts of SCs and OI in detail and 
shall clarify the existing knowledge on the barriers of both topics. 
1.3 
Structure of the Thesis 
This thesis is structured into seven chapters, which are comprised of two main parts, an 
in-depth review of the literature and an empirical study. The extensive survey of existing 
work covers diverse streams of research. Therefore, this thesis includes the continuum 
between slightly historical literature, for example on BMI and internationalization, as well 
as most recent publications on OI and SCs. The findings of the literature review are 
structured in two consecutive chapters. First, an overview of the fields of SCs and OI is 
presented, followed by the topics of BMI and internationalization. The empirical part of 
this thesis starts with an explanation of the applied method. Subsequently, the results 
and discussion of the empirical research are presented in consecutive chapters. Firstly, 
the sample of this researched is presented. Secondly, the results of theory and empirical 
research will be discussed, conceptualized, and contextualized in the context of 
Singapore. Finally, this thesis will conclude the presented work and give hints for 
potential avenues of future research.
==PAGE:15==

2 SMART CITIES AND OPEN INNOVATION 
The purpose of this section is to give an overview of the results of the conducted survey 
of the literature. The first part of this section deals with the literature on SCs. A framework 
that explains the relationships and influences within a SC is introduced, to provide a 
better understanding of this topic. Furthermore, the challenges of SC are reviewed, 
followed by an overview of the local context in Singapore and Copenhagen. The second 
stream of literature deals with the topic of business model innovation as a critical factor 
in the context of smart cities. At this point, it is of high importance to understand the role 
of open innovation in the development of new business models and to understand how 
and why companies internationalize their business models. 
2.1 
Smart Cities 
2.1.1 
The concept of a Smart City 
While it is evident that Information Technologies (IT) and creative industries can change 
the face of a city’s economic, social and spatial face (Hollands, 2008), one still must 
define the concept of a “Smart City”. Today in 2018, there is no agreed definition in the 
literature (Nam & Pardo, 2011). Problems that may arise in Dubai are different from the 
ones in Singapore. Even though the categories of the challenges are similar from city to 
city, solutions, and approaches for the challenges of the restrictive cites will be very 
specific. Therefore, instead of having a precise definition for a SC, characterizing 
attributes and features should be discussed to get a common ground for further 
discussions (Neirotti et al., 2014). 
The need for citywide planning and control, supported by central Information and 
Communication Technologies (ICT), is often stressed as one of the primary reasons to 
transform a city into a SC (Hollands, 2008). Processing real-time information and 
integrating data across multiple processes, systems, organizations, and value chains 
helps governments to be informed and to react to incidents within a short time (Neirotti 
et al., 2014). The benefit of using ICT systems for a city or government is comparable to 
the benefit it brings to an organization, it enhances the management and functioning of 
a city and increases its productivity (Chourabi et al., 2012). 
==PAGE:16==
Smart Cities and Open Innovation 
15 
Since ICT is unable to transform cities without human capital, an emphasis on the human 
dimensions of the city is needed when characterizing SC attributes (Hollands, 2008). 
Many SC initiatives welcome residents to participate in the governance and management 
of a city and support the importance of social learning, education, and social capital for 
the development of the city (Alawadhi et al., 2012). Cities, where an educated labor force 
is available, show the most rapid urban growth rates (Caragliu, Del Bo, & Nijkamp, 2011). 
Hollands (2008) stresses out, that smart cities should be based on human and social 
capital instead of focusing only on ICT, to achieve stability of using technology-based 
solutions and maintaining the humanness aspect. The role of human capital is, therefore, 
a key attribute for a SC. Moreover, cities that have a skilled, or educated workforce seem 
to attract more educated workers in the future than those who have a lower percentage 
of skilled workers (Caragliu et al., 2011). 
Finally, the term “smart” itself can create certain assumptions about the generated 
solutions a government provides. Giffinger and Pichler-Milanović (2007) define SCS as 
governments that pursue a forward-looking strategy that generates innovation in the way 
services, and communication is delivered to the local population. 
An example of two SC initiatives makes the complexity of the challenges governments 
face illustrative and shows how specific the generated solutions are outlined towards one 
city. Singapore launched its own SC campaign in 2014 (Smart Nation)2. One initiative 
was to add cameras throughout the city so that the government can effectively analyze 
crowd density, cleanliness of public spaces, and even the exact movement of every 
locally registered vehicle. Much of the data the city is collecting will be fed through ICT 
into an online platform called “Virtual Singapore”. This procedure will give the 
government access to how the city is functioning in real time. Therefore, generated data 
could help the government to predict how crowds might react to an explosion in a 
shopping mall, or how an infectious disease might spread throughout the city. 
In contrast, more than 50 smart services from 22 government-owned entities have been 
rolled out in Dubai as part of the governments “Smart Dubai” initiative3. Using the 
government’s provided app, DubaiNow4, users can pay their speeding tickets after 
having been captured on a public camera and having been emailed the restrictive user 
ticket directly. The same app can be used to pay a taxi driver, find ATMs near the user’s 
position, report a violation, track a visa status, or pay the monthly electric bill. 
                                                 
 
2 https://www.smartnation.sg/ 
3 http://www.smartdubai.ae/ 
4 https://dubainow.dubai.ae/en/Pages/default.aspx 
==PAGE:17==
Smart Cities and Open Innovation 
16 
Both Dubai and Singapore show similar attributes in their SC initiatives with regard to the 
use of ICT, the interaction with humans and use of human capital as well as the 
introduction of innovative service, but the ultimate applications differ significantly. Thus, 
smart cities offer entrepreneurial, social and community aspects of livable cities and 
combine the attributes of human capital, IT-based technologies, and innovation. A 
framework that puts these individual aspects into perspective is described in the next 
chapter. 
2.1.2 
Smart City Framework 
Having described the concept of a SC, the following paragraph will discuss a SC 
framework to explain the relationships and influences that occur within a SC initiative. A 
framework helps to assess and compare different SC initiatives and the role of the 
stakeholders, that are involved in the transformation of cities (Chourabi et al., 2012). 
Factors within the framework have a two-way impact on SC initiatives and are most likely 
to be affected by other factors. Some factors might be more influential than others, 
depending on the context (Joshi, Saxena, & Godbole, 2016). 
The vision of a SC initiative and execution towards the accomplishment of it is vital to 
ensure the success of the implemented solutions. The so-called SMELTS framework 
tries to explain the relationships between the elements that affect the ultimate success 
of a SC initiative (Joshi et al., 2016). According to the framework, social, management, 
economy, legal, technology and sustainability are the core elements of a SC that enable 
public and private institutions to organize and implement the transformation of a city 
(Joshi et al., 2016). According to Chourabi et al. (2012) and Joshi et al. (2016), there are 
external and internal factors influencing the outcome of a SC initiative. External factors 
include the governance, people, and communities, natural environment, infrastructure as 
well as the economy. These elements have a higher probability of being influenced than 
internal factors. Internal factors include technologies, management, and policies 
(Chourabi et al., 2012). Figure 2 summarizes the relationships of the SMELTS 
framework and puts it into perspective with the internal and external factors. 
==PAGE:18==
Smart Cities and Open Innovation 
17 
 
Figure 2: SMELTS framework for Smart City initiatives, based on Chourabi et al. (2012) 
and Joshi et al. (2016) 
2.1.3 
Challenges of Smart City initiatives 
Even though SCs have a positive impact on the physical patterns, layouts, and structures 
that make up an urban center, meaning its urban form (Hollands, 2008), there are 
limitations and challenges related to SC initiatives, which are summarized in Figure 3: 
• 
When looking at a SC initiative from the project perspective, the project size is an 
essential factor that can affect the strategy of a smart initiative. Joshi et al. (2016) 
view the project size as a challenge that should be balanced with the team’s 
competences. 
• 
Another challenge faced by the management of SC initiatives is the definition of 
clear goals. Multiple or conflicting goals often result in paralysis that leads to no 
action at all. Governments can overcome this challenge by having clear agendas 
and measurable deliverables (Joshi et al., 2016).  
• 
Subsequently, governments may face resistance to change as one major social 
challenge, that can be overcome with skilled communication and trust (Joshi et 
al., 2016).  
Social
Technology
Legal
Economy
Smart City 
Initiative
Sustainability
Management
==PAGE:19==
Smart Cities and Open Innovation 
18 
• 
Moreover, SCs may face challenges in the organization as they lack cross-
sectional coordination between department and lack of effective leadership that 
supports commitment amongst officials (Ebrahim & Irani, 2005). Consequently, it 
is necessary to employ people with socials skills. 
• 
Next, to organizational and social challenges, governments might face a 
technological challenge. The introduction of new ICT in a SC context is linked to 
professional skills and may result in a lack of IT skills (Ebrahim & Irani, 2005). 
According to Alawadhi et al. (2012), governments can overcome this challenge 
by offering IT training programs, and by hiring employees with the necessary 
skills. 
• 
However, not just the implementation of ICT can be a challenge for governments, 
threats from hackers, viruses, worms, and other intruders are a constant threat 
to SC initiatives that go hand in hand with the high cost of security applications 
and solutions (Alawadhi et al., 2012). 
• 
Finally, governments might face various legal, compliance, and political 
challenges. Political constituents (city council, government, and utilities) and 
external restrictions, such as the legislation, or political agendas may affect the 
outcomes of SC initiatives (Alawadhi et al., 2012). Analyzing the project, 
understanding the resource requirements, and attaining necessary approvals is, 
therefore, a crucial step for governments before the SC initiation (Joshi et al., 
2016). 
==PAGE:20==
Smart Cities and Open Innovation 
19 
 
Figure 3: Challenges of Smart City initiatives 
Summarizing, governments that want to transform their cities into SCs face numerous 
challenges in many dimensions. Continuous analysis of the organizational, social, 
technological, and political dimensions of the project is necessary, to build a functioning 
SC. 
Often, governments provide specific areas or regions within a city, where first solutions 
and tests can be rolled out. These areas are referred to as living labs (Cosgrave, 
Arbuthnot, & Tryfonas, 2013). Project-based organizations, which are either led by the 
public or private sector or by universities, can drive innovation and new product 
development within these living labs (Cosgrave et al., 2013). As part of this, they are in 
many cases dependent on OI practices and function as a marketplace for information 
that is built on an extensive and organic “system of systems” (Cosgrave et al., 2013). 
The following chapter will elaborate on the mechanisms, opportunities, and challenges 
of the OI approach and indicate that SMEs share similar challenges than SC initiatives, 
which will ultimately be the focus of the empirical investigation.

2.2 
Open innovation 
2.2.1 
Business Model Innovation through Open Innovation 
OI has become increasingly popular in many industries acting in different technology 
fields. Initially, the change of thinking stands for a shift in the way companies innovate 
and in the mindset behind the respective approaches. As Chesbrough defines it, OI is 
the “antithesis of the traditional vertical integration model in which internal innovation 
activities lead to internally developed products and services that are then distributed by 
the firm” (Chesbrough, 2006, p. 20). Chesbrough’s definition already shows two 
significant differences in comparison to the traditional closed innovation model: Firstly, 
external and internal ideas can enter the innovation process at any stage of the 
development. Secondly, all projects can go to market at various stages of the process 
and do not have to be fully developed within the company. Therefore, the concept of OI 
promotes the external path for BMIs. Companies and institutions that adopt the OI 
concept consider the same attention for internal and external ideas (Chesbrough, 2006). 
Since OI is the product of an evolutionary adaption, it builds on the theories of technology 
transfer and markets for technology. Therefore, instead of relying restrictively on internal 
skills and knowledge, institutions interact extensively with their environment in the form 
of commercialization of technological knowledge (Lichtenthaler, 2011). In the backdrop 
of the literature about SCs, firms, and institutions that apply OI use one of the three 
following types of OI, as introduced by Enkel et al. (2009): 
1) An inbound, or outside-in process of OI enriches the firm’s knowledge by the 
integration of external knowledge sourcing (Enkel et al., 2009). Therefore, a company or 
institution that applies the outside-in process opens up the innovation process to 
knowledge exploration from outside the company, as through customers, suppliers, and 
competitors as sources (Enkel et al., 2009). Often firms collaborate with public, 
commercial research institutions, or even city governments, in order to find new and 
innovative solutions (Gassmann, 2006).  
2) Contrary to the outside-in process, the outbound or inside-out process tries to find an 
answer to sell a firm’s ideas and resources on the market (Enkel et al., 2009). The 
outbound OI puts, therefore, a focus on opening up the firm’s strategy to knowledge 
exploitation (Lichtenthaler, 2011). This approach is often adopted by more prominent 
corporations that cannot use all inventions made internally, but still want to benefit 
financially from bringing ideas to market and exploiting knowledge (Chesbrough, 2006). 
3) Lastly, the coupled process is seen by Enkel et al. (2009) as a combination of the 
outside-in and inside-out process, that leads to a jointly developed and commercialized 
==PAGE:22==
Smart Cities and Open Innovation 
21 
innovation. Companies that adopt the coupled process develop a co-creation with 
complementary partners in forms of alliances, cooperation’s, and joint ventures (Enkel 
et al., 2009).  
2.2.2 
Opportunities of Open Innovation 
The OI approach involves specific benefits and challenges as suggested in the literature. 
For example, the implementation of the inbound process can increase a company’s 
innovativeness by leading to a more useful idea generation and fosters the market 
innovativeness as well as faster commercialization of technologies (Enkel et al., 2009; 
Laursen & Salter, 2006). Laursen and Salter (2006) identified in a large-scale sample of 
industrial firms that the use of the inbound process is more likely to achieve a higher level 
of innovative performance. Their study suggests that the performance increase is based 
on the access and use of external ideas and technologies, which allows them to increase 
the number of technological and innovative opportunities available to them. Moreover, 
the inbound process promotes spill-over and learning effects that are beneficial to a 
company’s invention and the number of inventions in general, and it opens the 
opportunity to outsource the Research and Development (R&D) and marketing function 
partially (Piller & Walcher, 2006). Additionally, the exploitation of external knowledge 
licensing can help companies to fill gaps in their capabilities and match internal 
capabilities (Chesbrough, 2006). 
The use of the outbound OI process offers a variety of benefits for institutions. Enkel et 
al. (2009) show in their study that time-to-market is considerably reduced by companies 
that adopt the outbound process, compared to an internal development process. 
Moreover, companies may benefit from additional revenue streams, by licensing or 
selling Intellectual Property (IP), and therefore contribute to the operating income. 
Furthermore, companies that use outbound OI create the opportunity to transfer ideas to 
other companies and, therefore, multiply their technologies (Enkel et al., 2009). Likewise, 
institutions that use the outbound process have a considerable impact on their product 
business and are often able to set new industry standards, due to the processes’ 
strategic dimension that focuses on the exploitation of external knowledge (Lichtenthaler 
& Ernst, 2007). 
The adoption of the coupled process for OI can lead to an increase in innovativeness, a 
reduction in costs and risks (of 60 to 90 percent) and simultaneously shorten innovation 
cycles (Enkel et al., 2009; Quinn, 2000). Enkel et al. (2009) discovered a positive 
relationship between the number of cooperation between a company and its 
innovativeness, which was measured by the number of filed patents. Consequently, 
==PAGE:23==
Smart Cities and Open Innovation 
22 
companies that do not cooperate with externals may lose their ability to enter exchange 
relations with other firms and face competitive shortcomings in a market (Enkel et al., 
2009). Enkel et al. (2009) summarize that the coupled process increases a company’s 
innovativeness and reduces its time-to-market. 
2.2.3 
Barriers to Open Innovation 
Even though there are a variety of benefits of using OI, companies still have to face 
particular challenges and barriers that prevent them to exploit the profits from their OI 
projects fully. 
First and foremost, companies that open up their R&D processes to external partners 
face the risk to lose internal technological knowledge. Next, they face higher coordination 
requirements with external partners and have to consider high transaction costs (Enkel 
et al., 2009). Generating additional profits through OI has to be traded off with lowered 
entry barriers and consequently higher competition (Lichtenthaler, 2008). Lichtenthaler 
(2008) warns companies with a “not invented here (NIH)” mindset of the danger of 
“missing the substantial monetary and strategic opportunities”. Nevertheless, companies 
with an NIH mindset should consider that if their competitors should exploit those 
potentials, they still might be forced to open up. Therefore, OI should not be seen as a 
complement to a company’s internal R&D, since it requires internal capabilities to set 
external technologies to use. Additionally, companies should consider that technology 
licensing and cooperation are coupled with high transaction costs for transferring 
technologies between organizations (Arrow, 1962), and possible dependence with 
external partners in a firm’s network and the consequent loss of control (Enkel et al., 
2009; Lichtenthaler, 2010). Moreover, the strong dependence on alliances, 
collaborations, and joint ventures might reduce a company’s opportunities to strategically 
respond to changes and adapt innovation processes (Lichtenthaler, 2010). This issue 
might become crucially important in the context of a SC project. 
Institutions that want to open up should consider their internal barriers that go beyond 
the loss of IP. Hence companies must find a suitable partner, a match between the OI 
initiatives and the daily business, and allocate time and capital to OI initiatives (Enkel et 
al., 2009). Likewise, companies should consider that OI bears the risk of lowering the 
motivation of its internal R&D employees, which might see OI activities as a threat to 
their work (Lichtenthaler, 2010). 
==PAGE:24==
Smart Cities and Open Innovation 
23 
2.2.4 
Open Innovation challenges and best practices for SMEs 
Often young ventures struggle to build innovations, due to their limited resources 
(Stanko, Fisher, & Bogers, 2017). Building up a company’s resources, or developing new 
products is often more consuming than cooperating with other companies. Therefore, 
SMEs habitually adopt OI approaches, to gain a substantial competitive advantage 
through the cooperation and co-development with other stakeholders, such as other 
start-ups, experts, and innovators. New opportunities for SMEs are enabled through new 
mechanisms of OI, and continuous paths to openness are continuously evolving. 
Nowadays, SMEs can take advantage of contemporary mechanisms of open innovation, 
such as innovation communities and platforms, social media, or crowdfunding (Stanko 
et al., 2017). However, not only the mechanisms of exploiting OI have changed in recent 
years, but also the possibilities for start-ups to discover and work with new and innovative 
ideas. Collaborations between larger corporations and SMEs are rapidly evolving. 
Neither large companies nor SMEs can or should develop and commercialize everything 
on their own. Therefore, the biggest argument to engage in OI is to make use of external 
knowledge instead of inventing innovations from scratch and making substantial internal 
investments (Chesbrough, 2006). For SMEs, authors see the advantages of OI in a 
higher rate of innovations, as well as lowered time-to-market (Enkel et al., 2009), the 
elimination of unnecessary costs and risks (Bianchi, Campodall'Orto, Frattini, & Vercesi, 
2010), as well as the boost of efficiency for OI activities, which holds true for large 
corporations (Gallini & Winter, 1985).  
However, authors and scholars highlight that there is still a visible gap in applying OI 
strategies in practice, especially for SMEs (Rahman, 2010), which have been researched 
first by van de Vrande, de Jong, Vanhaverbeke, and de Rochemont (2009). According 
to their research of 605 innovative SMEs, SMEs might use OI in several areas and 
ascend from activities such as spin-out, or spin-off processes from larger corporations, 
technology-, or Intellectual Property (IP) licensing, or customer and supplier 
engagement. For start-ups that adopt OI practices, IP plays a vital role as a consequence 
of in- and outflows of knowledge (van de Vrande et al., 2009). The exchange of IP has a 
significant mediating effect on an SME’s relationship between organizational inertia and 
business model innovation and the venture’s performance (Hossain & Kauranen, 2016). 
Hossain and Kauranen (2016) summarize, ventures that perform a radical innovation 
benefit the most by using technology sourcing (meaning the pursuit of implementing new 
technologies within a business’s strategic framework), and ventures that perform 
incremental innovation benefit the most of OI practices by using technology scouting (the 
active search for new and novel knowledge). 
==PAGE:25==
Smart Cities and Open Innovation 
24 
SMEs often face challenges with the OI approach, which are summarized in Figure 4:  
• 
Rahman (2010) sees an increasing degree of openness of a venture coupled with 
a slower product development-time,  
• 
and rising costs in comparison to average firm’s common projects.  
• 
According to Rahman (2010), the strong commitment to supporting innovations 
within SMEs and the actual processes whereby start-ups commence innovative 
activity remains unclear. It is clear that smaller ventures are weaker than larger 
firms in OI regarding resource scarcity, unsystematic innovation approaches, the 
complexity of fields, inadequate coordination of innovation activities and the lack 
of access to knowledge specialists inside the firm (Hossain & Kauranen, 2016).  
• 
One high barrier of OI that SMEs perceive are summarized by van de Vrande et 
al. (2009), who identified the main barriers of innovation, for small ventures, which 
are related to venturing, external participation, and outsourcing of R&D. For 
example, when cooperating with other companies, or during the involvement with 
external parties, small ventures often experience culturally related barriers, that 
can lead to problems, such as the division of task and responsibility, or 
communication problems (van de Vrande et al., 2009).  
• 
Contradictory to other authors (Bianchi et al., 2010), van de Vrande et al. (2009) 
show that scarcity of time and resources are not perceived to be the most limiting 
barriers for OI practices in SMEs. Instead, administrative challenges seem to 
occur more often and are often present when companies receive government 
subsidies and funds (van de Vrande et al., 2009).  
• 
Subsequently, smaller ventures perceive government support as highly inflexible, 
and it often hinders companies to enter or change further partnerships and OI 
collaborations (van de Vrande et al., 2009).  
• 
Other barriers to OI practices for SMEs are seen in the partner selection. When 
ventures choose to collaborate with external partners in the innovation process, 
they perceive that these partners often do not match their expectations, or do not 
deliver the necessary quality of a product or service (van de Vrande et al., 2009).  
• 
Additionally, ventures in the study of van de Vrande et al. (2009) see challenges 
in the OI collaboration with users that are connected to IP rights, and too specific 
customer demands that result in unclear goals for the ventures.  
• 
Lastly, a venture’s employees might result in a barrier to implement OI, due to 
low motivation, skills, or capabilities (van de Vrande et al., 2009). 
==PAGE:26==
Smart Cities and Open Innovation 
25 
 
Figure 4: Open Innovation challenges for SMEs 
To sum up, there has already been a considerable amount of research on the restrictive 
topics of OI practices for SMEs and smart cities. Many barriers for OI in SMEs are related 
to a company’s culture and organization. In the backdrop of the literature about smart 
cities, we see many similarities in the challenges a company, or a city, has to face when 
applying OI practices. However, a literature review has not resulted in insights that 
connect both topics of smart cities and OI, and there is still need for further research, as 
van de Vrande et al. (2009) and (Rahman, 2010) point out. Based on the findings from 
the literature review, the following section will derive the research question for this study.

3 BUSINESS MODEL INNOVATION 
3.1 
Business Model Innovation 
The second pillar of the literature review is the concept of Business Model Innovation, 
and a short overview will be given in this section. 
3.1.1 
Business Model 
BMI is a concept that is often used in literature (Chesbrough, 2006; Morris, Schindehutte, 
& Allen, 2005; Osterwalder & Pigneur, 2010; Teece, 2010), but finding a commonly 
accepted definition for the term “Business Model (BM)” leads to a broad diversity of 
understandings and usages. However, Morris et al. (2005) identified three general 
categories of definitions for a BM that can be labeled as economic, operational and 
strategic, each with an individual set of variables. Ventures can use a business model to 
represent a framework that helps to identify opportunities, by applying a systematic 
process (Girotra & Netessine, 2014). One of the most commonly used frameworks for 
the conceptualization of a company is the Business Model Canvas (BMC) by 
Osterwalder and Pigneur (2010, p. 15ff), that aims to describe the rationale of how an 
organization created, delivers, and captures value. The BMC consists of nine building 
blocks (value proposition, customer segments, channels, revenue streams, key 
resources, key activities, key partnerships, and cost structure) that cover the four main 
areas of a business: customers, offer, infrastructure, and financial viability (Osterwalder 
& Pigneur, 2010). 
The BMC is intended to function as a blueprint to help entrepreneurs to strategize their 
current business with the purpose to promote a general understanding, further 
discussions, creativity and an analysis of their business model. The fundamental 
assumption of the BMC is to: “show the logic of how a company intends to make money” 
(Osterwalder & Pigneur, 2010, p. 15). Figure 5 illustrates the BMC and the interrelations 
of the nine building blocks. 
==PAGE:28==
Business Model Innovation 
27 
 
Figure 5: Business Model Canvas (Osterwalder & Pigneur, 2010) 
Besides illustrating the current BM, the BMC itself can be a subject of innovation (Trimi 
& Berbegal-Mirabent, 2012), as shown in the next chapter. 
3.1.2 
Business Model Innovation 
BMI is about fundamentally rethinking a company’s business, including its customer 
needs, realigning its resources, processes and revenue streams with a new value 
proposition (Johnson, Christensen, & Kagermann, 2008). Exploiting innovations does 
not necessarily require a new BM. However, the essence of BMI is to find radically new 
customer value propositions and to solve a problem that has never been solved before 
(Johnson et al., 2008). As Mitchell and Bruckner Coles (2004, p. 14) put it into 
perspective, BMIs are “business model replacements that provide product or service 
offerings to customers and end users that were not previously available”. Osterwalder 
and Pigneur (2010, p. 244) suggest BMIs originate from one of four objectives: to satisfy 
existing but unanswered market needs; to bring new technologies, or services to market; 
to improve, disrupt, or transform an existing market with a better model, or; to create an 
entirely new market. 
The innovation of a company’s BM is especially significant in times of instability since 
BMI can provide new ways to break out of intense competition (Lindgardt, Reeves, Stalk, 
& Deimler, 2009). Furthermore, BMI can help to address disruptions, such as regulatory 
or technological changes within markets that open opportunities to offer new competitive 
approaches. BMI can, furthermore, help to address recessive opportunities that leverage 
companies to reinvent themselves (Lindgardt et al., 2009). Therefore, BMI is an essential 
capability for every firm seeking success in a market. For the development of new BMs, 
==PAGE:29==
Business Model Innovation 
28 
Lindgardt et al. (2009) suggest that a company should start to assess the current 
business context, the needs of its customers, and the models of its competitors. A 
common framework for the three-step analysis is the BMC as presented above, that 
helps to get clarity to reveal what is currently working, and what is not, and ultimately, 
what constitutes a better value proposition. 
 
A comprehensive framework that eases the broad and detailed approach of Osterwalder 
and Pigneur (2010) and supports managers and entrepreneurs to control their innovation 
work is the so-called 4I-framework (Frankenberger, Weiblen, Csik, & Gassmann, 2013). 
According to the framework, entrepreneurs can: “structure the business model innovation 
process and highlight the specific challenges which managers face during the initiation, 
ideation, integration, and implementation of new business models.” (Frankenberger et 
al., 2013, p. 19). A firm’s business model can, therefore, be structured through four 
phases: ignition, ideation, integration, and implementation. During these four phases, a 
venture develops and conceptualizes itself around four axial dimensions, which are: “the 
Who, the What, the How, and the Why.” (Frankenberger et al., 2013, p. 5). 
The “Who” describes the customer group of a restrictive business to which it wants to 
serve its BM. The “What” refers to the value proposition (Osterwalder & Pigneur, 2010) 
and involves an overall view of all values that a company offers towards its customers in 
forms of products and services. According to the framework, the “How” refers to the 
processes and activities that a firm has to perform, to serve its customers (Frankenberger 
et al., 2013). Finally, Frankenberger et al. (2013) conceptualize the “Why” as the fourth 
dimension of a company’s BM, which includes a venture’s cost structure, and the 
incoming revenue streams. Figure 6 summarizes the central dimensions of a company’s 
BM, which simplifies the core construct of Osterwalder and Pigneur (2010) and helps to 
strategize a company’s BMI by finding new ways of creating and capturing value 
(Frankenberger et al., 2013). 
==PAGE:30==
Business Model Innovation 
29 
 
Figure 6: The 4I-framework, own illustration based on (Frankenberger et al., 2013) 
According to the framework, finding new ways of creating and capturing value is made 
by changing one, or more components of the central dimensions (Who, How, What, Why) 
(Frankenberger et al., 2013). However, a question that remains unanswered is whether 
companies which internationalize into new markets and, therefore, change the "How" are 
automatically approved of being innovative?  
Thus, a company’s ability to innovate its BM is particularly essential in a context of an 
international market, where markets can be volatile, or BM needs to adapt due to rapid 
social changes and instability of the domestic policy. Chapter 3.1.3 will discuss how 
SMEs can internationalize their BMs, to accelerate internal innovation. 
3.1.3 
Internationalization 
Small businesses have grown all over the world, and today there is no longer an 
exclusive preserve of more prominent corporate brands for markets. The internet has 
opened many ways for SMEs to trade all over the world. Ventures are motivated by 
various factors to internationalize, which can be divided into two categories: companies 
being pulled and companies being pushed into foreign markets (Madsen & Servais, 
1997). Pull factors are motivating factors of an attractive market that pull a firm to go 
global. Attractive markets motivate companies to internationalize, by offering higher 
profitability, cheap labor, growth prospects, or the possibility of international sourcing of 
funds (Oviatt, McDougall, & Loper, 1995). Oviatt et al. (1995) explain the interest of SMEs 
Who?
What?
How?
Why?
==PAGE:31==
Business Model Innovation 
30 
to go global: “the current and increasingly global nature of demand in many markets“ 
Oviatt et al. (1995, p. 33). Push factors refer to the compulsion of a firm’s domestic 
market, which motivates ventures to internationalize. For example, a saturated market 
might compel a firm to go global into new markets. 
The process that firms undertake to internationalize their businesses is referred to as 
internationalization 
(Susman, 
2007). 
Authors 
have 
developed 
several 
internationalization theories that aim to explain why there are international activities. 
However, two theories are most prominent when it comes to explaining the 
internationalization process of a firm. The first of which is the Uppsala model, which 
describes how firms gradually intensify their activities in foreign markets (Elgar, 2003; 
Johanson & Vahlne, 1977; Johanson & Wiedersheim‐Paul, 1975). The authors of the 
Uppsala model believe that other than the cultural differences the internal foundations of 
a firm are needed to handle internationalization activities. The gradual increase of 
activities are characterized through the psychic distance and the establishment chain 
(Johanson & Vahlne, 1977), whereas the psychic distance is defined as: “the perceived 
degree of difference between two markets, in terms of differences in language, culture 
and political system, which disturbs the flow between the firm and the market” 
(Hollensen, 2007, p. 73). Ventures that undertake an internationalization, are extensively 
involved in an interplay between the acquisition, integration, and use of knowledge about 
foreign markets and operations, and the increasing commitment of resources to those 
markets (Johanson & Vahlne, 1977). Therefore, the model distinguishes the 
internationalization process of a firm in four different steps, which cannot be viewed 
independently of a company’s situation, market, and market knowledge: “(1) no regular 
export activities (sporadic export), (2) export via independent representatives (export 
modes), 
(3) 
establishment 
of 
a 
foreign 
sales 
subsidiary, 
(4) 
foreign 
production/manufacturing units” (Hollensen, 2007, p. 74). Figure 7 illustrates the 
internationalization of a firm according to the four-step approach of the Uppsala model. 
As seen in the figure, firms tend to intensify their internationalization activities as their 
experience grows (Hollensen, 2007, p. 72). 
==PAGE:32==
Business Model Innovation 
31 
 
Figure 7: Internationalization of the firm after the Uppsala model, based on Hollensen 
(2007, p. 75) 
The development of ICT has made it easier for a firm to become acquainted with foreign 
markets and authors and scholars consequently aimed to consider these changes. Thus, 
a complementary model of internationalization has been considered since the 90s, the 
“born global” theory. According to Hollensen (2007, p. 87), a born global firm can be 
defined as: “a firm that from its inception pursue a vision of becoming global and globalize 
rapidly without any preceding long-term domestic or internationalization period”. From 
the beginning of the venture creation born global firms are focused and committed to 
being international. Firms that follow the born global approach begin with a borderless 
world-view and develop strategies to go abroad. ICT enables these ventures to trade all 
over the world (Hollensen, 2007, p. 87). According to Oviatt and McDougall (1994), born 
global firms have a firm reliance on alternative governance structures, to access 
resources, and are based on vanguard technology that ultimately enables product and 
process innovations on a global scale. Hollensen (2007, p. 87) states that born global 
firms are characterized by having a visionary entrepreneurial leader within the firm, who 
sees the world as one big and borderless marketplace.  
Since born global firms most often have limited scale of operations worldwide, they are 
often forced to choose a business area with a similar and minimal adaption of the 
marketing mix (Hollensen, 2007, p. 88). Furthermore, these firms are often more 
vulnerable, due to their dependency on a single product that is commercialized in lead 
markets first (Hollensen, 2007, p. 88). 
Hollensen (2007, p. 89) compares the Uppsala model and the born global model as two 
extreme pathways of internationalization, whereas the Uppsala model is seen as an

4 METHOD AND DATA 
Based on the theoretic foundation of the previous chapter, this chapter proceeds with 
the second part of the thesis, the presentation of the empirical material. In doing so, the 
purpose and nature of this research will be explained at first. Subsequently, the research 
design and methodology will be explained. Successively, a brief overview of an outline 
of the Singaporean market context will be illustrated. Finally, the data collection and 
analysis will be discussed in detail. 
4.1 
Purpose and nature of research 
As noted earlier, the overall topic of SCs and OI is not thoroughly researched yet and 
therefore prior research, especially with a focus on OI practices in Singapore, is relatively 
limited. The purpose of this thesis is, therefore, to examine the innovation and 
entrepreneurship work of Nordic SMEs, meaning the countries: Sweden, Denmark, 
Finland, and Norway. Thus, this thesis provides academic work to an understudied field.  
The use of OI to innovate Nordic SME’s BM will be analyzed in the context of the SC 
initiatives in Singapore. As discussed in chapter “1.1 Problem Discussion and Research 
Question” and illustrated in Figure 1, both topics, OI and SC share similar challenges 
and opportunities and are key drivers of innovation for SMEs. The question remains open 
if OI might be the linkage to overcome these challenges on both sides. This thesis is, 
therefore, explorative by nature. Thus, a qualitative case study approach has been 
chosen for the empirical research, due to its ability to explore, describe, and explain a 
phenomenon (Eisenhardt, 1989, p. 536). By using a qualitative case study, this thesis 
aims to encompass explanatory and descriptive elements and to explore and explain the 
relationship between the concepts of OI, BMI, and SCs in the context of Singapore. 
4.2 
Research Design 
As this thesis is of explorative nature, an iterative research approach was seen to be 
favorable, to allow flexibility to move back and forth between theory, data and the 
analysis of data. The iterative approach was not seen as a repetitive task, but as a 
reflexive process, to engage with continuous meaning-making (Srivastava & Hopwood, 
2009). By drawing on the logic of abductive inference, an iterative approach enables the 
==PAGE:35==
Method and Data 
34 
researcher to modify the original analytical framework and serves to validate the criteria 
for determining the plausibility and credibility of results (Srivastava & Hopwood, 2009).  
In the case of this thesis, inspiration was found in the innovation lab SCWL. The program 
aims to introduce and co-innovate technologies and solutions from the Nordic region to 
meet specific challenges and problem statements, identified together with crucial SC 
players in Singapore. SCs, BMI and green technologies are growth areas for the public 
sector in Singapore and advance the overall competitiveness of the country, while 
improving the quality of life for their citizens. Thus, the problem field was outlined, and 
academic literature of the fields was studied, to gain an understanding of the concepts 
and relevant theories. Also, it was found that empirical data was of critical importance in 
outlining the final analytical framework. After gathering all data and gaining an overview 
of the information, this thesis was able to build the initial analytical framework. In the 
process of investigating the topic, this thesis generated a better understanding between 
concepts and context, which ultimately led to changes in the initial framework. This 
approach led this thesis to identify specific patterns and characteristics of Nordic SMEs 
that plan to deploy OI practices to address the challenges of SC initiatives in Singapore, 
which will be presented in the following chapters. 
As Dubois and Gadde (2002) put it in perspective, the constant confrontation of theory 
with the empirical data and analysis throughout the research process builds more in the 
refinement of existing theories than on inventing new ones. This perspective is in line 
with the approach of the thesis, as it is not aiming at generating new fields of research, 
but rather examining and combining the two building blocks of OI and SCs in an 
understudied context. This qualitative loop-like pattern (Srivastava & Hopwood, 2009), 
which combines an exploratory study with an iterative approach, can be investigated by 
using a case study strategy (Dubois & Gadde, 2002) and a Social Network Analysis, 
which will be discussed in chapter 4.4. 
4.3 
Understanding the context of Singapore 
To understand the context of Singapore, this thesis will introduce the country context and 
introduce the market for non-experienced readers, since this is essential to understand 
the results and analysis in chapter 5 and 6. 
The SC concept of Singapore differentiates itself significantly from other cities. The 
government initiated numerous projects iteratively from the 1960s and started to include 
technologic development since the 1980s. The island located in Southeast Asia has a 
multiracial population of 2.8 million people, on a land mass of approximately 250 square 
miles and offers one of the highest living standards in the world with a per capita income 
==PAGE:36==
Method and Data 
35 
of 16.500 USD (Choo, 1997). The country is located at the transaction point of different 
transportation nodes, has limited natural resources, and Singaporeans have learned 
over the decades to combine their skills and conscientiousness with education and 
technology, to stay competitive and support the economic growth of the state (Choo, 
1997). 
The foundation of the SC vision is embodied in the governments IT2000 plan, which 
plans to transform Singapore into an intelligent island with an advanced nationwide ICT 
infrastructure that interconnects computers with nearly every home. One essential 
element of achieving this goal was to invest heavily in Research & Development (R&D). 
The city strategized in 1991 the shift of Singapore’s ideological transformation to be 
unfolded in three steps (Choo, 1997). First, the IT initiative funded by the public sector 
targeted to digitalize government ministries and public services (1981-85). Second, a 
shift from the public to private sector enabled through the National Technology Plan 
(1985-90). Third, the transformation towards a “smart island” that connects every home, 
entitled through the IT2000 masterplan (Hollands, 2008). This vision is updated since 
2014 by Singapore’s Smart Nation5 program, founded by the government. According to 
the Smart Nation program, Singapore is not only meant to become an interconnected 
island, but a city with an urban identity, that is grounded on sustainability, economy, 
environment, and equality („Smart-Nation“, 2018).  
The government focuses on five critical domains in which digital technology can have a 
critical impact: Technology; Home and Environment; Business productivity; Health and 
enabled aging; and public-sector services. The initiative sees citizens and businesses as 
critical stakeholders to impactful co-create solutions that address these five key domains. 
Singapore’s government aims to facilitate innovation by the public and private sector, to 
drive innovation in the five essential domains of the country. Three enablers shall 
facilitate innovations within the city. Therefore, Singapore’s government facilitates smart 
solutions and nurtures a culture of experimentation and sustaining innovation, while 
building on computational capabilities („Smart-Nation“, 2018). For example, businesses 
can test solutions with residents in the Yuhua estate in the “Smart HDB Town 
Framework”6, which serves as a testbed for new and innovative solutions. With the 
framework, residents can give feedback, to evaluate a solution’s viability, suitability, and 
                                                 
 
5 http://www.smartnation.sg/ 
6 
http://www20.hdb.gov.sg/fi10/fi10296p.nsf/PressReleases/F93B15F80588397748257D500009
CE6C?OpenDocument 
==PAGE:37==
Method and Data 
36 
integration into the everyday lives. Collaborative and coordinated testbeds, living labs 
shall enable knowledge sharing, innovative ventures, and projects, that will accelerate 
sustainable solutions for the transformation into a SC. 
Furthermore, Singapore’s innovation is triggered by its robust start-up ecosystem. 
Accelerators (for example JTC Launchpad, BASH, Cleantech Park), venture capitalists 
(VCs) (for example B Capital Group, Golden Gate Ventures, Sequoia, Rakuten 
Ventures), and Multinational Corporations (MNC) build a solid network that nurtures 
young companies and bridges innovations with corporates („Smart-Nation“, 2018). 
Singapore’s start-up ecosystem encompasses 1.600 up to 2.400 ventures, many of 
which are supported by the government’s 13.2 billion USD R&D initiative called GovTech 
(Genome, 2017). According to the annual global start-up ecosystem report by Genome 
(2017), Singapore ranks as the world’s city number one regarding talent even before 
Silicon Valley. Founders show an average prior start-up experience of two years 
(Genome, 2017). Prior mentioned VCs and accelerators have a keen interest in 
Singapore’s start-up ecosystem, and investors are continuously capitalizing more into 
start-ups, as the total funding volume increased with a Compound Annual Growth Rate 
(CAGR) of 55 percent7. Singapore’s CleanTech sector is still under development, and 
only 2 percent of tech start-ups are located in the industry that aims to reduce reliance 
on non-renewable resources and promotes sustainability (Action Community for 
Entrepreneurship, 2017). Figure 9 illustrates the distribution of Singapore’s tech start-
ups by sector. A summary of the Singapore start-up ecosystem can be found in Appendix 
A (Singapore start-up ecosystem map 2017). 
                                                 
 
7 Funding from 2011 to 2016  
Calculation of CAGR: ((1190/133)^(1/5) -1 = 55% 
==PAGE:38==
Method and Data 
37 
 
Figure 9: Distribution of tech start-ups in Singapore by sector (Action Community for 
Entrepreneurship, 2017) 
Singapore’s geographical constraints for becoming a world-leading SC are in being a 
small city-state, having an area for development that is limited to the island, and the 
scarcity of resources in the area (Lee, Kwon, Cho, Kim, & Lee, 2016). Additionally, 
Cordeiro and Al-Hawamdeh (2001) observe a disadvantage of having an “info-elitists”, 
meaning a group of people with a certain degree of information advantage which divides 
the people with knowledge in IT and the people that do not, and therefore creates a 
digital divide inside the society. The digital divide might result in inequity of the use and 
impact of ICT that leaves unskilled and unqualified workers behind and makes them 
unemployable. 
Singapore faces many working areas, in which the government is extensively trying to 
innovate and optimize. Two of those are found in the Water- and Waste-Management of 
the city. The island is under constant pressure to provide its citizens with sufficient water 
resources because Singapore has no natural aquifers or groundwater (Luan, 2010; 
Tortajada, 2006). The lack of water resources results in a strong dependence on the 
neighboring countries. In recent years Malaysia has become the most significant water 
importer for Singapore (Tortajada, 2006). New and innovative solutions shall rethink how 
==PAGE:39==
Method and Data 
38 
Singapore can manage its water scarcity. The Public Utility Board (PUB) plays a 
significant role in this matter and introduce novel sources of water collection, such as 
rainwater collection, recycled water, and desalination (Tortajada, 2006).  
The demand for disposal of waste rose from 1260 tons per day in 1970 to 8559 in 2016 
in Singapore8. Again, finding new and innovative solutions for the Waste-Management 
sector in Singapore is a must, and recycling has become an essential part of the 
everyday lives of the citizens (Bai & Sutanto, 2002). To manage the solid waste within 
the country, the National Environment Agency (NEA) has set up several initiatives that 
include a solid waste collection system, the incineration of solid waste, sanitary 
landfilling, as well as general waste minimization plans (Bai & Sutanto, 2002). 
Summarizing, because of its size Singapore always needed to stay one step ahead of 
the curve to deal with its challenges. The current SC initiatives and the growing start-up 
ecosystem are a significant step towards the transformation of the island to a SC. 
Nevertheless, Singapore is still in need of innovators, investors, and talent from the 
outside, that can help to shape the future face of the city (Lee et al., 2016).  
4.4 
Case Study Research 
For the empirical investigation of this thesis, a case study research was chosen to 
examine in-depth relations between the phenomena in focus. The case study research 
strategy can be defined as a research method that involves the investigation of one or 
“a small number of entities or situations about which data are collected using multiple 
sources of data and developing a holistic description through an iterative research 
process” (Easton, 2010, p. 119). According to Easton (2010), this research method 
allows applying a certain degree of flexibility, by which the researcher can continuously 
go back and forth between the different stages of research. 
There is a general distinction between quantitative and qualitative research when 
conducting a case study research. While quantitative research tries to collect data 
evidence based on numbers, the qualitative research explores data evidence based on 
words. A case study can be qualitative, quantitative, or both (Eisenhardt, 1989, p. 535). 
This thesis adopts a research method in the form of a qualitative case study based on 
expert interviews, due to the purpose of this research, which is to analyze how 
companies from the Nordic region, and especially Denmark, can deploy OI practices to 
address challenges of smart city initiatives in Singapore. In contrast to quantitative 
                                                 
 
8 http://www.nea.gov.sg/energy-waste/waste-management 
==PAGE:40==
Method and Data 
39 
research, qualitative research requires less previous knowledge and is, thus, better 
suited for an explorative approach to a topic, and to analyze causal relations and to 
generate hypotheses. As Yin (2017) states it, the case study is an “empirical inquiry that 
investigates a contemporary phenomenon within its real-life context, especially when the 
boundaries between phenomenon and context are not evident” (Yin, 2017, p. 13). 
Although the method is academic, this field is also of great interest for practitioners, and 
the results of this thesis might be relevant for Danish/ Nordic SMEs and local 
stakeholders that are involved in SC initiatives. In order to find answers on the RQ, this 
thesis chose to apply an embedded single case study method as suggested by Yin 
(2017, p. 39). This case study strategy includes the analysis of embedded units of 
analysis and the dimension of context (Yin, 2017). Figure 10 illustrates the applied case 
study strategy. 
 
Figure 10: The embedded single case study method, based on (Yin, 2017) 
The RQ shall be answered by an investigation at a level of firms (Business A to N) and 
intermediaries (Intermediary A to N), which are all active or associated to the SCWL 
innovation lab (case), located at the Technical University of Denmark (DTU) in Lyngby, 
and are (SME1 – SME5) / or were (S1.1, S1.2, S2) active in the internationalization to 
Singapore (context). The qualitative approach to the case study involves the collection 
of data, which is mainly gathered by interviews and in the form of words. As Yin (2017) 
suggests, the case study method is applicable when the researcher wants to cover 
contextual conditions, due to the belief that these might be relevant to the phenomenon 
of study. Therefore, this thesis believes that the chosen method is well-suited to give 
insights and find answers about the RQ.

4.5 
Data collection 
As noted before, the collection of data was mainly done through interviews, which is a 
good method to discover and explore a phenomenon. Due to the limited timeframe, this 
thesis is limited in the number of interviews with companies and intermediaries in the 
SCWL. However, the ability to conduct and contact interviewees that are involved in the 
internationalization process towards Singapore can be credited to the affiliation with the 
SCWL innovation lab. Within the time frame of six weeks (20.03.2018 – 01.05.2018), in-
depth interviews were conducted. As seen in Appendix B  Timeframe of Interviews, this 
thesis was required to conduct interviews with businesses and intermediaries 
simultaneously throughout the interview phase. Interviews were conducted at the DTU, 
at the offices of the individual SMEs or intermediaries, or per telephone.  
In search of experts who would be willing and capable of giving insights and finding 
answers on the RQ, SCWL played an essential role as a “gatekeeper” (Saunders, 2006) 
and provided access to respondents. The SCWL stands between the governmental 
institutions in Denmark and Singapore and the SMEs that are participating in the program 
with the goal to internationalize in Singapore. The corresponding contact persons at the 
SCWL provided this thesis with a list of first 89 companies. The 89 companies were 
categorized in industries as follows: Water (23), Energy (37), Pollution (12), Mobility (11), 
Light (7), Urban planning (7), and Others (34). Those innovative Nordic startups and 
CleanTech SME’s that applied to the SCWL were offered a platform for entering the 
Singaporean market, based on articulated demand. Furthermore, companies were 
asked to frame their business offering towards three challenges. As a boundary, this 
thesis chose to interview CleanTech start-ups that were addressing the Water- and 
Waste-Management challenge offered by the SCWL: “EcoCampus 2020: Reduction in 
water and waste intensity & higher renewal energy adoption”. This delimitation led to a 
list of 25 SMEs, out of which five were chosen to participate in the SCWL innovation lab. 
All five SMEs are businesses located in Denmark. Based on this, this research started 
contacting companies and arranging initial interviews. Even though the SCWL is an 
innovation lab based in the Copenhagen area, many companies came from different 
areas in the Nordics, restrictively Denmark. Therefore, not all interviews were conducted 
in a personal interview, some interviewees made use of telephone calls, or Skype calls. 
Additionally, this thesis was able to contact the participants of the SCWL batch in 2017. 
Similar to the SCWL EcoCampus 2020 challenge, SMEs from last year were active in 
the Water and Waste-Management industry.  
The sampling of the businesses can be described as purposeful sampling, as they are 
groups, settings, and individuals where the process which is in the focus of this study is 
==PAGE:42==
Method and Data 
41 
most likely to occur (Denzin & Lincoln, 2011, p. 307). With the identification and selection 
of information-rich businesses-cases that are related to the RQ, this thesis generated a 
good understanding of what sample units are needed, and then approached potential 
businesses, to prove if they are suitable (Palinkas et al., 2015). In this research, suitability 
is based on the Nordic company’s desire to internationalize in Singapore and the will to 
use an innovation lab, such as the SCWL, that might help in their internationalization. In 
the sampling of this thesis one case did not meet the criteria; thus, one business (SME4) 
is instead classified as an agency that is advising other SMEs to formulate their vision 
statements. It is to mention that the role of the SCWL as a gatekeeper served as a pre-
check that resulted in a list of high potential SMEs that have a strong desire of entering 
the Singaporean market and deploying OI practices in doing so. 
Moreover, to get an understanding from a neutral point of view, intermediaries, such as 
consultants, universities, and governmental institutions were interviewed. Again, the 
SCWL played a substantial role in facilitating the contact towards these intermediaries. 
These intermediaries include the Danish consultancy Quercus Group, the EU’s climate 
innovation initiative Climate-KIC, as well as the Energy Research Institute at the 
Nanyang Technological University (NTU) in Singapore. This thesis also tried to contact 
two other governmental institutions in order to investigate the RQ from a political view 
(the Economic Development Board and Royal Danish Embassy in Singapore). However, 
these attempts were unsuccessful. As seen above, the field of research within the 
challenges and opportunities of SCs and OI has not been documented to a large extent. 
Thus, choosing these interview participants that are seen as experts in their fields, was 
crucial to find answers on the RQ.

4.6 
Expert interviews 
As noted earlier, this thesis applied expert interviews to find answers to questions of 
“how” and “why” (Meuser & Nagel, 2009; Yin, 2017). The expert interview is a semi-
structured type of interview led by a guide, which comprises openly formulated narration 
incentives (Meuser & Nagel, 2009). The semi-structured interview guideline helped to 
cover principal themes that are required to answer the RQ, but on the other hand, follow 
an open-ended approach where the interviewees can formulate their responses. 
In summary, this thesis conducted seven interviews with eight respondents from Nordic 
SMEs in the Water- and Waste-Management industry, one interview with an employee 
from Climate-KIC, two interviews with Consultants that were engaged in the SCWL 
innovation lab, and one interview with the Program Director of the Energy Research 
Institute at NTU (ERI@N). The interviews ranged from 36 minutes up to over an hour, 
with an average of 45 minutes. Table 2 gives an overview of the interviewees and their 
fields of expertise. 
The final interview guidelines (Question guide for companies, Question guide for 
institutional actors) consist of two key columns. The first column indicates the question 
block that narrates the research topic. The second column shall assess the research 
question in detail and tries to propose new theories, with the idea to stimulate the expert 
regarding the topic. 
The interviews conducted with businesses are structured around the themes: (A) the 
interviewees background, (B) the company’s entrepreneurial story and current setting, 
(C) the interviewees perception on Innovation and OI, (D) the interrelations happening in 
the context to Singapore as a SC, (E) their intention to internationalize and the process 
in doing so, (F) their general perception about the work of policymakers in Singapore 
and the context of the country itself. As Meuser and Nagel (2009) suggest, the question 
guide for companies (Question guide for companies) covers a broad range of topics and 
is intended to motivate the interviewee to come up with its own believes and ideas. 
Nevertheless, the RQ was the focus of the interviews. Therefore, a significant portion of 
the interview was focused on the two blocks of (C) innovation and OI, and (F) the SC 
context of Singapore. The chapter Results shows that the interview guideline was 
sufficiently deep for the analysis. 
The interviews conducted with intermediaries, including the SCWL consultants, the 
Singaporean university, as well as governmental institutions slightly differed from the 
interviews conducted with the businesses, to get a full understanding of the BMI of SMEs 
and how they deploy OI practices. Similarly to the interviews with the businesses, the 
interviews were conducted using a semi-structured interview guideline (Question guide 
==PAGE:47==
Method and Data 
46 
for institutional actors) that was adapted to the setting of the institutions and 
representatives that were interviewed. 
The locations and settings of the interviews varied, it was intended to conduct all 
interviews face-to-face. However, since many of the interviewed SMEs and 
intermediaries are spread-out through the world (Denmark, Sweden, Singapore), some 
interviews were held on Skype, or per telephone. All interviews started with a short 
introduction of the researcher and the topic and intention of this thesis and ended with 
the question to give general advice for SMEs that plan to internationalize in Singapore 
and want to use OI in doing so. The majority of the interviewees used this chance to give 
guidance for SMEs.
