4.7 
Data analysis 
Flick (2014) puts the approach of coding into perspective as follows: “the work with 
materials for generating concepts and for allocating excerpts of the material to 
categories. This is then followed by interpretation: how we understand and explain what 
the coding has revealed about the views in the field, their links among each other, their 
ties to context conditions, and the like” (Flick, 2014, p. 373). 
Following this guidance, this thesis recorded all interviews on tape. All recorded tapes 
were transcribed afterward. This thesis will present full transcripts of the interviews since 
these are of central importance to the data analysis. The transcript followed the 
guidelines of Flick (2014) who proposes a transcript that is easy to: “write, read, learn 
and to search”. Some dialects and grammar mistakes were corrected. See Appendix F 
for the full transcripts of the interviews.  
The transcripts of the interviews were analyzed in the evaluation tool MAXQDA. 
MAXQDA offers the researcher a platform to analyze text-form with line numbers, which 
is beneficial when paraphrasing interview lines in the chapter “Results”. Moreover, 
MAXQDA enabled this thesis to use the coding system for analysis purposes as shown 
in Figure 11: Coding with MAXQDA. Coding can help to build frameworks of thematic 
ideas about the data (Gibbs, 2008, p. 40). By using codes generated through MAXQDA, 
this thesis was able to describe the data, categorize it according to theory and analyze 
the data reflecting on the frameworks used in this thesis. It conducts a selective-coding 
method as suggested by Gibbs (2008, p. 50), that analyzes a core category, respectively 
the RQ that ties all other categories in theory into one story. Subsequently, the single 
codes are assigned to logical groups. See Appendix G for an overview of the coding 
results. In total, this thesis gave 547 codes, which are consequently clustered into the 
categories: Open Innovation, Smart Cities, Business Model Innovation, and the SC 
stakeholders. The chapter Results will use the code frequencies, to analyze the 
qualitative interviews in detail and find similarities or differences throughout the different 
interview units. 
==PAGE:51==
Method and Data 
50 
 
Figure 11: Coding with MAXQDA 
Although the aforementioned descriptive analysis with the focus on the data of the 
qualitative research was insightful, another method was needed to conceptualize the 
topics found in the sample. A tool that is highly suited for the conceptualization of 
concepts is Social Network Analysis (SNA). SNA is a statistical tool that is designed for 
describing and analyzing actors or concepts by their relations (Borgatti, Everett, & 
Freeman, 2002). SNA presents an opportunity to examine the relationship types among 
SCs, concepts of deploying OI, and its barriers. Moreover, this thesis uses SNA to 
identify relationships and measure the strength of these relationships. The strength of a 
tie between businesses and concepts is measured on ordinal scales (Scott, 2017). Using 
the SNA, it is possible to uncover underlying dynamics that may not be understood with 
a descriptive method only. The SNA was carried out with the UCINET software. The 
software enables a visual representation of social networks, to understand data and 
convey the results of the analysis (Borgatti et al., 2002). Thus, the visualization enables 
a qualitative interpretation of the network data, and to find conceptual linkages within the 
sample (Scott, 2017). For the purpose of this thesis, matrices were chosen by conceptual 
theme within OI, and SC (see Appendix E Social Network Analysis). The matrices 
summarized the qualitative data, by summarizing the mentioned concepts per 
interviewed business. The code frequencies of the interviewees establish relations. 
Therefore, a high code frequency of an individual concept indicates a strong relation of 
the business towards the concept. Since the relations are based on the code frequencies 
within one interview, the visualization of the SNA does not include directions of the 
==PAGE:52==
Method and Data 
51 
relations as they cannot be determined through the conceptualization of code 
frequencies. 
==PAGE:53==
 
 
 
52 
5 RESULTS 
The following chapter presents the findings of the qualitative analysis drawn with the help 
of the tool MAXQDA and the conceptualization with the help of a SNA. The results are 
attached to this thesis in the appendices. The findings are presented with the help of the 
line numbers that can be found in the left column of the transcripts. As described in the 
previous chapter, the goal of this thesis is to find answers to the RQ. The Results are 
based on the structuring of coding results as presented in Appendix G. 
This chapter will first present the key stakeholders that are involved in the SCWL case. 
Consequently, this thesis will explain the context of this case study, meaning the outline 
of the SC Singapore that will be illustrated from the perspective of the interviewees. 
Subsequently, this thesis will present the barriers to OI. Then, the ideas of the 
interviewees of how to deploy OI practices will be highlighted, and modes to bridge OI 
with the challenges of the SC Singapore will be indicated. Following this chapter, this 
thesis will discuss the results based on the findings in the chapter “ Discussion” and show 
if and how the RQ can be answered. 
5.1 
Introducing the stakeholders within the SCWL case 
The results of this thesis will start by introducing the institutions that are related to 
Singapore and the SCWL, to understand the various stakeholders and institutions within 
the SCWL case. The institutions in the context of the SC Singapore, as described by the 
interviewees, are clustered into three categories: public, private, and governmental. 
5.1.1 
Stakeholders in the government sector 
The policymaker as a stakeholder for the SC initiatives in Singapore has been mentioned 
43 times throughout all interviews. Most interviewees perceive the government as an 
essential stakeholder “To my mind, the government is the most important stakeholder, 
maybe even number one, two, and three. Everything ties back into some agency, or 
some ministry. […]. Everything is kind of entangled together, and the government 
typically has a stake in major companies.” (I1, line 29). As I1 describes it, in Singapore 
the government holds ownership over the most important institutions such as agencies, 
==PAGE:54==
Results 
53 
ministries, funding institutions (I1, line 29), real estates (SME3, line 46), and is closely 
tied to the industry (I2, line 32). 
Interviewees mentioned twelve governmental sub-institutions that are involved in the SC 
initiatives in Singapore. Figure 12 illustrates the government institutions that were 
mentioned by the interviewees and their code frequencies. 
 
Figure 12: Government Institutions in Singapore 
As seen in Figure 12, the most mentioned government institution is the Public Utility 
Board (PUB), the “local water utility” (SME 3, line 46). The PUB manages the water 
agenda of Singapore (I3, line 46) and is responsible for the initiation of new water 
solutions and the facilitation of test beds for those solutions. In summary, out of the seven 
business interviewees, the PUB was mentioned ten times. Compared to that, 
intermediaries mentioned the PUB only three times. 
The second most mentioned institution throughout all interviews is, with equal code 
frequency (code frequency: four), the National Research Foundation (NRF) and the 
Jurong Town Corporation (JTC). In Singapore, funding for innovations is supported by 
the NRF, through which the government has committed 19 billion dollars to R&D across 
multiple domains (I3, line 46-48). The NRF consolidates and gives out the budget for 
funding opportunities t the various agencies in Singapore (I3, line 46-48). 
. With the funding, the government aims to develop policies, plans, and strategies for 
research, innovation and enterprise and its funds’ strategic initiatives, such as the 
corporate labs as mentioned in chapter 5.1.3 “Stakeholders in the private sector” (I2, line

24). The JTC drives the industrial development in Singapore and aims to facilitate 
innovations through projects (I2, line 18). Projects that were mentioned by the 
interviewees include Incubator programs, like the JTC launchpad (I2, line 30), and 
projects that contain the development of land and space (S2, line 36-40).  
Continuing, government institutions that were highlighted by the interviewees are with 
equal code frequency the Info‐communications Media Development (IMDA) and the 
Economic Development Board (EDB). The EDB strategizes Singapore’s economic 
position with a focus on businesses, innovation, and talent (I2, line 24). The IMDA is a 
government agency that is focused on the SC initiatives and works together with the 
government agency GovTech (I2, line 32). The IMDA aims to support Infocom industry, 
whereas GovTech aims to improve government services by using the smart nation 
platform for both citizens and the government (I2, line 32). 
Other government institutions that were mentioned by the interviewees include 
Singapore Enterprise, the Energy Authority Market, the National Environment Agency 
(NEA), SG Innovate, and the Port of Singapore Authority (PSA). Singapore Enterprise is 
a government agency that helps companies to enter the market outside Singapore. 
Within this institution, Singapore does not distinguish between foreign or domestic SMEs 
(I3, line 68). The Energy Authority Market is a regulator, which aims to work on policies 
that are required to enable the energy deployment in Singapore (I3, line 46). NEA is the 
utility for waste management (I3, line 46).  
5.1.2 
Stakeholders in the public sector 
The public sector is part of the government and an important stakeholder for the SC 
initiatives in Singapore, which has been mentioned six times throughout all interviews. 
Stakeholders in the public sector include universities in Singapore (code frequency: 5). 
Universities that were mentioned in the interviews include the NTU (SME 3, line 46; I1, 
line 29; I2, line 18; S1.1, line 31) and the National University of Singapore (NUS) (I3, line 
58). Universities as stakeholders in the Singaporean SC can be both: “potential partners, 
or potential customers to work with” (SME 3, line 46). Interviewees see the universities 
in Singapore as: “[…] a major driver” (I2, line 18). Furthermore, interviewees perceive 
that there is a lot of innovation and research going on in Singapore, especially at the 
NTU (S1.1, line 31). One sub-institution that was mentioned by the interviewees is the 
innovation and enterprise company of the NTU, called NTUitive (code frequency: 1). I2 
explains this institution as: “an interesting organization, [that is] trying to spin out and 
commercialize innovations coming from NTU and I am [I2] trying to matchmake the 
corporate and industry partners. But when we talk about spinouts from NTU, we talked 
==PAGE:56==
Results 
55 
about research-based innovation and not student projects” (I2, line 18). Furthermore, 
interviewees perceive that: “universities, such as NTU, [have] quite an ambitious smart 
city plan” (I1, line 29). 
5.1.3 
Stakeholders in the private sector 
The private sector as a stakeholder for the SC initiatives in Singapore has been 
mentioned seven times throughout all interviews. However, the private sector as a 
stakeholder in Singapore is perceived to be active in the form of corporate labs. Most 
more prominent corporates have a program that keeps the innovation departments 
updated on the market by collaborating with companies in OI projects (I4, line 16). 
Interviewees state the role of the corporates either in a collaborative approach, such as 
with corporate labs and accelerators, incubators or in acquiring innovative companies 
(I2, line 28). Interviewees mentioned three specific institutions out of the private sector 
that is involved in the SC initiatives in Singapore. First of which is the corporate lab “Rolls-
Royce@NTU”, where development is funded from both the NRF and Rolls-Royce 
themselves (I2, line 24). Second, is the BMW Future Mobility Research Lab, which is 
researching together with the NTU on new solutions in mobility (I2, line 24). Third, 
interviewees mentioned the non-profit organization Gate21 that was involved in the 
initiation of the SCWL and aims to bring together the municipalities, companies and 
knowledge and research institutions and has the goal to promote green transition and 
growth (I2, line 6). 
5.2 
Outlining the context of the Smart City Singapore 
This section of the results is dedicated to highlighting the opportunities and challenges 
of Singapore’s SC initiatives as mentioned by the interviewees. The coding of the 
interviews resulted in a precise categorization of the opportunities and challenges in 
Singapore, whereas opportunities were mentioned with a coding frequency of 67 and 
challenges were mentioned with a coding frequency of 100. 
5.2.1 
Opportunities for Singapore’s Smart City initiatives 
The interviewees mentioned several opportunities within the SC initiatives in Singapore. 
The key opportunities are summarized in Figure 13. 
==PAGE:57==
Results 
56 
 
Figure 13: Opportunities for Singapore’s Smart City initiatives 
The most mentioned opportunity by the interviewees is the funding opportunities that 
Singapore offers (code frequency: 15). Interviewees perceive that “there is a willingness 
in Singapore to fund projects that have an impact and that can make a change” (SME3, 
line 62). The Nordic SMEs mentioned several reasons to apply for foreign investments, 
such as the risk awareness of Danish investors: “If you can raise capital, do it outside of 
Denmark. In Denmark the investors […] are not very risk-taking” (SME1, line 88).  
Interviewees mentioned three funding opportunities that are important for them or could 
become important in the future: VC, governmental funding through tax incentives, and 
governmental funding through salary support. One business and one intermediary only 
mentioned the VC funding. However, the government funding received a higher focus 
with a code frequency of twelve. Out of the twelve mentions, the coding shows that SMEs 
were more dedicated or interested towards the governmental funding initiatives (code 
frequency: nine) than intermediaries (code frequency: five). During the analysis of the 
answers of the interviewees, it is to mention that only one SME could give more details 
on the specific government funding opportunities (SME1). The opportunity of acquiring 
funding in Singapore are various. The government in Singapore offers many financial 
incentives to start up in Singapore, whereas in Denmark only a few possibilities of 
acquiring funding are given (SME1, line 18). One example in the Nordics is the Vækst 
fund which is given for SMEs in Denmark. However, interviewees perceive the funding 
possibilities in Singapore as more beneficial, since they are more risk-taking (SME1, line 
18). As explained above in chapter 5.1.1, Singapore Enterprise is responsible for the

funding of SMEs in Singapore and offers monetary incentives. Furthermore, the 
government-owned investment company Temasek is funding many SMEs out of the 
SCWL focus sector (Water- and Waste-Management) and was mentioned several times 
(I1, line 33; I2, line 38; S2, line 18; S1.1, line 33). 
Second most mentioned opportunity is the Singaporean government itself (code 
frequency: ten), which is perceived to be active with “a lot of initiatives, [that are] 
governmentally funded and pushed to make them a smart and sustainable city. […]. They 
have really ambitious goals.” (S1.2, line 71). Moreover, the government is seen as a 
quick-acting organization: “Whereas in Denmark, it is much more individual decisions. 
Of course, you have governments that have long-term strategic goals, but it takes longer 
and a lot of the incentives have to come through the individual. I think that makes a 
difference [in Singapore].” (S1.1, line 25). The strong vision of the Singaporean 
government was highlighted several times (code frequency: three). It is noteworthy that 
not all interviewees agree to the statement of a strong vision (I2). However, the SC vision 
and government initiatives are mentioned to be connected to the government’s 
leadership, that is undergoing a significant change and paradigm shift with the current 
prime minister in Singapore (I2, line 14). Furthermore, Singapore is perceived to be 
strong in attracting foreign companies (code frequency: three): “Singapore as a city-state 
is a very advanced state and is a hub that is able to attract a lot of international 
companies and also has a lot of money that can be invested. They have a huge potential 
in taking a lead and showing how smart cities can look like.” (SME4, line 54). Additionally, 
interviewees perceived the government layers as an opportunity. Since most ministries 
are structured in a single government layer, SMEs see the opportunity of reaching out to 
one ministry as an advantage due to the fact that they only need to get in contact with 
one authority that controls the majority of a sector: “If we can get them on board, saying 
that with this solution we are actually able to save a lot of water, I think they could be a 
key partner for us, the local authorities.” (SME3, line 46). Similar, the government is seen 
to be quick in taking plans into action, due to the “singular government layer. […] 
everyone knows what the path is and where we are going.” (I2, line 40).  
Businesses and intermediaries see opportunities for SMEs in Singapore as being a hub: 
“Singapore is a highly developed country, especially in terms of innovation.” (S2, line 44). 
Businesses (S2 and SME3) see potential in Singapore as being a hub for science and 
technology (SME3, line 30) and as a hub for sustainable businesses (S2, line 10). 
Interviewees see Singapore as a place that is aspiring to a thriving community that “wants 
to become an innovation hub and the Silicon Valley for the [South East Asian] region.” 
==PAGE:59==
Results 
58 
(I2, line 10). I3 sees the concentration of businesses in Singapore in a mix that consists 
of the local industry and foreign companies (I3, line 42). 
Next, businesses and intermediaries see opportunities in the SC initiatives in Singapore, 
since they have a strong focus on sustainable businesses (code frequency: six). 
According to the interviews, this focus had changed throughout the recent years and the 
projects of the SCWL: “At the time when we did the analysis, sustainability really was not 
a big thing in Singapore it was more a nice to have, but not necessarily a need to have. 
The green agenda has always been part of Singapore and its development project, but 
it was not sustainability for the sake of sustainability. It was more based on the resource 
deficiency that Singapore is struggling with since day one. They had to become 
sustainable because there simply is no choice. When you have no water, you have no 
natural resources and have to come up with different ways. Now there is a much larger 
focus on sustainability and climate change on top of the agenda and the whole green 
transition” (I2, line 8). Additionally, I2 mentions that the newly introduced paradigm shift 
towards sustainable businesses is to be seen holistically, Singapore should integrate 
solutions and see sustainability holistically for the country (I2, line 40). S2 confirms I2’s 
position and adds that the green agenda is of special importance for the Singaporean 
universities: “In my opinion, yes there is a green orientation of Singapore. From a political 
and a strategic point of view” (S2, line 64). The opportunity in Singapore’s focus on 
sustainable businesses is seen from both sides, the businesses (code frequency: three) 
and the intermediaries (code frequency: three).  
Continuing, interviewees see the opportunities of Singapore as a SC in the access to 
South East Asia (code frequency: six). For example, SME5 has the intention to “use 
Singapore as an Asian hub and then have a demonstration solution, that we can show 
to interested customers in Singapore and then see what we can find.” (SME5, line 6), 
the SC is seen as “an opportunity […] to start up in Singapore, where the IP protection 
is much better than in China. Then go with Singaporeans to reach China, this is our idea” 
(SME5, line 6). I3 confirms the growth opportunity for SMEs in Singapore by highlighting 
the potential of the region. In his perception, Singaporean companies have a strong 
footprint in South East Asia, since the Singaporean market itself is too small: “A lot of 
Singaporean SMEs and Singapore’s SMEs are known for 50 percent of the GDP and 50 
percent of employment in Singapore. This is just the SMEs in Singapore. They have 
been venturing into the market.” (I3, line 78).  
Subsequently, an opportunity that was mentioned by the interviewees is the business 
environment of Singapore itself, which is on the one hand perceived as a safe 
environment (code frequency: five) and on the other hand as very similar to the structures 
==PAGE:60==
Results 
59 
that businesses are used to from Europe (code frequency: five). The safe environment, 
as characterized by the interviewees, mainly applies to IP protection (SME4, line 34), 
trust among businesses (SME2, line 64) and a stable economic environment (SME1, line 
66). Interviewees appreciate similar thinking as in Europe concerning a Western-oriented 
mindset (SME3, line 60), ownership, company structures, and the trust between 
business relations (SME2, line 44). 
Another opportunity is the possibility of using living labs and opening these up for testing 
solutions. Utilizing so-called living labs, Singapore offers “a district where you have a 
particular focus on trying out new things” (I4, line 50), where companies, corporates, and 
research institutes can test solutions (I4, line 52). According to I1, the idea of using living 
labs in Singapore is not just the possibility of testing out a solution, but also the exchange 
of innovations between other countries, such as Denmark or the Nordics (I1, line 6). 
Examples of living labs in Singapore include the Changi Airport, which has a commercial 
living lab (I1, line 25), or the EcoCampus at the NTU (I3). I3 highlights the benefits of 
using living labs for the SC initiatives, which are seen as “giv[ing] a visibility internationally 
[…] attract[ing] partners and corporates and start-ups, that otherwise would be hard to 
get in as partners” (I4, line 52). The living labs offer the opportunity of gaining visibility 
by showcasing the solutions in a real-world scenario with citizens (I4, line 52). 
Additionally, the living labs create attention for the whole city and what companies are 
doing within the country. 
Other opportunities of the SC initiatives in Singapore that were slightly mentioned include 
the need for innovations coming from external parties (code frequency: three), 
Singapore’s ability to bring together supply and demand (code frequency: one), such as 
through the SCWL innovation lab, and Singapore’s approach of putting words into action 
(code frequency: three). 
5.2.2 
Challenges of Singapore's Smart City initiatives 
The interviewees mentioned several challenges within the SC initiatives in Singapore. 
The key challenges are summarized in Figure 14.  
==PAGE:61==
Results 
60 
 
Figure 14: Challenges of Singapore's Smart City initiatives 
The government in Singapore is not just perceived as an opportunity, as described in 
chapter 5.2.1, but also as the biggest challenge for the SC initiatives in Singapore (code 
frequency: 23). There are several reasons that interviewees mentioned throughout the 
interviews that underline the Singaporean government as an obstacle to their BMI. These 
are found in the government’s top-down approach (code frequency: seven), the difficult 
access to ministries (code frequency: five), the lack of cross-sectional coordination (code 
frequency: five), a general fear of the SMEs of the government (code frequency: two), 
the perceived nationalism of the government (code frequency: two), and a lowered 
economic growth rate (code frequency: one). I2 explains the top-down approach of the 
government as a struggle, which the government needs to address (I2, line 10). The top-
down approach of the policymaker limits companies to innovate within the country (I2, 
line 10). I2 adds to the problem statement that the government should adopt a bottom-
up approach, where the prime minister relinquishes control over ministries, funds, and 
legal institutions (I2, line 40). The top-down approach of the government is linked to the 
difficulty of getting access to the Singaporean ministries (S1.2, line 68). “They have clear 
hierarchies, in the companies and the government. In Denmark it is very flat-lined, […] 
everyone […] is kind of on the same level. It is really easy to talk to the people you need 
to. In Singapore, it is certainly way more formal” (S1.2, line 68). In S1.2 view, companies 
need to build a network in Singapore. In this process, a delegation or partner can 
introduce the companies to the relevant stakeholders in Singapore (S1.2, line 68). The 
difficulty of the access to the ministries is based on the fact that the Singaporean 
Government

Challenges of Singapore’s Smart City initiatives
==PAGE:62==

61 
ministries are structured in one government layer, meaning one acting body (see 
Opportunities for Singapore’s Smart City initiatives). This can result in a challenge since 
the ministries are big institutions with slow and bureaucratic processes (S2, line 36). 
Going hand in hand with that, interviewees perceive that the individual government 
institutions are not sufficiently coordinating cross-sectionally. I2 suggests putting policies 
in place to achieve better integration between the agencies and how they function (I2, 
line 64). Additionally, there is the challenge to align all agencies under the same vision 
(I3, line 50). Interviewee I3 indicated that there are projects that are aiming to achieve 
better collaboration between the institutions: “We had intergovernmental programs. For 
example, when the national energy efficiency program was set up, it had all the different 
agencies sitting on the project, and when we had the Clean Energy program set up, we 
had all the different agencies involved. If we look at energy efficiency on a national level, 
then everyone plays a role in that” (I3, line 50). Moreover, Interviewees perceive that 
there is a certain degree of fear of the government. S2 mentions that there is a perception 
that the government is rigorous and that certain actions might end in imprisonment (S2, 
line 60). I2 links this fear of the government to the top-down approach, which was 
described above and explains that “there is [… a] focus from Danish companies that 
Singapore is top driven. I think it is very much a question of can you live with it or not. 
Singapore is a democracy but also has its constraints. There are some things that you 
cannot say. This is also part of the whole perception of Singapore. This is how it is” (I2, 
line 44). The nationalism that was mentioned as another challenge within the SC 
initiatives of Singapore is seen in a protectionist behavior, where the Singaporean 
government is looking at the “upside in Singapore for [these] investment[s]” (S2, line 22), 
which is often bigger when the country invests in the “local innovation base so that they 
are able to do innovation by themselves” (I2, line 16). Finally, another challenge within 
the Singaporean government is the Gross Domestic Product (GDP) growth rate, which 
is still at a high percentage. However, I2 mentions that “if you look at the growth rate that 
Singapore is expecting over the next year, it is at three and a half percent. For many 
years it has been at least six or seven percent. There are definitely some challenges that 
he [the president] is facing” (I2, line 40). 
Continuing with the challenges of the SC initiatives in Singapore, the coding shows 
cultural barriers as the second biggest challenge (code frequency: 19). However, the 
challenge is seen as contradictory throughout the interviews; some interviewees even 
changed their minds about the cultural barriers during the interviews (S2, line 60). 
However, the coding show a clear cluster of cultural barriers, which are seen in the risk 
awareness of Singaporean stakeholders (code frequency: two), the effort that needs to 
==PAGE:63==
Results 
62 
be put in to build trust (code frequency: two), and in the perceived fact that Singaporeans 
are only interested in so-called lighthouse projects (code frequency: ten). Interviewees 
perceived that Singapore was eager to import foreign innovations, but these needed to 
be well-proven solutions, or also called lighthouse projects (S1.1, line 31). S2, for 
example, mentioned that specific facts and best practices in the form of reports were 
required for their internationalization by the Singaporean stakeholders, which the 
company could not provide at the time of their venture stage (S2, line 60). S1.1 adds that 
“for the government to decide to implement something, they want to see it having been 
implemented somewhere else. I do not think they are first movers” (S1.1, line 74). This 
perception of Singapore not being a first mover is seen equally by businesses (code 
frequency: five) and by intermediaries (code frequency: five), whereas only the 
consultants highlighted this barrier on the side of the intermediaries and mostly (code 
frequency: 4) the businesses that participated in the SCWL of 2017. Next, businesses 
(SME1 and SME3) see a cultural challenge in the fact that building trust takes longer 
than what they are used to in Europe: “Asians themselves are a little bit more trust-
oriented, you need to gain their trust, in order to be open-minded towards you. That could 
be a challenge, it takes time to build that trust” (SME3, line 44). SME1 adds that from his 
experience it typically takes a long time to build trust, and many meetings are needed 
before first projects are initiated (SME1, line 42). Following, both consultants (I1 and I2) 
suggest that Singaporeans are somewhat risk averse and would prefer to invest in more 
prominent companies (I1, line 47). 
The third most mentioned challenge is found in the CleanTech sector (code frequency: 
14). Water, waste, energy, and urban farming were perceived as the most important 
sectors within CleanTech, where Singapore is working on innovative solutions. The 
challenges within Water-Management were elaborated in detail by the interviewees. 
Specific challenges include water leakages (code frequency: one), water savings (code 
frequency: two), the water import from other countries (code frequency: four), and the 
citizen’s perception about water usage (code frequency: six). However, interviewees 
were quick in finding solutions for Singapore’s water shortage, which include: smart 
water sensors, water cleaning facilities, solutions that enable the reuse of rainwater, and 
desalination systems. Singapore sets specific targets to tackle these challenge, such as 
for the NTU, which has to “reduce greenhouse gases within 35 percent. They have to 
reduce energy consumption by 35 percent. They have to have less waste. These are 
definitely areas where we know what they are focusing on. We can help them achieve 
those.” (SME5, line 42). The demand for new and innovative solutions in Singapore is 
driven by the country’s lack of natural resources. Singapore has little to no renewable 
==PAGE:64==
Results 
63 
resources, except for solar energy. Innovation labs can help to find solutions that fit the 
country’s needs and solve the renewable energy integration (I3, line 54). 
Continuing, the intermediary consultants see the lack of talent in entrepreneurship as 
another challenge for the SC projects in Singapore. I1 and I2 mention the reasons for 
that, which are to be found in the diminishing workforce in Singapore and the pressure 
that arises through the high living costs in Singapore. I1 argues that “the top tier students 
in Singapore do not choose to go into entrepreneurship but go to large companies and 
this is actually something that we have confirmed from researchers at NTU in Singapore” 
(I1, line 15). According to I2, the diminishing workforce is a disruption for the SC initiatives 
in Singapore and “Singapore is struggling with too little labor force. The labor force is not 
big enough. They are addressing a lot of operational efficiencies through automation.” 
(I2, line 40). Moreover, according to I2 students are often forced to choose other career 
options than starting their own companies: “living standards are high in Singapore, but 
the costs of living [are] also extremely high. What you often see is that students who 
finish at the university would rather go and land a job with some of the big four 
consultancies or big engineering companies, because they cannot afford to live 
otherwise. So, security is something that is very important. You do not have the [financial] 
safety that you have here in Denmark. That of course also [creates] some pressure from 
the entrepreneurial point of view.” (I2, line 18).  
The last challenge that was mentioned by the interviewees is the technical barrier. This 
barrier was only seen by businesses (S2 and SME1) which are offering hardware 
solutions for the Singaporean market. As seen in the challenges within CleanTech, one 
technical barrier for the SC initiatives in Singapore is the lack of natural resources, that 
forces the city to open towards new solutions and to trade and sell other goods, to acquire 
resources (SME1, line 16). The city does, in some areas, not provide the sufficient 
infrastructure for solutions that are made in Western countries. Often, companies have 
to adapt their BMs to be able to deploy their solutions in Singapore. S2 mentions the 
infrastructure as the most significant challenge for his business (S2, line 66). 
5.3 
Barriers to Open Innovation for SMEs 
This section of the results is dedicated to highlighting the barriers of OI for SMEs as 
mentioned by the interviewees. The coding of the interviews resulted in a precise 
categorization of the barriers. Interviewees mentioned nine significant barriers that 
potentially hinder them from deploying OI for the SC initiatives in Singapore. Figure 15 
summarized the barriers as mentioned by the interviewees. 
==PAGE:65==
Results 
64 
 
Figure 15: Barriers of Open Innovation for Nordic SMEs 
The most-often mentioned challenge was discussed from several perspectives 
throughout the interviews. Businesses mostly mentioned the challenge of how to handle 
Intellectual Property Rights (IPR) when applying OI. I3 suggests that it is vital for Nordic 
SMEs to understand “how they treat their IPs. If you are getting a joint funding from the 
university, then the IPs are shared. You need to be comfortable with that. […] it is more 
a joint licensing, or licensing fees. That is something that you need to decide first. Unless 
[you] really think there is a specialty within the Singapore university that you want, to 
procure their expertise to a fully funded program then the IP could stay with the company 
[…]. There are different models for the IP, that needs to be talked about.” (I3, line 28). 
For the intermediary I3, it is a prerequisite for OI that companies are willing to collaborate 
and share their IPs, he states that “if there is innovation coming out of the platform [the 
university and innovation lab collaboration], and there is co-funding, then it is definitely 
[a] shared [IP].” (I3, line 30). Some companies see the barrier as one that should be 
taken into account when adopting an OI approach in Singapore (SME3, line 16; S1.1, 
line 37) and believe that the company will benefit more from the BMI than from protecting 
their IP and closing themselves up: “Yes, there is a risk that people will copy us, but we 
see it as following: they will never get as fast to our stage, where we are right now, 
because they will start from scratch. They will still have to build up that knowledge base, 
that we already have got now.” (SME3, line 16). Some companies perceive the IPR issue 
as the biggest barrier that could hinder their internationalization plans and having IP 
protection in place is a precondition for them (S1.1, line 65; SME2, line 32). Other 
IPR

Barriers of Open Innovation for SMEs
==PAGE:66==
Results 
65 
companies see their IPR as protected within the collaboration with other stakeholders 
but have experienced that contracts were designed so that they lost their IPs (SME1, line 
90). The general fear of the companies before starting OI projects is seen in “how much 
do I tell, how do I protect my IP, how do I protect my ideas, or formalize the legal 
relationships, before you actually formalize the technical relationship.” (I3, line 60). 
The second most mentioned barrier of OI is seen in the maturity of the companies (code 
frequency: eight). As mentioned in chapter 5.2.2, Singaporean stakeholders demand 
lighthouse projects that have been tested in other markets before. The maturity of a 
company can, therefore, be a barrier for OI, since stakeholders might not be interested 
in collaborating on an OI project. According to I2, the SCWL has changed their focus of 
companies that are considered for the participation in the innovation lab: “With this batch 
of companies [2018], we have chosen companies that are more mature. Basically, where 
you have a track record of their solution. It does not have to be in Singapore, Singapore 
is happy to be the first and to provide a proof of concept within their market. But the 
solution has to be tried and tested. I need to know that it actually works in real life. This 
is what we have been focusing a lot on these companies that we are working with so that 
they are able to deliver” (I2, line 54). Interviewees explain that with decreasing maturity 
of the company, the Singaporean stakeholders’ interest decreases and that it is essential 
to have a certain degree of market readiness for the OI collaboration in Singapore 
(SME3, line 52). Furthermore, the organizational maturity eases the relevance of OI 
projects (I2, line 54). 
Next, interviewees perceived a barrier to the physical presence that is needed for OI 
projects. The physical presence is not just needed from the companies, but as well from 
the platforms, such as the SCWL, which need to have “the resources to have somebody 
permanently on the ground in Singapore that could help them navigate and help them 
[…]” (I2, line 46). Companies that want to deploy OI in Singapore need to bear in mind 
that: “Singapore is a little bit far away. […] you need to be on the ground, and you need 
to have some people there” (SME1, line 40). SME1 advises, that Nordic SMEs should 
evaluate the additional costs that are needed for an international OI project, whereas S2 
proposes to “Either mov[e] there, […]. Alternatively, finding a partner there, who would 
get upside from us succeeding there” (S2, line 56). 
Furthermore, interviewees perceive the lack of possibilities to showcase their solution as 
a potential barrier for OI. For example, SME3 develops a sophisticated technology that 
is difficult to understand for other stakeholders, explaining and showing the solution is 
necessary, to collaborate with others and to make them understand the technology 
(SME3, line 58). SME1 remarks that companies should aim to showcase their solution: 
==PAGE:67==
Results 
66 
“typically, when you are new in a market, you need to do some pilots” (SME1, line 40). 
Establishing these pilots is not an easy task as S2 explains (S2, line 42). 
Additionally, interviewees perceive that another barrier for OI is to find and work with 
suitable partners that are willing to collaborate and are capable of achieving a BMI 
together with the companies. Interviewees mention two factors that make this a 
challenge. On the one hand, SMEs out of this sample find it challenging to find partners: 
“On the business, or commercial side, it has been the challenge to establish closer 
partnerships with someone.” (S2, line 56). On the other hand, SMEs might choose a 
wrong partner for collaboration that is either not capable of achieving useful results or 
might even be harmful as in the case of SME2 (SME2, line 22).  
Also, interviewees perceive that OI projects, such as the SCWL, do not set goals that 
are clear for both participating stakeholders, the SMEs that are planning to collaborate 
and the city’s stakeholders that are scouting for new solutions. According to I4, it is a 
challenge to define clear goals from the demand side, and he stresses that these goals 
should neither be defined too bro asad nor too narrow (I4, line 20). Goals from the 
demand side that are set too broad will result in difficulties in bringing together the 
specific stakeholders that are relevant for the OI project (I4, line 20). Goals from the 
demand side that are set too narrow will limit the solutions that are to be developed or 
implemented in its creative process and stakeholders will stop thinking about the problem 
in a broader sense (I4, line 20). I4 perceives a responsibility in the platforms that are 
bringing together demand and supply for OI projects to help define the goals in the right 
way (I4, line 20). It is to mention that only intermediary I4, which comes from a 
governmental institution, mentioned the lack of clear goals in OI and SC projects as a 
barrier. 
Connected to the barrier of unclear goals, SMEs perceive that an unclear vision of an OI 
project might be capable of slowing down the whole innovation and internationalization 
process of their company (SME5, line 28). The participation in OI projects with unclear 
goals might, therefore, slow down SMEs. However, this barrier has only been mentioned 
by one business (SME5). 
Continuing, intermediaries perceive that there is a lack of openness in the mindset of the 
companies participating in OI projects (I3, line 64). I3 suggests that companies that 
participate in OI projects need to change their mindsets and change their perception of 
losing their idea. Overcoming their fear of the Asian market, in general, is crucial (I3, line 
64). I3 sees the OI platforms and state embassies as responsible for navigating the 
companies (I3, line 64). It is to mention that only intermediary I3 stated this barrier. 
==PAGE:68==
Results 
67 
Finally, businesses mentioned the adaptation of their BM as a barrier to OI projects. The 
adaptation of a company’s BM is seen to be time-intensive and investing that time for an 
OI project, where the outcome is unclear, seems to be a risk that businesses take (SME1, 
line 40). The challenges of the BM adaptation for OI projects, and especially for the SC 
initiatives in Singapore will be elaborated in chapter “5.5 Internationalization and BMI in 
the context of the SCWL”. 
5.4 
Deploying OI by SMEs in Smart City programs 
In the backdrop of the barriers that interviewees perceive in OI projects, many ways were 
mentioned of how SMEs can deploy OI practices for SC initiatives. This section will 
present the results of the interviews, by presenting mechanisms of OI for SMEs. 
Subsequently, the opinions of how Nordic SMEs can deploy OI practices will be 
presented. 
5.4.1 
Mechanisms of Open Innovation for SMEs 
The popularity of OI as a methodology for sourcing innovation resulted in many 
mechanisms of how interviewees strategize to make use of the concept. The results of 
the interviews developed into eight categories of OI mechanisms that intermediaries and 
businesses either plan to use or recommend. Figure 16 summarizes the eight OI 
mechanisms for Nordic SMEs and shows their coding frequency illustrated as 
percentages throughout all interviews. 
==PAGE:69==
Results 
68 
 
Figure 16: Mechanisms of Open Innovation for SMEs, with code frequencies in 
percentage 
Interviewees mentioned OI mechanisms 60 times throughout all interviews. In 44 percent 
of the times interviewees mentioned OI mechanisms, they mentioned Co-design (code 
frequency: 26, see Figure 16) as a mechanism. Co-design is seen to be useful for the 
elaboration of products or services and also for product evolution and can, according to 
the interviewees, be established through different partnerships. Partners for co-design 
project are seen in cities (code frequency: two), competitors (code frequency: one), 
customers (code frequency: one), corporates and MNCs (code frequency: five), and 
universities or similar research institutions (code frequency: 13). According to the 
interviews, the co-design with cities allows stakeholders to concentrate on specific 
challenges within a city, where SMEs aim to connect the city’s demand within a pre-
defined challenge area with the supply of their company’s solutions (I4, line 16). Often, 
these challenge areas are characterized as pre-commercial projects, where SMEs are 
asked to deliver ideas and solutions which the city has not thought about itself (SME5, 
line 26). In return, cities can help companies to develop their solutions, for example by 
giving them access to open data (SME1, line 72), or by connecting them with other 
Co-design
44%
Living labs
25%
Joint venture
10%
Accelerators
9%
Using bigger 
corporations as 
best practice and 
enter new market
5%
Co-funding
3%
Spin-out
2%
Incubator
2%
==PAGE:70==
Results 
69 
stakeholders that either co-develop the products and services or agree to buy the 
solutions (I4, line 16). The co-design with cities has been mentioned by both, businesses 
(SME5) and intermediaries (I4). The possibility of co-design, as a mechanism of OI, with 
competitors has been mentioned by one business (SME3). Getting into dialogue with 
competitors can benefit both stakeholders by supplementing both company’s products 
and services (SME3, line 22). Similarly, businesses see the opportunity of co-design 
together with customers as a beneficial mechanism of OI (SME1, line 82; SME3, line 22). 
Receiving feedback and working together with customers might open new markets that 
entrepreneurs have not thought about before (SME1, line 84). Next to that, interviewees 
perceived the co-design with corporates as a useful OI mechanism. As seen in chapter 
“5.1.3 Stakeholders in the private sector”, corporates are actively seeking the 
collaboration with SMEs and establish a unique program, such as the corporate labs (I4, 
line 16). However, it is to mention that mostly intermediaries (code frequency: four) 
mentioned the co-design with corporates, only one business mentioned this mechanism. 
Ultimately, interviewees perceive universities and research institutes as an essential 
partner in co-design. These can for example help in developing the product and service. 
For example, SME1 worked closely with DTU, where students helped in analyzing data 
that was gathered by the company, which in fact led to new business opportunities 
(SME1, line 26). Furthermore, the universities are seen as experts in the local context of 
the country and their fields of research, SME1 sees them as interesting partners for the 
company’s product development and BMI (SME1, line 52). Often, universities have 
facilities to test solutions, such as the NTU with its EcoCampus, that interviewees 
perceived as an advantage in the development process of their products and services 
(S2, line 8). 
The second most mentioned mechanism are the living labs (25 percent, see Figure 16). 
The living labs are often characterized as test beds in designated areas of universities 
(I2, line 6; S1.1, line 33; S2, line 6), or in neighborhoods within the city, such as the 
Yuhua state in Singapore, that is provided by the government, where solutions can be 
tested in pilot projects (I2, line 6; I2, line 62). For companies and city stakeholders, the 
living labs serve both as an innovation tool where solutions can be further developed, as 
well as an investment promotion tool (I2, line 6). The Singaporean state offers an area 
for SMEs where regulations and laws are loosened in order to foster innovations and to 
sharpen the regulations within the country (I2, line 62), I2 states that the government 
aims to solve the question of: “How can we, on a very small scale, try and work around 
these agencies [for example PUB, EDB, and others] and put some pilots into place?” (I2, 
line 64). One intermediary mentioned that the Singaporean living labs lack openness and 
==PAGE:71==
Results 
70 
that they could improve by accepting projects that are in an early stage of their venture 
process and work with them on new concepts that might shape the SC. According to the 
intermediary, an approach that adopts “more of an open and playful look towards 
entrepreneurship could be beneficial” (I1, line 47). 
Ten percent of the feedback regarding OI mechanisms was about joint ventures (see 
Figure 16). I3 puts the OI mechanism in perspective and states that if the products do 
not need to be acclimatized or adapted to the region, SMEs should consider to working 
with local partners in Singapore in the form of a joint venture to penetrate the market (I3, 
line 32). In return, local partners can then help to market the company’s products and 
bring a benefit to the partnership by providing their experience within the regions (I3, line 
32). Companies perceive joint venture as beneficial since they can keep their lean 
company structures in their home markets while going into new markets (SME3, line 40). 
Nine percent of the quotes regarding OI mechanisms were about accelerators (see 
Figure 16). I4 explains the benefits of accelerator programs as a way to overcome 
uncertainty in a market, such as pricing, customer needs, or the market in general (I4, 
line 30). The collaboration with accelerators might help these SMEs to acquire market 
intelligence (I4, line 30). I4 explains that the prerequisite for the collaboration in 
accelerators is that “both parties open up and are vulnerable and tell each other exactly 
about what they know and what they do not know, they can help each other a lot. I think 
there is a great potential in the collaboration of big corporates and start-ups in making 
the whole process of scaling your company up much faster” (I4, line 30). It is to mention 
that incubator programs were not a focus area during the interviews, only one 
intermediary mentioned these as a useful OI mechanism (I2, line 30). 
Other OI mechanisms, which were mentioned by the interviewees, include the use of 
bigger corporations to enter the Singaporean market. The collaboration with more 
prominent companies allows SMEs to use the branding of a well-established company 
(S1.1, line 76; S1.2, line 77; S2, line 24). Furthermore, one business mentioned that the 
possibilities of university spin-outs are a viable option to use OI, where the interviewees 
business resulted from a research project (S2, line 4). Lastly, acquiring a co-funding 
together with universities, or within an industry is perceived to be another beneficial OI 
mechanisms, the perceived benefits of the co-funding were not elaborated in detail by 
the interviewees (I2, line 24; I3, line 38). 
5.4.2 
Deployment of Open Innovation for Nordic SMEs 
The interviews result in several ways of how SMEs can overcome barriers of OI and 
deploy OI, to solve challenges of the SC initiatives in Singapore. The interviews revealed 
==PAGE:72==
Results 
71 
that there are several ways how SMEs can use OI to solve essential challenges of the 
Singaporean SC. In summary, interviewees discovered eight critical aspects that should 
be taken into consideration when using OI. Figure 17 shows the most mentioned modes 
of OI deployment for Nordic SMEs. 
 
Figure 17: Deployment of Open Innovation for SMEs for the SC initiatives in Singapore 
Interviewees elaborated on partnerships (code frequency: 48) and innovation labs (code 
frequency: 48) as successful ways to deploy OI. For many businesses, partnerships are 
the key to success and one of the most crucial OI mechanisms that enable them to 
address the challenges of SC initiatives in Singapore (S2, line 26). As seen in the OI 
mechanism of joint ventures in chapter 5.4.1, partnerships help businesses to get access 
to new markets and overcome barriers of the local market. Partnerships are 
characterized in many forms by the interviewees, who choose to work with: research 
institutes, local stakeholders, other companies, government institutions, or investors.  
The partnerships between Nordic countries and Singapore are seen to be useful for OI. 
Interviewees see the exchange between both regions of mutual benefit: “I think in 
Denmark and in Singapore there are definitely some things what we can learn from each 
other. Both are small countries with an innovation focus and looking at sustainability the 
way that both countries do, it is an interesting case” (I2, line 22). Singapore’s Smart 
Nation plan is perceived to be the foundation of the Nordic – Singaporean collaborations 
(I1, line 6). The collaboration between both regions is not just to be seen on a company 
level, but interviews perceive that the governments are in direct dialogue and aim to find 
solutions on the SC agenda through an open collaboration approach (I1, line 55).

Deployment of Open Innovation for SMEs
==PAGE:73==

72 
Research institutes, such as the NTU or NUS in Singapore are seen to be beneficial for 
purchasing innovations (I1, line 55) piloting of new solutions in test beds (S1.1, line 55), 
product development (SME5, line 20), acquiring new contacts out of the local market 
(S2, line 24) and funding that is provided by the government (I3, line 38). Furthermore, 
interviewees perceive that the collaboration with the Singaporean stakeholders 
(Introducing the stakeholders within the SCWL case) is involved with high engagement 
and time-investment (Barriers to Open Innovation for SMEs). However, the investment 
into the collaboration and meetings with these stakeholders is seen to be beneficial for 
the SMEs and helps the interviewed businesses. For example, the participation in the 
SCWL enabled the businesses to acquire relevant contacts, as the embassy (S1.1, line 
53). Additionally, interviewees perceive that trust is an important aspect when deploying 
OI (code frequency: five). I3 explains that the process of building trust in Singapore is 
long, but a necessity for collaborations within the country (I3, line 78). Moreover, hiring 
locals can help to overcome market barriers, since these understand the legal 
requirements, pricing, customer needs, and cultural conditions (I2, line 22; SME5, line 
12). Likewise, interviewees perceive the government as a stakeholder that can help 
SMEs in deploying OI for the SC initiatives in Singapore (see chapter 5.1.1 for the 
government institutions). According to the interviews, the embassies play an important 
role in the exchange of innovative solution in the Nordics and Singapore. I2 states “the 
Danish Embassy […] thought that this might be a great opportunity for Denmark to 
strengthen its commercial ties with Singapore, which were very much based on the 
maritime industries in the past 15 years. So, we thought [about] how we can extend this” 
(I2, line 8). Additionally, interviewees perceive the embassies as institutions that can help 
navigate SMEs through new markets, such as in Singapore (I3, line 64). Lastly, 
partnerships with investors are perceived to be beneficial for the deployment of OI in 
Singapore, but the specific benefits were not elaborated on in detail by the interviewees 
(S2, line 22-24). 
After the second most mentioned way of OI deployment (code frequency: 48), the use of 
innovation labs as platforms is the third most mentioned deployment. Mostly 
intermediaries (code frequency: nine) perceived the innovation labs as a way to 
overcome barriers of the SC initiatives in Singapore. By facilitating the collaboration with 
Singaporean stakeholders, innovation labs are seen to overcome barriers that SMEs 
face when entering the Singaporean market (I4, line 34). However, it is not just the 
companies that are benefiting from the use of innovation labs. According to I2, in the 
case of the SCWL, the innovation lab revealed to the Singaporean government 
stakeholders’ new ways of how innovations can be funded and how stakeholders can 
==PAGE:74==
Results 
73 
ideally interact in the ecosystem of a SC (I2, line 14). For the businesses, the interviews 
resulted in six real opportunities that innovation labs can potentially offer and one 
potential danger that is seen in the innovation labs. According to the interviews, the use 
of innovation labs helps Nordic SMEs by providing a safe access to the Singaporean 
market (code frequency: six), shaping their BM in order to adapt it to the Singaporean 
market (code frequency: five), increasing their speed of innovation deployment in new 
markets (code frequency: two), facilitating meetings with potential partners and 
stakeholders in Singapore (code frequency: eleven), connecting a demand of SC 
challenges in Singapore, with a supply of innovative solutions from the Nordic countries 
(code frequency: seven), and the establishment and connection towards test beds in 
Singapore (code frequency: four). 
The dimension of acquiring safe access to new markets through an innovation lab is 
seen to be useful for SMEs that are mature enough to internationalize (I4, line 32) and 
provides a framework that helps SMEs to navigate through new markets (SME4, line 88). 
In the case of the SCWL, Business Developers from DTU are helping the participating 
SMEs to shape their BM towards the challenges of the SC initiatives in Singapore. The 
help of DTU Business Developers is seen to be constructive for finding new growth areas 
in the setting of the SC (SME3, line 27; SME4, line 28). It is to mention that this dimension 
of the innovation lab was solitarily mentioned by business (code frequency: five). 
Furthermore, the increment of speed in the innovation deployment by SMEs is seen to 
be increased by the use of innovation labs, since these help with connections to 
stakeholders and partners in Singapore, the BM adaption facilitated by the Business 
Developers and the navigation through the new market in Singapore (I3, line 24; SME4, 
line 28; SME5, line 68). Furthermore, interviewees highlighted the possibility of meeting 
relevant stakeholders and potential partners within innovation labs from which they can 
benefit. SME5 explains that it is crucial for his business to “have the right network and 
you have to find out how to get in touch with the right people, and we see this [the SCWL 
innovation lab] as a much faster way to get in touch with the right people. It is a shortcut. 
[…] Just the fact that you have the DTU, the Danish state as a stakeholder is very 
beneficial. And I know in Asia that means a lot when you can come with partners who 
are from the Danish state” (SME5, line 48). However, one interviewee explains that 
facilitating meetings with stakeholders in Singapore is not enough to path the 
internationalization of a Nordic SME towards Singapore (I2, line 56). Therefore, 
innovation labs aim to target specific challenges within the SC initiatives and choose 
SMEs to participate that are offering solutions which are targeted towards these solutions 
(I2, line 56). According to I4, innovation labs enable a two-way collaboration by 
==PAGE:75==
Results 
74 
connecting the CleanTech sector of the Nordics to the challenges that the Singaporean 
government faces in their SC initiatives: “I think it is a two-way collaboration, where we 
can learn from Singapore and they can learn from us” (I4, line 12). The Nordic countries 
have strong center around CleanTech, which is a good basis to collaborate, and 
Singaporean stakeholders are interested in working together with the Nordics (I4, line 
12). Lastly, according to the interviews innovation labs help in the establishment of test 
beds. In the case of the SCWL, the innovation is aiming to establish “sandbox initiatives” 
where SMEs could showcase their products and services and run smaller tests, without 
any commercial binding (S2, line 42). The previously discussed living labs (5.2.1 
Opportunities for Singapore’s Smart City initiatives) offer such an opportunity for SMEs 
to test their solutions and innovation labs and can connect SMEs with such test beds (I3, 
line 54; I4, line 32). Contrary, one intermediary perceived that there is a potential danger 
of companies participating in innovation labs that have an opportunistic mindset and 
exploit the benefits of such programs (I4, line 44). In general, innovation labs have to be 
concerned about which participants they attract and which ones actually should benefit 
from the program, since they attract all kind of potential participants (I4, line 44). 
Subsequently, interviewees mention the triple helix concept as a successful way to 
deploy OI for the SC initiatives in Singapore. The triple helix concept is seen to be a 
collaboration between university – industry – and the government. I4 explains that the 
SCWL was established through the funding of the governmental institution Climate-KIC 
(I2, line 6), which is a triple helix program that is initiated by the European Commission 
(I4, line 6). The initiative aims to create innovation and to deploy it based on the market 
needs (I4, line 6). According to I4, the triple helix collaboration is often established in 
cities which have complex structure but are the right focus areas with an explicit limitation 
of the demand that is coming from its citizen (I4, line 16). In the triple helix collaboration, 
I3 sees a responsibility in the private institutions that ought to make the first step in 
starting new initiatives (I3, line 62). Interviewees add that the triple helix concept is often 
established through co-funding agreements (I2, line 24). However, the vision for the 
collaboration can either come from the private institutions (I3, line 62), or result from a 
top-down policy-initiated approach coming from policymakers (I4, line 22). Nonetheless, 
I4 suggests another approach that could replace the concept of the triple helix 
collaborations: “Rather than talking about triple helix, I think you should talk about the 
elements of collaboration and what makes a good collaboration. Because triple helix is 
just a name of a form of collaboration.” (I4, line 22). It is to mention that intermediaries 
mainly mentioned the triple helix collaboration (I2, and I4), only one business (SME1) 
mentioned the triple helix concept as a way to deploy OI. 
==PAGE:76==
Results 
75 
Another important aspect when deploying OI is the alignment of timelines (code 
frequency: three). When deploying OI, it is vital that both SMEs and SC stakeholders 
consider the timelines of the participating parties. A start-up typically has a three to six 
months’ timeline before they need to move to the next milestone, due to their obligations 
towards their capital providers (I4, line 28). However, when working with cities, the 
timeline is very different, governments mainly aim to reach long-term targets that are 
planned for the upcoming four or three years (I4, line 28). I4 adds that it is necessary to 
consider the timelines of SMEs when inviting them for collaboration on SC projects (I4, 
line 28). Instead, I4 suggests that governments should not invite start-ups until they have 
a short list of project goals and needed solutions (I4, line 34). Cities that follow this advice 
will be able to integrate solutions in an OI project, where SMEs deliver innovative 
products and services to solve the challenges of the SC initiatives in time (I4, line 34). It 
is noteworthy that only the government intermediary I4 mentioned this aspect of OI 
deployment. 
Successively, the use of students to bridge OI projects was mentioned as an aspect to 
deploy OI successfully. Businesses cannot only use students to co-develop their 
products and services (SME1, line 26) but serve as a tool to facilitate collaboration within 
OI projects. The different stakeholders in an OI project often have different virtues, 
cultures and work approaches (I4, line 28). According to I4, students are a successful 
tool to link the diverse stakeholders in an OI project since they are faster to adapt and 
faster to identify opportunities (I4, line 28). Using students that are doing their thesis as 
a way to initiate the collaboration between the corporate and the university can be an 
excellent bridge. When knowledge about each other is established, and trust is built, then 
it is useful to create a research program or innovation program on top of that (I4, line 28). 
Subsequently, interviewees perceive the living labs in Singapore not just as a 
mechanism to test-bed their solutions and work on the company’s product development, 
but they are perceived to serve as environments that can support public OI processes 
(I4, line 30). The Singaporean living labs are seen as key enablers of innovation 
processes, and they might be able to bridge the collaboration between public 
organizations, SMEs and citizens (I2, line 6; I4, line 30; S2, line 18). Moreover, 
companies can use the successful deployment in a living lab as a best practice and 
promote the project in their Public Relations strategy in order to enter new markets (I4, 
line 30; SME1, line 78).  
Consecutively, businesses (SME1 and SME4) suggest that governments should make 
data freely available so that innovations can be created faster. In a SC, data is generated 
on a daily basis about the city’s performance through sensors, IoT systems, and other 
==PAGE:77==
Results 
76 
data sources (SME4, line 62). Making this data freely available can create innovations, 
since businesses might be able to find new BMs and increase, therefore, the city’s 
performance (SME4, line 62). However, this suggestion was only mentioned by two 
businesses; no intermediary suggested this aspect as a way to deploy OI. 
Lastly, I4 explains that a successful deployment of OI needs to consider the resources 
that are required for the execution of the project, from both participating stakeholders, 
the SMEs and the government stakeholders (I4, line 28). According to I4, companies 
need to be ready to for big scale orders in Singapore and should calculate the new 
needed workforce for the new market in Singapore (I4, line 28). However, government 
stakeholders and city partners should be ready to act on the challenges that might arise 
in the projects, not only with regard to time, but they have to set aside resources as well 
(I4, line 28). 
5.4.3 
Conceptual linkages between the deployment of Open 
Innovation and the Smart City context of Singapore 
In order to conceptualize the two topics of OI and SC, a SNA was conducted, to find 
insights and connect both fields of research. The SNA of the qualitative interviews 
revealed many exciting results, which are illustrated in Figure 18. The blue quadrants 
illustrate the businesses which are related to the respondent concepts. Concepts are 
illustrated as circles. SC challenges are illustrated as red circles, barriers of OI are 
denoted as orange circles, and the deployment of OI is represented as blue circles. The 
results of the sociogram only show the conceptual linkages of businesses. The 
Sociogram did not include any directional information of the ties, since the relations within 
the sample are not directed, but represent mentions of one interview (code frequencies). 
The numeral indexes define the strengths of the connections. Chapter 6.1.1 will discuss 
the SNA in detail. 
==PAGE:78==
Results 
77 
 
Figure 18: Open Innovation and Smart City Sociogram 
Utilizing the SNA, there are specific connections between the deployment of OI for the 
Nordic businesses and the SC challenges of Singapore. The challenges in CleanTech 
within Singapore and the cultural barriers are strongly related to most of the businesses. 
The technical barrier of the Singaporean SC and the government itself are challenges 
that are not related to most business. The technical barriers of Singapore are only related 
to SME1 and S2. The government as a challenge for the SC initiatives is only related to 
S2 and SME3. Furthermore, the Sociogram reveals opportunities to deploy OI for SMEs 
that can be conceptualized to overcome the SC challenges (see  
Conceptualization of Open Innovation and the Smart City context of Singapore). 
Likewise, the Sociogram shows specific barriers of OI for SMEs, that are related to the 
deployment of OI. For example, SME1 mentioned the adaptation of the BM as a barrier 
for OI, which was, on a conceptual level, overcome by the engagement of students in 
the project to bridge OI and the SC challenges. Moreover, the sociogram highlights 
specific concepts which are not connected to the deployment of OI for Nordic 
businesses, and that only was mentioned by intermediaries (Aligning time management, 
Sufficient use of resources, No clear definition of goals, Mindset of Nordic companies, 
and the lack of talent in entrepreneurship - upper left corner in the diagram). 
==PAGE:79==
Results 
78 
5.5 
Internationalization and BMI in the context of the 
SCWL 
This section presents the results on the barriers of internationalization in Singapore, the 
critical factors for choosing the born global or Uppsala model of internationalization, and 
the triggers for the BMI of Nordic SMEs as found in the sample.  
5.5.1 
Internationalization of Nordic SMEs to Singapore 
Interviewees see several barriers in the internationalization to Singapore. Figure 19 
summarizes the barriers mentioned by interviewees perceived during their experience 
while introducing an internationalization to their company and shows their coding 
frequency illustrated as percentages throughout all interviews, out of 15 mentions. 
 
Figure 19: Barriers of Internationalization for Nordic SMEs 
Out of the mentions, 47 percent of the internationalization barriers were about the 
maturity of the company. According to the interviews, less mature companies are 
predicted to be less successful in Singapore. Interviewees perceive that best practices, 
empirically proven facts, and first tests in other markets are essential, to be successful 
in Singapore (S2, line 6; S2, line 62). Furthermore, I4 suggests that companies that plan 
to internationalize and that are still in their product development phase need to 
Maturity of company
47%
Infrastructure
27%
Complex 
structures of 
stakeholders in 
foreign country
13%
Cultural barrier
13%
==PAGE:80==
Results 
79 
understand their customers in the first place (I4, line 38). He suggests that, when 
companies go from a setting in Europe to a setting in South East Asia, they should 
understand the customers there and analyze their customer validation (I4, line 38). This 
barrier has been equally mentioned by intermediaries (code frequency: three) and 
businesses (code frequency: three). 
The second most mentioned barrier, with 27 percent of the mentions about the 
internationalization barriers, is the technological infrastructure in Singapore that is in 
some areas lagging. Obsolete technologies make it difficult for Nordic companies to 
market their BM (S2, line 18; S1.2, line 79). These obsolete technologies include old 
water meters or missing heating systems inside the buildings in Singapore (S2, line 18; 
S1.2, line 79). In some cases, rolling out Nordic technologies would require a 
reinvestment into newer technologies and infrastructures for the Singaporean 
government (S2, line 18). However, this barrier has only been mentioned by the business 
that participated in the SCWL 2017. 
Subsequently, businesses perceive the differences between the Nordic and the 
Singaporean culture as a barrier. According to the interviews, trust is an essential factor 
for doing business in Singapore, and the top-down mindset of the Singaporeans (see 
chapter 5.2.2) is seen to be a hurdle (SME1, line 62). 
Next, businesses perceive the complex structures of stakeholders in Singapore as a 
barrier to internationalization. As seen in chapter 5.1.1 Stakeholders in the government 
sector, there are many utilities and government agencies with which SMEs could 
potentially collaborate with, and which SMEs potentially need to get in contact with, to 
acquire access or permissions to roll out innovation within the city (SME1, line 30). 
According to SME1, it is a challenge to identify the stakeholders that are taking the 
decisions. If businesses need to get something done, they have to get a good 
understanding of which organization they should start with, and then be sent down the 
system (SME1, line 50). However, only one business (SME1) mentioned this as a barrier 
for the internationalization to Singapore. 
Nonetheless, facing the barriers of Nordic companies during their internationalization to 
Singapore, they choose to enter markets according to two models: Born global and the 
Uppsala model. The reasons to internationalize according to the born global model were 
elaborated in detail by the interviewees, whereas the Uppsala model was only barely 
mentioned. Most interviewees (code frequency: eight) state that they chose to enter 
markets inorganically, meaning according to the born global model, because the 
opportunity was at hand. Many of these mentioned, that they were contacted by the 
intermediaries of the SCWL to participate in the innovation lab, with the goal to 
==PAGE:81==
Results 
80 
internationalize in Singapore (S1.1, S1.2, SME1, SME3, SME4). The internationalization 
of most businesses was, therefore, opportunity-driven (SME3, line 56). Moreover, 
businesses perceived the opportunity of entering the South East Asian market as 
attractive and, therefore, chose to grow inorganically in Singapore (SME5, SME3, S1.1, 
S1.2, S2). I4 explains that the market for CleanTech and Greentech in China and India 
is still developing (I4, line 46). The possibility of testing in Singapore and scaling towards 
the South East Asian markets is seen as a viable path for many businesses (I3, line 22). 
Similarly, businesses perceive that the possibilities of distributing their products and 
services will be higher in Singapore than in their domestic markets (S1.1, line 62). The 
internationalization in Singapore is seen as a market that is fast in delivering results 
(SME4, 72). Additionally, one business chose to internationalize according to the born 
global model, because new regulations in Singapore opened up opportunities in the 
market which allow him to compete in the country. However, the specific regulations were 
not mentioned by the interviewee (SME1, line 32). Additionally, one business mentions 
internationalizing according to the born global, due to a weak competitive environment 
in Singapore (2, line 10). Lastly, interviewees perceive the funding opportunities in 
Singapore as more attractive than in the Nordic countries (I2, line 46). I2 explains that 
the funding opportunities in Singapore, as presented in chapter 5.2.1 Opportunities for 
Singapore’s Smart City initiatives, are attracting many companies to internationalize in 
Singapore. SME1 adds that Asian investors are, in his perception, much more visionary 
than investors from the Nordics and are more familiar with emerging technologies 
(SME1, line 88). 
However, interviewees mentioned that the born global model might not be as applicable 
as the Uppsala model for some companies (I1, line 45). Building a stable home base in 
the domestic market and entering neighboring countries in a step-by-step-approach is 
seen as an alternative that is more secure (I4, line 46). Moreover, interviewees perceived 
that following the Uppsala model is beneficial since they can apply the same 
technologies with little adaptations on the product itself in markets with a low psychic 
distance (S1.2, line 79). The differences in standards between Singapore and the 
domestic market were seen as broad and would force the company to invest time and 
research into product development (S1.2, line 79). One intermediary confirms this 
statement: “Over the last two years, it has proven to be quite a bit more difficult than what 
we imagined. I believe it would be easier to enter the Nordic market. Basically, following 
the Uppsala model” (I1, line 45). 
==PAGE:82==
Results 
81 
5.5.2 
Business Model Innovation for the Singaporean market 
Many businesses need to adapt their BM and innovate within the dimensions of the Who, 
the What, the How, and the Why, to deliver innovations to Singapore. Figure 20 
summarizes the causes for their BMI for the SC initiatives in Singapore. 
 
Figure 20: BM adaptation for the Singaporean market 
According to the interviews, there are different factors why businesses need to adapt 
their BMs for the Singaporean market. The most mentioned factor for the BMI of Nordic 
SMEs is the revenue and cost structure (code frequency: eight). Interviewees mention 
that Singaporean businesses are specialized in copying technologies from European 
companies and offering them at a lower price (SME5, line 14). Thus, Nordic SMEs face 
a position where products and services need to be delivered at lower pricing than in their 
home market (SME5, line 14). Nordic SMEs are forced to innovate within their revenue 
and cost structures, to be successful in Singapore (S1.1, line 37; SME3, line 58; SME5, 
line 14). 
Furthermore, the BM adaptation of the value proposition, based on the customers’ 
needs, was most-often mentioned in the sample, together with the adaptation of the 
revenue and cost structure (code frequency: eight). Consumer behavior and cultural 
differences in Singapore are seen to be the factors that classify the value proposition as 
an uncertainty (I3, line 16). Analyzing the needs before deploying innovations in 
Singapore is, therefore, a necessity (I3, line 18). For example, companies that are 
offering solutions for Water-Management in Singapore need to be aware that the water 
usage is different than in most Nordic countries (S1.1, line 33).

Subsequently, interviewees perceive the product development as an essential aspect of 
their BM adaptation towards Singapore (code frequency: six). Technologies that were 
developed for the Nordic market often need to be adjusted for the Singaporean market. 
Therefore, innovating the status quo is often needed when deploying Nordic innovations 
in Singapore (SME2, line 28; SME5, line 54). Triggers for the product development are 
mentioned to be in Singapore’s infrastructure, environment, and legal conditions. 
As seen above, Singapore’s infrastructure is seen to be another trigger for BMI. The 
condensed area requires technologies to be space-efficient (SME5, line 14). 
Furthermore, other technological and infrastructural standards (as seen in chapter 5.5.1 
Internationalization of Nordic SMEs to Singapore) force companies to rethink their 
products and adapt them towards the setting of the Singaporean market (S1.1, line 80).

(code frequency: three). Therefore, the climate and tropical environment of Singapore 
forces companies to adjust their technologies to the new setting (I3, line 16; SME4, line
