are different regulations in the new market context. If specific policies in the domestic 
market support innovations and if those do not exist in the new market it will create 
alterations (I3, line 16). Lastly, one business perceives that the way how they distribute 
their products and services will change in the new market context of Singapore (SME2, 
line 52). 
==PAGE:84==
 
 
 
83 
6 DISCUSSION 
This thesis is aimed to investigate how Nordic SMEs can deploy their OI practices to 
address challenges of SC initiatives in Singapore. Throughout the empirical analysis, this 
thesis has looked for emerging themes and concepts, which would allow a better 
understanding of how Nordic SMEs can deploy OI, and by that ultimately overcoming 
challenges of the SC initiatives in Singapore. This section is dedicated to discussing the 
findings and how they relate to what the thesis has found in theory ( Smart Cities and 
Open Innovation and  Business Model Innovation). 
The discussion is divided into four sections. First, this thesis tries to bridge the 
deployment of OI with the SC challenges and to conceptualize both topics. 
Subsequently, the Singaporean SC will be contextualized on the frameworks of the 
theory. Next, this thesis discusses the BMI of Nordic companies that are planning to 
internationalize to Singapore. Lastly, the discussion will reflect on the methodological 
strengths and weaknesses of the applied research. 
The results of the empirical research reveal interesting cross-case patterns of the 
interviews. Overall the research question can be answered through the interviews. There 
are several connections between the barriers of OI and the challenges of SC initiatives. 
Interviewees have revealed several ways of how Nordic SMEs can use and deploy OI to 
overcome challenges of SC initiatives in Singapore. New findings based on the applied 
research can be identified, and presented frameworks of the theory can be applied to 
the context of the SC Singapore.  
6.1 
Bridging the deployment of OI to overcome SC 
challenges in Singapore 
It is clear that OI offers several benefits for companies of all sizes (Chesbrough, 2006, 
2010; Enkel et al., 2009) and SCs are dependent on such collaborations and innovations 
coming out of this process (Cosgrave et al., 2013). However, bridging the capabilities of 
ventures towards SCs, to address SC challenges, is attracting the attention of many 
different sectors and professionals. The most important insights that are coming from the 
analysis of the empirical research is that governments need to open up their cities and 
==PAGE:85==
Discussion 
84 
let external stakeholders innovate within the boundaries of the cities and that OI 
approaches can help to bridge the deployment innovations from one country to another.  
The results (see chapter 5.4.1 Mechanisms of Open Innovation for SMEs) show that 
Nordic SMEs choose to adopt eight OI mechanisms to deploy their solutions and develop 
their innovations further (Figure 16: Mechanisms of Open Innovation for SMEs, with 
code frequencies in percentage). A detailed look at the mentioned barriers of OI and SCs 
in the sample shows that interviewees confirm many of the issues as mention in the 
theory (2.2.3; 2.2.4), and some OI barriers and SC challenges were newly identified 
through the empirical investigation. Through the analysis of the empirical research (5.3 
Barriers to Open Innovation for SMEs), this thesis confirms the beforementioned 
challenges and barriers for OI (2.2.4 Open Innovation challenges and best practices for 
SMEs) that are seen in:  
• 
the IPR challenges,  
• 
outsourcing of R&D, 
• 
unclear goals of the projects, 
• 
rising costs due to the physical presence that is needed,  
• 
slow product development time, due to collaboration and organization with other 
stakeholders, 
• 
and the difficulty of finding strong and capable partnerships. 
Interviewees did not state the administrative challenges and the cross-sectional 
collaboration as an obstacle for OI projects. A reason for that can be, that most of the 
interviewed businesses (five) are only starting in the OI project, and therefore, these 
challenges did not arise in that stage of the OI process in the SCWL. However, the results 
reveal new challenges for SMEs in the context of their internationalization in cooperation 
with the SCWL, which were not identified in the literature review: 
• 
the necessity of a certain degree of maturity of the venture, to be attractive for 
Singaporean stakeholders, 
• 
the difficulty of showcasing their products in Singapore, 
• 
the adaption of the venture’s BM, to be able to deploy solutions in the context of 
the new market, 
• 
and the lack of openness in the mindset of stakeholders participating in OI 
projects. 
The challenges of the SC initiatives in Singapore are in some issues confirmed with the 
theory of the interviewees (2.1.3 Challenges of Smart City initiatives) (Alawadhi et al., 
2012; Ebrahim & Irani, 2005; Hollands, 2008; Joshi et al., 2016): 
• 
The size of the projects that should neither be defined as too broad nor narrow, 
==PAGE:86==
Discussion 
85 
• 
the definition of clear goals from the government, or stakeholder in Singapore 
with the desired outcome of the SC project, 
• 
a lack of cross-sectional coordination within the government institution, 
• 
a lack of talent with social and technical skills, and the willingness of talent to 
work within SC initiatives, 
• 
and political challenges. 
The interviewees did not mention the compliance challenges of a SC. However, 
interviewees defined other challenges of SC initiatives, with focus on the Singapore case, 
that was not identified in the theory (Alawadhi et al., 2012; Ebrahim & Irani, 2005; 
Hollands, 2008; Joshi et al., 2016): 
• 
the access to ministries 
• 
the fear of the power of the government 
• 
nationalism and the preference for local innovation 
• 
a low growth rate within the country  
• 
cultural barriers, such as the top-down mindset of Singaporeans, the risk 
awareness, and the process until trust is built 
• 
and technical barriers, due to old infrastructures within the country and little 
natural resources in Singapore 
The results show that OI and SC initiatives suffer similar challenges. Through OI between 
countries and governments, the boundaries between cities and ventures can be 
overcome, and innovation can be transferred inwards and outwards. A shift for cities 
towards OI can, therefore, increase the competitiveness of a city. The results show that 
there are several mechanisms which can serve as platforms to deploy innovations from 
country to country to solve issues of SC initiatives. According to the sample of this thesis, 
OI projects in Singapore are initiated by triple helix collaborations (I4), where the 
government collaborated with private and public institutions, to address challenges of the 
country. Based on that, initiatives like the European accelerator Climate-KIC are aiming 
to connect the public and private sector, utilizing different OI mechanisms, as innovation 
labs, co-funding, spin-outs, or other collaborations. These OI mechanisms can be seen 
as platforms, where stakeholders are enabled to deploy innovations and benefit of 
solutions on both sides. In the sample, interviewees chose to deploy their innovations 
through the SCWL innovation lab, that facilitated the SMEs by connecting them with 
relevant stakeholders in Singapore, offered them a consultation in their BM adaption 
towards Singapore and targeted specific challenges that were identified within SC 
stakeholders in Singapore. In the backdrop of the SCWL, interviewees can test their 
products and services in the Asian market and collaborate with the SC Singapore. 
==PAGE:87==
Discussion 
86 
Furthermore, these platforms can help to overcome the barriers of OI and connect SMEs 
to the SC initiatives, to solve critical issues of the city. 
When innovations are to be deployed through the use of an OI platform, such as an 
innovation lab, certain archetypes will facilitate the success of the OI project. The 
timelines of SMEs and governments should be considered and aligned with the needs 
of both parties. As seen in the results, cities set goals that are to be achieved in the long 
term, whereas SMEs have to accomplish their milestones in the short term. By 
addressing specific challenges of the Singaporean SC initiatives, for example with 
platforms such as an innovation lab, participants, and SC stakeholders set a clear goal 
that is to be realized through new solutions by SMEs. Furthermore, OI platforms offer 
SMEs the possibility of testing their innovations in living labs, such as in the case of the 
NTU in Singapore, where products and services for Water- and Waste-Management 
solutions are tested in the EcoCampus. In order to overcome cultural barriers and to 
build trust, platforms can connect stakeholders from private companies to universities 
that can be helpful to establish a certain degree of knowledge about the participating 
stakeholders in Singapore. Involving students to work together with companies on 
projects, can help to identify new business opportunities for the companies while initiating 
the collaboration between the corporate and the university. However, the statements of 
the interviewees also need to be interpreted with caution, since all interviewees in the 
sample are or were involved in the SCWL innovation lab and therefore might be biased 
in the answers given. There is additional research required to validate the qualitative 
findings. 
Thus, OI platforms should not only be seen as facilitators for the deployment of 
innovations in Singapore, but they are also a method that enables to connect public, 
private and government institutions, and therefore, an enabler for triple helix 
collaborations. As Singapore is continually growing and the challenges are increasingly 
menacing, governments should work together with OI platforms, such as the SCWL, to 
find new solutions that will solve the city’s concerns. By that Singapore will be able to 
find solutions for the SC initiatives from the outside-in (Enkel et al., 2009). Furthermore, 
OI platforms, which aim to solve the challenges of SCs by providing innovations from the 
outside can serve to create a universal language that can communicate SC’s benefits to 
the individual stakeholders. 
Figure 21 aims to illustrate the deployment of OI for the SC initiatives in Singapore in a 
framework, that potentially could be used for a generalized approach of OI projects with 
SCs. However, this framework is a hypothetical assumption, which is based on the 
==PAGE:88==
Discussion 
87 
findings of the results, and should further be researched and confirmed in future 
research. 
 
Figure 21: Framework for the deployment of OI in the SC Singapore 
6.1.1 
Conceptualization of Open Innovation and the Smart City 
context of Singapore 
From the qualitative findings, it can be said that SCs and OI are somehow connected. 
However, a conceptualization of both topics, as seen in Figure 18: Open Innovation and 
Smart City Sociogram, was needed to find linkages between the deployment of OI and 
SC challenges. Specific subsets of vertices, also referred as Cliques (Scott, 2017), within 
the SNA can be identified. A detailed look on the SCWL participants of 2018 compared 
to the ones of 2017 reveals differences within the OI approaches of the Nordic 
companies and their relations to OI and SCs within the SCWL case.  
In the sample of the participants of the SCWL 2018 (see Figure 22), it is noticeable that 
all SMEs addressed challenges within the CleanTech of Singapore. This might because 
all businesses participated in the SCWL with a focus on Water- and Waste-Management. 
Innovation labs and partnerships in Singapore helped businesses to deploy OI and 
overcome the challenges within CleanTech. It can be interpreted, that innovation labs 
prepared the businesses to address the CleanTech challenges. Furthermore, 
partnerships did not only help to overcome challenges within CleanTech but also helped 
to overcome cultural barriers. By partnering with local stakeholders in Singapore, trust 
can be built, and new opportunities are created, as locals might have access to an

essential network of additional partners within the SC. The technical barrier of 
Singapore’s SC was overcome by SME1, which applied several OI concepts. Thus, the 
triple helix collaboration, the involvement of students to bridge OI, the access to open 
data, and the use of living labs helped, in a conceptual view, to overcome not only the 
technical barrier of Singapore but the challenges within CleanTech, and the cultural 
barriers. Regarding the triple helix, it is interpretable that SME1 used the collaboration of 
government-public-and-private to find partners that helped the business to start projects 
in the living labs of a municipality and therefore deploy OI. Lastly, SME3 addressed the 
Singaporean government as a challenge for the SC initiatives. They used innovation labs 
as a method to deploy OI. In this scenario, innovation labs might have helped to get into 
contact with relevant stakeholders of the Singaporean government, which hold power 
over certain utilities of the cities. 
 
Figure 22: Open Innovation and Smart City Sociogram of businesses in the SCWL 2018 
When analyzing the SNA of the businesses in the SCWL 2017 (see Figure 23), it is 
interesting to see that many OI practices are omitted (upper left corner) and that the 
interviewees have not mentioned the lack of talent in entrepreneurship. Similar to the 
participants of the SCWL 2018, businesses aimed to overcome the challenges within 
CleanTech in Singapore with partnerships and innovation labs, such as the SCWL. 
However, the strengths of the ties to the CleanTech challenges (S2: 15, S1: 9) are 
significantly higher than in the SNA of the SCWL 2018. Interestingly, S2 deployed the 
innovation labs and partnerships to overcome cultural and governmental barriers. It could 
be argued that S2 used innovation labs, similar to the participants in the SCWL 2018, to 
==PAGE:90==
Discussion 
89 
be introduced to an essential stakeholder in Singapore. Moreover, the innovation labs 
and partnerships helped S2 to overcome the challenges of the technological 
infrastructure in Singapore. It can be assumed that partners and the innovation lab 
helped to strategize the BMI of S2. For the business S1, it is noticeable that only the 
challenges within CleanTech have been conceptualized. The business overcame and 
addressed the challenges within Water-Management, by utilizing the triple helix 
collaboration and partnerships. Partnerships were used to develop the BMI for 
Singapore, and to find potential buyers of the Water-Management solution. It can be 
argued that the use of universities and governmental institution might help S1 to find 
business opportunities and to deploy OI practices, which ultimately help to overcome the 
challenges of Singapore’s SC initiatives. 
 
Figure 23: Open Innovation and Smart City Sociogram of businesses in the SCWL 2017 
In summary, the SNA shows many ways and possibilities of how Nordic SMEs can 
deploy OI practices, to overcome the challenges of Singapore’s SC challenges. There 
are specific differences between the SCWL participants 2017 and 2018. The individual 
businesses perceive and face distinct barriers during the OI process and their 
internationalization to Singapore; Thus, different OI practices help them to overcome the 
challenges. The SNA helped to conceptualize the topics of research in OI and SC and 
shows that there are connections between both fields on a conceptual level. Future 
research could investigate specific relations in detail and confirm the conceptualization 
of this thesis. 
==PAGE:91==
Discussion 
90 
6.2 
Singapore’s Smart City put into perspective 
Singapore’s SC initiatives help to shape the face of the city and aim to respond to the 
high pressures of the country, such as the scarcity of resources, the high growth within 
the country, climate changes, the declining workforce in entrepreneurship, as well as the 
declining economic growth within the country in recent years. By introducing the IT2000 
plan (Choo, 1997) and the Smart Nation program („Smart-Nation“, 2018), Singapore 
aimed to transform the country into an intelligent island. Analyzing the results of this 
thesis in the backdrop of the SMELTS framework (Chourabi et al. (2012); (Joshi et al., 
2016)) this thesis presumes that the SC initiatives are well targeted towards the 
economic, legal, and technological advancement of the country.  
Many economic institutions are involved with the government. Agencies like Singapore 
Enterprise or GovTech are highly tangled in the industry and are supporting not just local, 
but also foreign companies, which are willing and able to innovate within the city. The 
strong economic vision is seen to be able to drive the transformation of Singapore 
towards a SC.  
However, the technological direction of the SC initiatives is still in its early phase of 
development. As mentioned by the intermediaries (I1; I2), Singapore is just starting to 
install newly technical infrastructure, just like Smart Meters, or IoT sensors, to be able to 
offer a high standard of infrastructure, through which SMEs can deliver their products 
and services. For Singapore, it is essential, that technology supports the productivity of 
companies. As seen in the results chapter many companies (S1; S2; SME1; SME5) 
mentioned the old infrastructure as one of their most significant hurdles to deploying their 
BMs in Singapore. By investing in Singapore’s technology, the country supports a more 
resilient economy that can withstand environmental challenges, such as in the Water- 
and Waste-Management that was addressed by the SCWL.  
The legal situation in Singapore is seen diversely, and the results show areas where 
additional work within the legal structures is required. As seen in the results, most 
businesses perceive Singapore as a safe environment. However, the fear of the 
government and the top-down approach is seen as an obstacle within the legal 
dimension of the SMELTS framework. The results indicate that the top-down mindset 
can be overcome if the government cuts down on control and allows innovations to 
develop within the city. The first step in this direction already happened. The living labs 
in Singapore offer test beds, where specific legislation can be bypassed so that 
innovative solutions can be tested. Furthermore, the top-down mindset of the 
Singaporean, government is not only seen as a disadvantage. In Singapore there are no 
lobbies that could block the testing of new solutions in the living labs and offer new 
==PAGE:92==
Discussion 
91 
products to the market are fast. Furthermore, other than in the Nordics, where several 
utilities govern different regions within one country, SMEs only have to convince and sell 
to one utility, whereas in the Nordics utilities are divided and clustered into the specific 
subregions of the countries. 
Continuing in the analysis of the SMELTS framework, Singapore has a strong emphasis 
on sustainability. This emphasis is given due to the nature of the countries natural 
resources, growing population, and growing demand for new water and waste solutions. 
The results show many challenges that are being addressed by the government within 
water, waste, energy, and production of new resources. Nevertheless, this strong 
emphasis found in the results should not be over-interpreted since all interviewed 
businesses were active in Water- and Waste-Management and addressed this challenge 
within the SCWL. Therefore, the focus on the CleanTech sector is given due to the nature 
of the interviewees’ background within the SCWL case. The results do not give a 
complete picture of the social dimension within the SMELTS framework. However, it is 
mentioned in the interviews (S2), that the Temasek organization is supporting projects 
for the social dimension, such as for social housing, with government funds. The sample 
does not provide any data on the interaction of Singapore with its citizens, or how they 
interact in the SC context.  
Even though a strong vision of the government leads the SC initiatives, there are areas 
where Singapore’s management can be improved. Utilities and agencies in Singapore 
are not aligned, and cross-sectional coordination is missing in many institutions. By 
introducing intergovernmental programs, Singapore is aiming to connect the government 
institutions to one single SC vision. A realignment of the government’s internal structures 
could help to increase the coordination between governmental institutions. Instead of 
introducing one intergovernmental program, Singapore could introduce a new institution 
that serves as a “Chief Officer of Innovation” and aligns all stakeholders under the city’s 
vision. 
Concluding, the SMELTS’ inner and external levels of Singapore’s SC are led by a strong 
vision that was set by the state. However, by focusing only on the capabilities of the city 
itself, relying on the private institutions and MNCs to bring change, Singapore might 
struggle to innovate and to come up with new approaches for the SC in a long-term view. 
OI can potentially offer the opportunity to address dimensions in the SMELTS framework 
(Joshi et al., 2016) that cannot be solved by the city’s capabilities. The first step in that 
direction is made with the introduction of the government agency Singapore Enterprise 
that fosters domestic and foreign innovations. Inviting external stakeholders, such as 
SMEs, research institutes, or even governments for collaboration in OI projects within 
==PAGE:93==
Discussion 
92 
the city, could be rewarding for SCs. Thus, this thesis proposes to add a third 
intermediary level to the SMELTS framework that consists of OI collaborations. The 
proposed intermediary level has the potential to address the external and internal levels 
of the SMELTS framework and functions as a substitute through which cities can source 
external knowledge, innovations, and partners. Thus, should cities struggle within 
specific dimensions, as in social, management, economy, legal, technology or 
sustainability, they could access the third level and partner up with other knowledge 
stakeholders in an outside-in approach (Enkel et al., 2009) and thus, facilitate the 
success of individual dimensions and levels within the SMELTS framework. This 
proposed third level could be analyzed in further qualitative research, as it was not 
directly mentioned in the sample of this thesis. Furthermore, the interrelation of this 
proposed third level should be discussed, especially the extent to which this new level is 
affecting and is getting affected by the traditional layers of the framework. 
6.3 
Business Model Innovation for Nordic SMEs in the 
context of the SC initiatives in Singapore 
6.3.1 
Born global and the Uppsala model of internationalization 
in Singapore 
As the literature research shows, there are different ways of internationalization for 
companies of all sizes. Interviewees differentiated their internationalization process 
between the Uppsala model and the born global model. Whereas the sample laid a 
strong emphasis on scaling their venture after the born global model, only a few 
interviewees followed the Uppsala model of internationalization. However, the results 
and answers of this sample are to be seen critically, since the SCWL innovation lab is 
already a call for global growth and rapidly scaling in the foreign market of Singapore. 
When applying the born global model, it is essential to understand the prior experience 
of the companies. Businesses that had prior experience in internationalization were more 
realistic in the assessment of barriers to internationalization in Singapore. Businesses 
that had little to no prior experience were more uncertain in reasoning their participation 
in the SCWL and going international with their business towards Singapore. Interestingly, 
intermediary I1, who has been leading the SCWL, mentioned that the born global model 
of internationalization is not as applicable for the Singaporean market as presumed at 
the beginning of the SCWL innovation lab in 2017 (I1, line 45).  
Seeing this statement of I1 in perspective of the businesses’ ability to assess the barriers 
of internationalization realistically, it is recommended to gather experience in 
==PAGE:94==
Discussion 
93 
internationalization in other countries than Singapore, before deciding to enter South 
East Asia. Additionally, companies should follow the advice of (Bloch et al., 2008) and 
assess the new market and the opportunities in Singapore even before considering to 
participate in innovations such as the SCWL. This market assessment should happen at 
latest during the innovation lab that is aiming to prepare the SMEs to enter the 
Singaporean market. Moreover, SMEs should be aware that stakeholders in Singapore 
are mostly interested in collaborating with products and services, which were 
successfully tested in other markets, as seen in the results. The Singaporean mindset of 
not being Early Adopters and having a risk-averse view on entrepreneurship is a call for 
SMEs to proof their BMs with empirical data and best practices in order to convince 
Singaporean stakeholders to collaborate with them. 
In summary, SME´s are advised to prove their products and services and build a strong 
market position in other countries, as well as, to develop a deep understanding of 
customer needs before entering the Singaporean market. 
6.3.2 
Business Model Innovation in Singapore 
Capturing and redesigning the BM of the company for internationalization in Singapore 
is an important step to be able to deploy innovation in a new market. The results show 
many factors that triggered innovation for the interviewed businesses in Singapore (5.5.2 
Business Model Innovation for the Singaporean market). The results show that BMI in 
Singapore can happen due to different reasons. Connecting these to the literature 
(Frankenberger et al., 2013), the results reveal that businesses address all of the four 
central dimension of BMI: the Who, the What, the How, and the Why. 
In the literature review, Frankenberger et al. (2013) suggest that BMI should take place 
only when one or multiple components of the BM are changed. However, in the case of 
the SCWL and the internationalization in Singapore, it is questionable if this statement is 
feasible. With the internationalization in Singapore, the change of the Where is axiomatic 
given since businesses deploy their products and services in another market, other as 
the one in which they are already active. Therefore, it is questionable if the change of 
the Where in the BM is solely enough, to call an internationalization the discovery of 
radically new customer value propositions and to solve a problem that has never been 
solved before, as Johnson et al. (2008) defines it. In countries like Singapore that are 
fast in adopting innovations and have been proven in other markets, it is not enough 
offering the same product or service only. The change of only one component in the BM, 
restrictively the Where in the internationalization, might thus not be enough to call, the 
offering in the new market, an innovation but is instead seen as an invention. According 
==PAGE:95==
Discussion 
94 
to Mitchell and Bruckner Coles (2004, p. 14), BMI only takes place when the change of 
one component meets a customer group, that is buying the offering. Thus, this thesis 
suggests that in the case of internationalization, as in the SCWL case, BMI is not 
generally given by the change of only one component but should instead be seen 
holistically and customer-centric. Frankenberger et al. (2013) themselves state that BMI 
exceeds the simple step of just introducing a new product or service offering, but that it 
should engage in economic exchanges. Often, companies need to adapt their offered 
values towards the customer needs (Who), since their desires might change in a different 
market setting. Furthermore, different market scenarios might require an adaptation of a 
technology or service (What). For example, the climate can be a factor that forces 
companies to adapt their technologies, as a solar panel that is distributed in a sunny 
country, might not find a market to engage in a cloudy market scenario where little 
sunshine can be used to generate energy. Finally, different markets demand different 
price structures or have higher or lower margins (Why). Companies that do not engage 
in this dimension might be forced to quit their internationalization plans due to insufficient 
financial planning. Thus, only if the new offering meets the needs of a customer base, it 
can be called an innovation. This statement can be supported by the data, which shows 
that the simple copy of the BM in Singapore is not enough to succeed (5.5.2 Business 
Model Innovation for the Singaporean market). All interviewed businesses of the SCWL 
recognize that they need to change more than just the Where within their BM, to be able 
to innovate in Singapore successfully. 
Furthermore, the Singaporean market requires an additional effort of the businesses in 
finding the right market fit. Factors, like the climate, legislation, or missing technological 
infrastructure require a rethinking of the current BMs and demand an adaptation of the 
products and services. OI can help businesses to find the right market fit in Singapore. 
OI projects, such as the SCWL, enable SMEs to address specific challenges in a new 
market, as seen in the SC initiatives in Singapore, while preparing them to 
internationalize by giving guidance and facilitating the adaptation of the BM, with the goal 
to create innovation. The sample suggests that this can affect the speed and impact of 
innovation deployment (I3, line 24). Nordic SMEs can excel with their BMI in Singapore 
by collaborating with other stakeholders in OI projects. 
6.4 
Methodological strengths and weaknesses 
After discussing theories and concepts in this thesis, it is essential to discuss how 
sensitive the results are to methodological choices and how qualitative the chosen data 
of this thesis is. The explorative nature of this thesis fitted the purpose and aim of 
==PAGE:96==
Discussion 
95 
answering the RQ and led to several new findings as discussed above (6.1 Bridging the 
deployment of OI to overcome SC challenges in Singapore; 6.2 Singapore’s Smart City 
put into perspective; 6.3 Business Model Innovation for Nordic SMEs in the context of 
the SC initiatives in Singapore). In the literature review, there was little to no research 
and academic work that connected the two fields OI and SCs, especially with regards to 
how SMEs can deploy OI practices to solve SC challenges. Therefore, an essential 
aspect of this thesis was aimed at exploring and describing both concepts in a new 
context of Singapore, and to connect both fields of research. Analyzing the relationships 
between OI and SCs added an explanatory dimension to this thesis and helped in finding 
answers to the RQ in an explorative, descriptive, and explanatory way. Furthermore, the 
analysis of the results and their connection with the prior academic work has implications 
for theory as well. 
In this thesis, initial information was gathered by supporting the DTU Management team 
in the SCWL innovation lab. Specifically, information was gathered by supporting the 
SCWL with reports and academic papers, through analysis of the Singaporean 
CleanTech market and preparations for workshops intended to equip SMEs for 
internationalization in Singapore. This information helped to formulate an interview 
framework with which data was gathered. It also helped to understand and analyze the 
data by which the analytical and theoretical framework was constructed. Moreover, the 
iterative approach to writing the thesis - by going back and forth between theory, data 
and the analysis of data - helped to gain a holistic overview of the topic. Nonetheless, a 
critical look at the applied iterative approach proved to be challenging. Revisiting and 
rewriting sections was in retrospect time-consuming, and new aspects were continuously 
found in the process, which led to the difficulty of combining various topics that are 
relevant but also inter-related for answering the RQ. 
Furthermore, building the theoretic framework of this thesis was found to be challenging, 
even though the initial information was gathered through the SCWL. The theories used 
in this thesis were not pre-determined. The iterative process enabled the finding and 
analysis of relevant topics that are needed for the analysis of the data and helped to 
understand the subject for readers. Nonetheless, when analyzing the results and 
comparing them with the theoretic framework of this thesis, it is essential to be critical in 
the interpretations of the qualitative data and their meanings in the given context, since 
these are given based on the interpretations of the interviewees. Being critical in the 
analysis of the data was, therefore, a critical aspect of the process of this thesis. 
==PAGE:97==
Discussion 
96 
6.4.1 
Data collection 
Applying a qualitative case study research through expert interviews enabled the 
collection of relevant data that address different aspects of the analytical framework. 
However, specific challenges arose during the preparation, collection, and analysis of 
the interviews. One challenge during the preparation of the interviews was found in 
finding the right scope of focus for the analysis as the research approach is explorative 
and the area of focus was unknown before data collection. Thus, this thesis used semi-
structured interview guidelines (see Appendix C Question guide for companies; D 
Question guide for institutional actors) that covered several themes of the theoretic 
framework and supported the analysis within different thematic fields. In retrospect, the 
use of semi-structured interviews was useful as it allowed a certain degree of flexibility 
and provided depth to the data due to its openness. In particular, the semi-structured 
interviews were used as they allowed in-depth questions on various thematic fields, 
depending on the expertise of the interviewees. However, the interview guidelines were 
continuously adjusted, and irrelevant questions were omitted, while new questions were 
added throughout the interview process. Thus, it is to assume that the final expert 
interviews might be more abundant in data, as the interviewer became more experienced 
in both the topics of the research and asking techniques to find new answers for the RQ. 
For future research, it might be beneficial to conduct follow up interviews to confirm the 
consistency of answers through a broader number of interviewees. 
Moreover, it is to mention that the attainment of interviews and first knowledge generation 
about the Singaporean context was actively facilitated by the collaboration with the 
SCWL, which served as gatekeeper to a broad range of businesses and intermediaries, 
which were interviewed in this thesis. The innovation lab facilitated the possibility of 
interviewing intermediaries of the EU. The contact with the businesses was exclusively 
enabled through the SCWL. While the use of this gatekeeper was highly beneficial for 
acquiring experts involved in the internationalization of SMEs towards Singapore, it 
should also be seen as critical. As an interviewer, it was important not to reveal sensitive 
information that could violate the respondents’ position in the innovation lab and its 
participation, although all interviewees agreed to share the results of the interviews. 
Additionally, the SCWL focused only on SMEs that addressed CleanTech solutions for 
Singapore. Thus, exploring the RQ in a broader context could lead to new findings. The 
limitations of the quality of data will be discussed below. 
==PAGE:98==
Discussion 
97 
6.4.2 
Quality of data 
To conduct an empirical study of high quality, it is necessary to adhere to the quality 
criteria for research: controllability, validity, and reliability. It is necessary to work towards 
these criteria, to reach the goal of intersubjective and representative results. For this 
thesis, it was relevant to conduct expert interviews and to meet with the relevant 
stakeholders involved in the SCWL case, instead of doing a desk study. 
The controllability of data refers to the requirement to be able to trace back results. The 
controllability explains how what and under what circumstances the researcher has done 
his research and was able to draw his conclusions (Van Aken & Romme, 2012). In this 
thesis, controllability is achieved by providing memos of the circumstances under which 
the interviewees were interviewed and through recording and transcribing the interviews. 
Furthermore, building up a coding-tree on MAXQDA, which goes deep into each thematic 
block, shall enable future research to follow the logic of this research. 
Results which are independent of the particular characteristics of the study and which 
can, therefore, be replicated, can be deemed reliable (Yin, 2017). Similar to the 
controllability, attention was paid to recording and transcribing data from the interviews 
and the coding process to increase the reliability of this thesis. Providing a list of codes 
used during the analysis supports the reliability of data, which is given since any scholar 
that has the same access to data should be able to present similar results and findings. 
However, as mentioned above, the connection of the researcher to the SCWL might 
influence some of the respondents as participants in the innovation lab. Nevertheless, 
through ensuring confidentiality of the findings and presenting as an independent 
researcher, it is to believe that the respondents felt confident in sharing their own 
opinions. 
The validity of data answers the question of whether the sample truly represents the 
phenomenon that is claimed to be researched (Van Aken & Romme, 2012). Validity 
refers to both the internal validity of research methods and the external validity of the 
findings. The nature of this thesis is explorative and shall find answers on the prior 
discussed RQ, supported by a qualitative research method, which allowed both flexibility 
and openness. Simultaneously, the research approach enabled this thesis to gain in-
depth and contextualized data with the scope targeted for Singapore. Furthermore, the 
iterative approach towards data and theory supports the internal validity. The iterative 
approach of this thesis leads to important discoveries, which influenced the analytical 
framework and led to new topics in the theory. 
The external validity of research refers to the generalizability of the results. The external 
validity of this thesis is debatable as it is meant to study the explorative issue of deploying 
==PAGE:99==
Discussion 
98 
OI in the context of the SC initiatives in Singapore. Thus, the external validity is instead 
seen in the contribution with further insights on the topics and context. The of the sample 
delimitation on SMEs that are solving issues for Water- and Waste-Management might 
limit the generalizability of the results of this thesis and future research is needed, to 
generalize the results. Nevertheless, the findings provide insight into the innovation work 
of Nordic SMEs that aim to deploy their BMs in the context of the SC initiatives in 
Singapore. This research thus provides a starting point for further research, which could 
make use of elements of the findings of this thesis. 
==PAGE:100==
 
 
 
99 
7 CONCLUSION 
This thesis empirically investigated how Nordic SMEs can deploy OI to solve the 
challenges of SC initiatives in Singapore. To the best of my knowledge, this thesis is the 
first to analyze this phenomenon that combines both fields of research OI and SCs in the 
context of Singapore. The thesis aimed to contribute to debates regarding OI, SCs, and 
BMI at a firm level of Nordic SMEs. Via a qualitative case study and a SNA, this thesis 
provides a conceptualized and contextualized research, based on qualitative data 
gathered through semi-structured interviews with seven businesses and four institutional 
intermediaries out of the SCWL during the time frame of March to May 2018. 
This thesis was structured based on an analytical framework that indicated the link 
between OI and SCs and highlighted that both encounter similar challenges. The 
challenges as presented in the theory (2.1.3 Challenges of Smart City initiatives; 2.2.4 
Open Innovation challenges and best practices for SMEs) of OI and SCs were partially 
confirmed by the sample, while new challenges were explored. Furthermore, the 
interviews shed light on a potential framework that connects the deployment of OI and 
SC initiatives through innovation platforms, such as the SCWL innovation lab. In this 
potential framework, certain archetypes facilitate the deployment of OI for the SC 
initiatives and help to connect both contexts. Furthermore, the conceptualization of OI 
and SCs through a SNA revealed theoretical relations between both fields of research 
and new methods of addressing SC challenges through OI were identified. 
Attempting to categorize the Singapore context to the SC framework according to 
Chourabi et al. (2012) and Joshi et al. (2016), revealed the need for an additional third 
level within the framework, that is potentially able to assist the individual dimensions of 
the frameworks through OI collaboration and, thus, provide SCs with external knowledge, 
partners, and innovations when needed. Moreover, categorizing the SC initiatives 
through the SC framework based on Joshi et al. (2016), contextualized the framework 
and revealed areas of the SC Singapore, that should be improved, to facilitate 
innovations within the city. 
The analysis of the BMI of Nordic SMEs for their internationalization in Singapore 
revealed exciting findings on existing research (Frankenberger et al., 2013). Insights of 
various forms of BMI contributed to the analysis of four distinct types of BMI, namely in 
==PAGE:101==
Conclusion 
100 
the dimension of the Who, the What, the How, and the Why. The analysis revealed that 
Nordic SMEs are often forced to innovate within at least one of these dimensions, when 
planning to enter the Singaporean market, due to several triggers and barriers that the 
Singaporean market exhibits. Furthermore, this thesis identified specific constraints in 
the BMI framework of Frankenberger et al. (2013) in the context of internationalization of 
a company. Thus, this thesis highlights that BMI is not axiomatic given, if a company 
changes or replaces one of the dimensions in the framework of Frankenberger et al. 
(2013), but rather the acceptance of a satisfied customer group that is willing to buy the 
products and services of a company, after the change and replacement of one of the 
dimensions in the framework. Additionally, a lack of infrastructure, complex government 
structures, and cultural barriers were found to be constraining for the BMI of Nordic SMEs 
in Singapore. 
In summary, answering the RQ of how Nordic SMEs can deploy OI practices to solve the 
challenges of SC initiatives in Singapore has provided insights into the various forms of 
OI practices. Nordic SMEs are engaging in various forms of OI practices that are aiming 
to facilitate their BMI in Singapore and ultimately address the challenges of the SC 
initiatives in Singapore. Finally, these insights can be translated into practical 
implications for SMEs, as well as actors involved in the transformation of SCs, which 
framed the problem field of this thesis. 
7.1 
Implications for Theory and Practice 
The findings of this thesis include some practical recommendations for theory and 
practitioners. This research’s findings contribute to three streams of literature on the 
topics of OI, SCs, and BMI. First, this thesis extends the literature on OI and SCs and 
connects both fields of research by a suggested framework of OI deployment in SCs. 
Furthermore, this thesis conceptualized and contextualized the existing literature on SCs 
and BMI on the Singaporean context within the CleanTech sector. Additionally, alongside 
already conducted firm-level research on the barriers of OI for SMEs, this thesis 
enhances knowledge on the barriers of OI in the context of SCs. Therefore, this research 
adds value to the literature of OI as it is of high importance for businesses to adjust their 
OI processes when working together with SC stakeholders. In this respect, the results 
show that a collaborative approach between cities and innovative businesses is highly 
beneficial for both parties and helps them to overcome the critical challenges within OI 
for SMEs and SC initiatives. 
Consequently, this thesis provides useful information for practitioners. The findings of 
this thesis highlighted that the Singaporean SC initiatives are guided by a strong SC 
==PAGE:102==
Conclusion 
101 
vision of the government, which sets high goals for the transformation of the country. 
However, it was emphasized that some dimensions within the SC are still in an early 
phase of their development, such as the city’s infrastructure, or the lack of transparency 
in the management of the individual government institutions. An intensified focus in 
developing the weak dimensions within the SC of Singapore will facilitate future 
innovations, coming to Singapore, to spread out faster. The government should raise 
and enforce standards within these dimensions of the SC framework, to ensure that 
innovative businesses meet a high standard in Singapore, on which new solutions can 
be deployed. This thesis is aware that the Singaporean government is already engaged 
in this regard, but further efforts must still be made. 
For businesses aiming to enter the Singaporean market, the focus should be on planning 
the BM adaptation within the country, as Nordic solutions can, in most cases as seen in 
the sample, not solely be copied into another market without further development. 
Finding strong partners that know the Singaporean market, can help to internationalize, 
and to overcome significant obstacles during the BMI in Singapore is crucial. Partners 
are not just characterized as other companies or joint venture partners but also as OI 
platforms, such as innovation labs, accelerators, incubators, or even universities or other 
research institutes. 
Finally, it is recommended that intermediaries, that are aiming to connect OI projects and 
SCs, should consider the barriers of collaboration. These can be addressed by aligning 
time requirements of cities and businesses, building trust between the individual 
stakeholders, and considering the maturity of companies that will be introduced to the 
city stakeholders. 
7.2 
Suggestions for Future work 
This thesis highlights some issues that can be important for the optimal design of future 
research in the fields of OI, SCs, and BMI. The exploratory nature of this thesis already 
provides several avenues of future research and further investigations. Firstly, the 
delimitation of this thesis on the CleanTech sector, particularly on businesses that are 
providing Water- and Waste-Management solutions, provides the possibility of analyzing 
the RQ on a broader scope that goes beyond the CleanTech sector. A starting point for 
future research could be investigating the deployment of OI practices in Singapore, as 
found in this thesis on a broader scope. Furthermore, future research could include a 
more detailed look from the perspective of institutional intermediaries that are facilitating 
the deployment of OI in Singapore. Moreover, the view and perspective of Singaporean 
stakeholders on OI and BMI could be beneficial, as this thesis mainly researched the 
==PAGE:103==
Conclusion 
102 
phenomenon from the perspective of stakeholders from the Nordics. Singapore’s SC 
initiatives offer several exciting sectors, which are calling for OI projects within the 
energy, transport, and mobility, and waterfront sectors. 
Secondly, the two fields of research of SCs and OI are still not fully discovered. As this 
study is mainly focused on how SMEs can deploy OI practices for SC initiatives, future 
research could connect both topics and study to what extent OI can help SC initiatives 
and investigate the impact of OI projects on the development of SCs. For example, the 
directions of the influence in the SNA is still undiscovered and could be researched in 
future work. A quantitative study could help to understand the impact of OI projects on 
SC initiatives and research to what extent these are useful for the city development. 
Thirdly, the newly proposed frameworks of the findings could be analyzed in future 
research. Further qualitative work could confirm the proposed framework for the 
deployment of OI in the SC of Singapore. Additionally, this framework could be 
generalized, since it has only been researched in the SC context of Singapore. Moreover, 
it is interesting to analyze the role of OI as a substitutive third level within the SMELTS 
framework of SCs. The role of OI inside the SC is still insignificantly researched. To shed 
light on the role of OI within SCs could help to clarify the work of triple helix collaborations, 
as these are dependent on OI projects of the private, government and public. 
Finally, the role of OI platforms in the context of SCs, such as the SCWL, could be 
researched in detail in future work. The supporting role of such OI platforms in supporting 
the innovative work of SMEs has been proposed in this thesis. However, the literature 
review did only reveal little to no academic work that examines the role of such OI 
platforms, which connect SC initiatives and businesses. Especially, quantitative 
researches could investigate the impact of such OI platforms on the businesses ability 
to deploy innovations for a SC. Thus, analyzing on a large scale a businesses’ speed of 
innovation deployment and potential market impact could be an appealing avenue of 
future research.

F  TRANSCRIPTS OF INTERVIEWS 
1 
2018.03.20_10.00 
March 2018 0:42 MINS 
2 
Interviewee: I1 
[Other comments: University] 
3 
I: Thank you very much for taking the time for the interview. Could you for 
us maybe just a describe your organization and what you do in it? And could 
you describe the background of the Smart City World Labs initiative? 
4 
I1: So yes, I do not know if you have that entire organizational relationship with 
DTU and NTU and Singapore? 
5 
I: The SCWL maybe? 
6 
I1:  SCWL was started based on a report by Quercus group, an international 
consultancy and the Danish embassy in Singapore. The report focused on 
Singapore’s - by that time - very new and recent smart nation plan and the 
opportunities that Danish companies could find within Singapore. So, I believe they 
highlighted the mobility sector and the lighting sector. That was the initial phase. 
SCWL has been running in two phases. Phase one, where we have the final 
reporting tomorrow with the Danish industry foundation, that was a two-year 
program with a really broad scope to try to figure out what was possible. The dream 
was to have living labs in Denmark and in Singapore and being able to exchange 
companies between them. So, a Danish company would test out their smart city 
technology in a Danish living lab and then go to Singapore and set it up in a 
Singaporean living lab, in a tropical setting and then adapt to the local 
surroundings.  
7 
I: Do you remember the government initiated but this product is based on?  
8 
I1: no, I think it was roughly little smart nation plan. Singapore’s smart nation plan. 
9 
I: So, the IT Vision 200 plan is already too old? 
==PAGE:122==
Transcripts of interviews 
123 
10 
I1: Yes. I’m not sure but anyway that was actually before I started on a project and 
before I was hired I am now half a year into the project and I’m responsible to run 
the DTU part. And then the second phase start it up in September last year when 
we were quite a bit more focused and in where we have found some organizations 
in Singapore to be detailed three organizations. The harbor, a consultancy firm and 
a University. We have asked them to provide some challenges consultancy firm 
and the University with us room to provides some challenges that they cannot 
really see solved right now with the capabilities in Singapore.  
11 
Now we found all of these challenges to Nordic echo system and smart city 
companies hoping to find some really innovative solutions. 
12 
I: What are the specific challenges that you have right now in the project? 
13 
I1: It is focused around CleanTech mostly because of course due to the fact that 
the funding partner of the projects if Climate KIC. In some way the challenges 
should revolve around a Co2 reduction. We have challenges within better batteries 
for electrification of the harbor. We have challenges for the CO2 emission 
reduction for the consultancy challenge, coupled with urban farming. And we have 
within renewable energy integration at the university waste reduction and also 
water reduction as a challenge. So quite broad. 
14 
I: Maybe if we go into the next topic, which innovation and Open Innovation 
(OI). This is about your personal opinion. What do you think, how do you 
understand the term innovation and what is the difference between 
innovations made in Western companies, vs. innovations made in 
Singapore? Or is there actually a difference? 
15 
I1: So at least speaking from Danish companies versus the ones in Singapore, I 
think while there is a lot of talk in Singapore about early adopters, the truth in my 
opinion is that most of the talented people in Singapore are not seeing 
entrepreneurship as a way of making a living. So, the top tier students in Singapore 
don't choose to go into entrepreneurship but go to large companies and this is 
actually something that we have confirmed from researchers at NTU in Singapore. 
As I see it in Denmark and in DTU you can almost see that there is a Rock star 
approach to the entrepreneur. It's even more cool than being like a top 
management consultant. So, if you can make it on your own with a start-up that's 
really the Highmark, at least for an increasing group of students. I think in Denmark 
==PAGE:123==
Transcripts of interviews 
124 
- compared to Singapore - the possibility for innovation is bigger because the 
population that is seeing innovation is bigger whereas in Singapore there might be 
more incremental innovation in big companies in the giving framework. But of 
course, then you also see innovation there. But I think one of the researchers we 
talked to, who is looking into this was saying is that: while Singapore is boasting to 
be an Early Adopter of technology, that is not true. But what they really are is that 
they are a fast follower. So, they want to see results of some of these new 
innovations and then the implement quite rapidly. So, in Denmark you would see 
that it is easier to get into pilot projects and tests and it takes more time to scale 
up. And in Singapore the entry barrier to pilot testing seems quite high. 
16 
I: Maybe to see it in a different way, having the talent in the backdrop, which 
you just mentioned, would you say that innovations in Singapore are more 
locally driven or coming from external sources? Having the talent situation 
and innovation labs in the mind? 
17 
I1: What we see in Singapore, some of the most successful and innovative 
companies are run by westerns. That is the answer basically. 
18 
I: So, there is actually a need for innovations coming from foreign countries? 
19 
I1: There definitely is. And there is a big focus area in Singapore. At least in some 
circles there is a big awareness about the risk awareness that maybe still resides 
in the Singaporean culture. Take the example of the US, where failing a start-up is 
not an issue. You are only seen as an entrepreneur when you have failed three 
times and maybe had a success. I think in Denmark, we are close to that where in 
Singapore a failure is a failure. You risk losing your face. That is why probably a 
lot of Singaporeans are deterred from starting up. 
20 
I: If we stay in Innovations what do you think what kind of Innovations do we 
see mostly in Singapore? Maybe products? Services? 
21 
I1: I think you do see a lot of services. The thing with Singapore is that - to my 
understanding there is a lot of R&D, but the foundries, meaning the factories 
building stuff are actually found in China. Same as Hong Kong, you would 
conceptualize and make prototypes, but the actual production of hardware will be 
outsourced. 
==PAGE:124==
Transcripts of interviews 
125 
22 
I: What do you think about the ability of Singapore to cooperate with other 
cities and innovations, do you think this is something that is important for 
Singaporean innovations? Or would you rather say that innovations are born 
inside of a domestic market?  
23 
I1: I think in Singapore as in Denmark, maybe even more in Singapore they are 
very aware that they are a very small market, its 5.5 million people, so the domestic 
market is very small. The companies in Singapore do not really see the whole 
market as a testing ground. And they are quite aware that the surrounding markets 
have a significantly different buying power that what they see in Singapore. They 
speak a lot about the ASEA markets, so not China and India, but Malaysia and 
Vietnam and Indonesia. So, what they are really focused on is creating products 
and services that are quite cheap - to be honest - to be able to fit those markets. 
And you can say that this is maybe a difficulty for Danish companies to handle. In 
Denmark we tend to create the Rolls Royce model, the BMO - Big expensive 
equipment. And we can sell that in Singapore, but in Singapore they know that it 
is not viable to export that to their surrounding markets. So maybe there is a good 
fit between Denmark to Singapore, but then there is a next step of going from 
Singapore to the rest of Asia. That is a big leap. 
24 
I: So that is a challenge in Singapore. I know you said already a lot about 
innovation labs, but what do you think how can Danish/ Nordic companies 
use these facilities to foster innovation in Singapore? What should they do? 
25 
I1: Singapore is as said very tricky, because the entry barrier is quite high to get 
into these pilot projects. However, ones you are in, there a quite a few of these 
living labs, like Changi Airport has a commercial living lab, where it will cost a bit 
of money coming in to test their prototype there, but the companies are quite willing 
to do so, because they know there will be a very big opportunity for large orders 
afterwards. I think for Danish companies, if they have the capabilities of setting 
aside some time and money for developing in these living labs in Singapore – I 
think there is a big potential for scaling up within the market. And then having 
strong partners when going to China, or India, or to the closest surrounding market 
like Indonesia. 
26 
I: Do you think that start-ups should actually be physically present in the 
market, or like the Born global companies that are handling everything from 
==PAGE:125==
Transcripts of interviews 
126 
one market and try to scale it into different international countries. Is there 
the need of a physical appearance if you want to be successful in Singapore?  
27 
I1: I definitely think that’s a factor. Both for legal reasons, for partnering up creating 
joint ventures with Singaporean entities, receiving funding from Singaporean 
sources, they require that you have a legal status as a Singaporean company – 
most of the funding opportunities there. But then there is also just the logistics, it 
is a seven-hour time difference. To form a close partnership and to build a market, 
I would say you need local presence. 
28 
I: To jump into the next topic of the smart city initiatives in Singapore. If you 
think about Singapore and the smart initiative of the government: What do 
you think are the most important stakeholders? E.g. public, private, foreign 
investors, or maybe others? 
29 
I1: To my mind, the government is the most important stakeholder, maybe even 
number one, two, and three. Everything ties back into some agency, or some 
ministry. While you do have very big monetary funds, like Temasek, the 
government will typically have a major shareholder part in those companies. 
Everything is kind entangled together and the government typically has a stake in 
major companies. But you also see universities, such as NTU which has quite an 
ambitious smart city plan. You see companies like the ones that we have in our 
program. Surbana Jurong is especially looking very much into smart city initiatives. 
There are other stakeholders. The public sector is just massive. The building sector 
– housing in Singapore, 80% of the population in Singapore live in public housing. 
So, if you want to roll out any sort of IoT device within homes, then you need to go 
to HDB, the Housing Development Board, which is a public entity. It is the one and 
only player. 
30 
I: So, if a start-up wants to promote their innovation, they should go the 
government? 
31 
I1: Yes, and that’s the problem. Because it is very hard for start-ups to get access 
to the government. We had quite a few meetings with very promising start-ups that 
have had success in Denmark later on. Finally, we were lucky enough to be 
received in the Public Utility Board (PUB), so the water ministry. There we had 
three promising companies. They received us, I think mostly because we had the 
Danish embassy and their “seal” opening the door and they were polite and 
==PAGE:126==
Transcripts of interviews 
127 
actually also impressed by the products that we promised. But then there was a 
long procedure where they mentioned that they wanted to see results on this and 
that level, before we can even consider them to enter the living labs.  
32 
I: In this case it was helpful to have an OI to access government funds, or 
even just to talk? 
33 
I1: I wish it would have been even more open. We only got to the initial talk and 
then we formed partnerships about funding applications for Temasek, which is the 
government itself. But it never really took off from there. Whereas in Denmark, for 
these companies it has not really been a problem to find a funding or pilot test sites 
and growing their business. I think in Singapore, we talked to EDB, which is the 
Economic Development Board, another public funding agency, they said: yes, we 
do fund start-ups. They funded Uber and another major start-up. When they start-
up, they mean unicorns and no-risk and billion Euro potential start-ups. We see 
start-ups differently. 
34 
I: There is still a lot to-do, but what do you think is the status quo of 
Singapore’s smart city initiatives and how could it possibly develop in the 
future? Your opinion matters here. 
35 
I1: Right now, it seems like they extended the pilot phase a little bit. Initially they 
had two or three years where they wanted to pilot. Then they wanted to go for a 
roll-out. I am not aware of much going on in the roll-out phase. I think they are still 
testing waters and trying to find the proper business cases for these smart city 
initiatives. I think we tend to see the same in Denmark, where we had a lot of pilot 
projects: people setting up sensors in all sorts of places, and now increasingly, 
when I talk to the Copenhagen municipality and when I talk to DTU, their focus is 
not anymore on setting up sensors everywhere, but it is on getting the most out of 
the data that is collected. Finding new methods of doing that, so bringing the 
investment level down and maximizing the output level. I am not sure if it is the 
same in Singapore. If it is, then it you would see service companies, software 
developers probably being more successful in Singapore rather than hardware 
developers. That is how I could imagine it in Denmark. 
36 
I: How intense is the competition in the market for new and innovative 
products? You said that in some cases the government is shutting down the 
==PAGE:127==
Transcripts of interviews 
128 
talk to start-ups, is that due to the fact that they want to foster the domestic 
market?  
37 
I1: I think the government is – they do not want to shut down foreign companies. 
The thing is that they really want them to establish themselves, before going into 
any R&D or similar. Yes, there is a lot of focus of building the domestic market and 
fostering entrepreneurship, but in my personal opinion, there is still the cultural 
barrier, that is not easy to overcome. 
38 
I: Maybe summarizing, what do you think are the main challenges in 
Singapore and thinking about Danish/ Nordic companies, do they actually 
perceive this cultural gap when they go there and they try to enter the 
market? The main challenges in your opinion? 
39 
I1: For the Nordic companies that we have brought, both from our side and from 
the companies’ side – again we haven’t been finding companies that were mature 
enough for the Singaporean market. So that is a problem. It is a bit complicated, 
because the companies that enter into these export programs, such as SCWL, will 
typically be smaller in size, meaning start-ups, or early SMEs. The bigger 
companies will go in more established programs, for example through the 
embassy. We have access to a certain of group of rather small companies. In 
Singapore they are interested in another group of a bit more established 
companies. The fit might not be as good as we first imagined. And what we first 
perceived, based on the talk in Singapore about being Early Adopters, very 
innovative. That is a major obstacle for Danish and Nordic companies. There is 
also a cultural sense of – A Danish company will typically say that the y expects 
their solution to provide 10 to 15 percent savings, based on a given reason, but 
not an empirical testing. A Singaporean company would rather say, that they know 
that their product will give 3 percent savings, and the adopter in Singapore will see 
that as much more serious and prefer the promise of three percent rather than 15 
percent. They would much rather take three three percent because that is 
something that has been tested and promised. So, there is a cultural gap in the 
way Danish companies address the adopters in Singapore.  
40 
I: So, it is very hard for start-ups to convince in people the Singaporean 
market?  
==PAGE:128==
Transcripts of interviews 
129 
41 
I1: I would say so. Of course, we knew this to a certain extent and this is why we 
wanted to push the living lab agenda, setting up the hardware in living labs, testing 
it out, and then you could say we know it is eight percent of improvement. But even 
that has been quite tricky to set up. 
42 
I: Are there may be other mechanisms how Danish and Nordic start-ups 
could proof their proof of concepts, other than living labs? For example, 
using their ideas in OI opportunities like innovation labs and by that 
convincing the Singaporean market? Would it help to have an investor in 
Denmark? 
43 
I1: Of course, if they can proof a track record of having deployed their product in a 
thousand Danish homes and maybe even collaborating institutes, like 
technological institutes, or the universities and research projects and just providing 
empirical evident, that would help. A Danish investor would help as well, but I think 
we also brought in companies that had the initial funding, had the backing from 
Danish investors, but that has not really opened doors in any major way.  
44 
I: So, they should participate in SCWL?  
45 
I1: There is also – and I am sad to say this – the option of looking on other markets. 
Over the last two years, it has proven to be quite a bit more difficult than what we 
imagined. I believe it would be easier to enter Nordic market. Basically, following 
the Uppsala model. The Born global model might not be as applicable - in my view 
at least – as the Uppsala model, where you go to the new market where you have 
the company familiarity and then you go based on that global. 
46 
I: Jumping into the last block of government activities. This is to get some 
ideas how the government in Singapore could help start-ups. What do you 
think how policy makers should approach the planning of smart cities and 
how are they approaching it in Singapore? 
47 
I1: I think their planning is quite right. They set bold ambitions, they put a lot of 
research funding in play, also a lot of venture capital funding, they do set up pilot 
tests. The problem I see is that they rather go for a few big bets, sort of the more 
organic approach in Denmark where we basically set up a lot of living labs and 
then the entry barrier is really low for companies to enter and then we know that 
maybe one out of twenty companies will be successful. I think it would be 
interesting to see living labs in Singapore that have a different approach to the 
==PAGE:129==
Transcripts of interviews 
130 
companies coming in from the outside that say: it does not really sound plausible 
that your solution will actually work, nut maybe in six months you have adapted, or 
you have come up with something completely different and that might work. Maybe 
a bit more of an open and playful look towards entrepreneurship could be 
beneficial. Then I do think that their set up for scaling up is quite unique, given that 
they have their presence in such a huge area of the domestic market. Even though 
I cannot really come up with examples of them having scaled up in a major way, 
the set up for doing so should be quite capable.  
48 
I: And do you think that the government supports sustainable businesses, 
do they have a focus on that? Or do they generally look on the growth of a

I1: When you say sustainable do you think of?

I: Water management, etc.? 
51 
I1: And Co2 emissions?

I: I mean you have the challenge of water management in the project. Do you 
think that the government fosters innovations in this area?  
53 
I1: Yes. We had a few meetings with the CEO of the sustainable energy 
association in Singapore. The association is actually only ten or eleven years old. 
What she said is that over the last four years there has been an increasing focus 
on sustainability in Singapore. Before that it was quite lacking, she would be 
knocking on doors, but now people are knocking on her door. I think for the 
sustainability as such there is a growing awareness in Singapore. For the water 
management in particular is and will always be the number one priority of the 
country. That is why we also see major companies like Danfoss, or Aquapurin 
setting up in Singapore and being quite successful. We also see initiatives, such 
as the Danish water technology house, which unfortunately was not successful. 
But we such initiatives being set up in Singapore and they should have the ability 
to grow them. But that is based in the fact, that Singapore is using more water per 
capita than in Denmark, and they have very little access to water. They import a 
lot water from Malaysia. Malaysia is raising the prices of water. There is a big focus 
on increasing the catchment area of rainwater in Singapore. There is a big focus 
on desalination, I think they have four desalination plans and they are planning for 
three more. They are doing a lot in that regard and also in terms of reusing 
==PAGE:130==
Transcripts of interviews 
131 
rainwater for the industry. They have cleaning facilities called new water. There is 
famous picture of the president drinking new water. Even though, it is only used 
for the industry. Water is a major industry and focus area in Singapore 
54 
I: How do you see the partnership between Denmark and Singapore in terms

I1: I do not know who approached who. But there is definitely a lot of interaction.

in November. The climate minister has been there quite a few times. We have the 
former mayor of TMF in Copenhagen, the environmental mayor, was in Singapore 
quite a few times. So, from the high level we do see a lot of interaction. DTU itself 
has a strategic partnership with NTU. I think there is a big export push from 
Denmark towards Singapore. Not as much the other way around, but that is also 
due to the fact that you do not really see a lot of Singaporean start-ups and SMEs, 
due to the facts that we already discussed.  
56

have any other aspect that we should maybe address, this the chance. 
Otherwise thank you very much.

S1.1: In a way it is a little bit contradictory, because the utility board, the PUB, are

not know what comes out, if it is recycled grey water, or if it is fresh water imported 
from Malaysia, or other sources, it might be an issue. For example, the pricing of 
water, even though Singapore has high water scarcity, the water price is so low

the costs of water in Denmark, but they are importing 30 percent of the water from

the user.

hiked their prices in the last 17 years. In 17 years they have increased the price of 
water, I think that is bizarre. The need is really high. Whereas our system makes 
a lot of sense. If you have high water costs here in Denmark it makes a lot of sense 
to install it, you can save a lot of money and resources. But if water is free, or very 
cheap, then there is not a lot of incentive for the user to buy the product.

S1.1: It is a bit similar to the situation in the US, in Las Vegas, where you have a

is one of the lowest in the US, even though they built a city in a desert, with their 
water sprinklers and golf courses.

S1.2: It is a fake city.

==PAGE:138==

why Singapore would be a great market for us, because at a more adapt point we

I: you already talked about culture, but maybe to go into the theory, there are

you try to have a more organic growth and go to countries that are close to

==PAGE:139==
Transcripts of interviews

Born global model, where you try to spread out everywhere and scale rapidly

there is also a high awareness about resource use and high water and energy 
costs. But it was that Singapore arose as an option, that we had not being aware 
of and that seemed like it might be an opportunity to scale fast. If things go through 
in Singapore, they are really scalable and that can go fast. But it is really difficult 
to get through. I do not think it was a choice, but rather an opportunity that arose.  
62 
S1.2: We always know that there is markets elsewhere, like the US California, that

63 
S1.1: There is a lot of places for this opportunity and of course we have to focus.

approaching right now. The same goes for Singapore and other places like in India, 
or the Middle East. That does not mean that we put everything in for those markets. 
We have to find the best market, where we can securely grow organically. That is 
what we came to realize last year.

2018.04.03_10.30 
 April 2018 
 0:46 MINS
