Interviewee: S2 Aquibiq 
 [Other comments: Loud background, People working 
in the background] 
3 
I: Maybe we can start with the background of your company and see where 
you are working in. If you could describe your background of Aquibiq and 
what you are currently doing there? 
4 
S2: My personal background, and my role here at Aquibiq is that I am formally the 
CEO of Aquibiq. We usually call ourselves a spin-out, or start-up from DTU. We 
are one and a half years old, and we develop, as we call it, a technology to help 
people safe water. It is related to water use. That is the role. My personal 
background is that I am not an engineer, but I have my background from DTU. I 
was trained as a geophysicist and I have a PhD from DTU. And I have worked 
there as an Assistant Professor for some time. The company has started on the 
background of that. I started it together with some of my former colleagues at DTU, 
DTU Compute and DTU Photonics. Afterwards more people joined. The product, 
maybe I should say what we do. The product that we had in Singapore and which 
I1 saw, was [Showing prototype, IoT sensor that measures the water usage] this 
one. Time has changed and things look a little different than what they used to 
when we went to Singapore. I am going to try to show things as they were at that 
time, and I will say what has happened since then. We develop a smart water 
sensor to help people safe water. In Denmark you take water for granted. You just 
open the tap and water comes out and that is probably the same in Munich? And 
over the course of the day, the average Dane uses more than 100 liters of water. 
And few are aware of that, that they actually use that much water and even fewer 
know what they use their water for. The product we did is based on the idea, that 
we think that this lack of knowledge of how much water you use and what you use 
it for creates an over use of water. We make a solution that helps you understand 
what you use your water for. There are three parts of that [Demonstrating the 
prototype]. There is the sensor device, which is a prototype version to something 
like that. What we had in Singapore was even cheaper. This one is a bit more 
advanced. But the basic principle of what we had in Singapore was the same. 
There is a sensor that mounts on top of the water meter. This is a water meter that 
measures how much water flows into a building. The water comes in here [Showing 
on prototype] and here is a house with a washing machine and shower and so on. 
==PAGE:144==
Transcripts of interviews 
145 
Then you mount this here on top of it, which pulls the data out from the water meter, 
collects it in the box, where we have a small GSM model, that transmit data to the 
Cloud, where we collect and analyze data. Afterwards, the final element is the App 
[Showing the app]. The whole idea is that you follow your water consumption on 
an app. More than just ow much water you use and when you used it, but also get 
an estimate of what you use your water for. Is it the toilet, the washing machines, 
or the shower and how much is that compared to other households, families, or 
your own consumption? You get a tailored feedback on your performance and 
water consumption. The whole idea is to use this tool as a way to understand you 
water consumption. Our mains selling point has been to say that information and 
awareness of water consumption. You can safe between 10 to 20 percent of water. 
We usually say 15 percent water saving. We can analyze, that a user should 
replace his washing machine, or should change his behavior a little bit. That is the 
main idea, of course we have some extra features like competing with friends. You 
can add alerts about suspicious water usages, like something dripping or detecting 
a water leak. It was something like this, this idea we took to Singapore. It was a 
little less mature, but the basic principle was that.  
5 
I: And since Singapore you started developing and evolving your product. 
Does that have something to do with the internationalization which you did 
last year in Singapore? Did you try to adapt your product for Singapore, or 
was it just something that was natural? 
6 
S2: When we were in Singapore, it was relatively early back then. I think we have 
been working on – when the opportunity emerged, we have been working on this 
for a little less than half a year. We did not have that much to point to. One of the 
first things we did back then was together with I1, to establish a project here at 
DTU. A small demo project where we had an early version of this installed in one 
of the administration buildings at DTU in 409.  
7 
I: So, in the smart campus? 
8 
S2: exactly, the smart campus project. We used that to get some data and a proof 
of concept. We took that – we used that in Singapore, to say that we have this 
project running. We did not adapt anything towards Singapore exactly, but why 
was Singapore so relevant? It was relevant because of – We have plenty of water 
in Denmark. Of course, it is a misuse to pull up water, just to throw it put again. But 
==PAGE:145==
Transcripts of interviews 
146 
in Singapore, there is a real scarcity of water. They are running out of water. There 
is a high awareness of water consumption. Most of the – it is an island with five 
and a half million people, and there is not enough water that comes down from 
above. They desalinate seawater and they import water from Malaysia, and they 
even reuse water and clean it up. There is awareness that you have to safe water. 
That was why Singapore was relevant. Our goal was to go there present the idea 
and plant the idea: that this is actually true that we do not have enough water and 
that is a scarce resource. There was no adaption, but we were looking for 
feedback, what do we need to do to make this a product that you can actually use.  
9 
I: Do you think that there is a difference between the competition here in 
Denmark and in Singapore? 
10 
S2: In the sense within water, Singapore is very much a hub and there are a lot of 
initiatives going on within the water technologies. In that sense, if you get a product 
out in Singapore, or if you are based in Singapore, you stand much better than 
coming from Copenhagen, or being a start-up from DTU. As a way to bigger 
markets. Singapore would be strategically very good as a place to start. In that 
sense, I think the competition is bigger there. Specifically, for this product, at the 
moment we have a water sensor. There is no competition in Singapore, as far as 
we know. The only start-ups are based in California in Silicon Valley. There are at 
least two start-ups and more working with related technologies.  
11 
I: So, you are quite unique in the worldwide market? 
12 
S2: Perhaps there is someone working on something similar that we do not know 
yet. That is very likely.  
13 
I: Going into the block of innovation and OI. What do you think is the 
difference between innovations made in Denmark and innovations made in 
Singapore? Is there any difference?  
14 
S2: I may not be the right to answer this question. I might actually be a little bit in 
doubt. Through this process of SCWL, there were some meetings held and the 
design aspect of Danish products was emphasized quite a lot. I think this is one 
thing were certain parts of Danish start-ups try to make a stand point. It is 
intelligent, but also it looks good. As you can see, that has not been our focus. We 
are more focused on the functionality and getting the infrastructure.  
==PAGE:146==
Transcripts of interviews 
147 
15 
I: Do you think that people in Denmark adopt innovations earlier than in 
Singapore? I had a feedback to this regard in other interviews, but it was 
stated that in Singapore people are more Early Adopters, meaning that they 
just want to test and buy things that have been tested before, other than in 
Denmark. Would you agree to such a statement? 
16 
S2: The thing is – to finish the story with Singapore. We went out and we got 
involved with NTU, the Nanyang Technology University. And we were targeting a 
pilot project there with their Eco campus initiatives as a base line, where we tried 
to get the system out in a number of apartments and see if we can reduce the 
water consumption. This was actually the political and strategic focus of the 
university in Singapore. They were actually very open to our solution. However, it 
stranded on the fact that we did not get any funding in Singapore. We applied for 
a project there, but that application did not go through. 
17 
I: Was it through the Temasek? 
18 
S2: Yes, the governmental Temasek. It did not get funded. It turned out that the 
demand for this initiative was not that big for the universities project. It was a way 
to test things out, but to do that, they were not willing to fund it themselves and we 
did not have the money either to fund it ourselves and to go and fund a pilot project 
in Singapore. We landed between the two situations where the NTU and we 
wanted the projected, but not that much that they wanted to fund it, and we did not 
have the money. This is where that landed in the end. However, we also had some 
initiatives and good conversations with SCWL. They facilitated the meetings. As 
well with the national utility board, the PUB. They were less interested in this. They 
have a technological challenge. The water meters in Singapore are not smart water 
meters. They are old manual and mechanical water meters. They seem to not 
support enough data to do what we try to do. The way we do it is that we collect a 
lot of data, we analyze it, and we look for patterns. We can see if the water use 
was a toilet flush, since when you flush the toilet, it uses water in a certain way. 
When you use your washing machine, it uses water in a certain way. But you need 
a lot of data to do that. And the water meters in Singapore do not have enough 
data to do this. To roll this out in entire Singapore, which was our dream, they 
would need new water meter. 
19 
I: So, they would need to invest by themselves in the infrastructure? 
==PAGE:147==
Transcripts of interviews 
148 
20 
S2: Exactly. And that is not going to happen before the next ten years, at least. 
There is definitely a technical barrier to enter that market. Maybe it is to early right 
now. They are testing out smart water meters. What we were hoping for was a 
pilot, where we could test the solution and see the potential. But we ran into the 
issue with the PUB, that they were actually testing a different and cheaper solution. 
Basically, just an app, where you can follow your water consumption. Not as much 
detail. I feel that they had made the decision politically, that this is the solution they 
wanted to go for. It was my feeling that they already made this decision. We tried 
to test possibilities with another university. The NUS, national university of 
Singapore. We found a professor there who helped us to contact with PUB. That 
was not part of the SCWL, but on the wink of that. This where we actually found 
out that they were already testing out another solution. A cheaper and simpler 
solution. That combination, together with the NTU not being interested in investing, 
showed us that this is not going to happen. This what happened to us. 
21 
I: And do you think that the alternative solution with which they were 
ultimately working with – Do you think that the people in Singapore prefer to 
work with local people? 
22 
S2: I think all other things being equal they would go for something local. I definitely 
felt that they were open. It is a hub for science and technology. I would definitely 
consider them as very open. I doubt that it would be the same here in Denmark. I 
think there is a certain degree of nationalism. And that was also part of the issues 
we had in this process, we are a Danish company and we have a technology that 
we would like to test out in Singapore. What I heard was, that the reason that the 
Temasek application got rejected was partially because there was no upside in 
Singapore for this investment. If they invested in this, the money would go into a 
Danish star-up. Sure, it would be a product that could benefit Singapore, but there 
was no clear upside to the Singaporeans. There was a university that could test 
things, but nothing more. Those were the rumors, there was no written answer, but 
the main applicant on the application said, that that's what he heard. My feeling is, 
yes if we had had a distinct partner in Singapore, that would benefit from it and 
that would have been part of the project, I think we would have gotten a funding. 
23 
I: What kind of partner? An investor? 
==PAGE:148==
Transcripts of interviews 
149 
24 
S2: An investor of course, but it could also be a technological further development. 
If we had a company, or if we had made a small company there in form of a platform 
or affiliate, that would have been the official basis for the product development 
there and the business development would have been better off. We were trying 
to use the university as the argument for going in an invite. We argued that there 
is someone benefiting from this project, namely the university, but I do not think 
that this was enough. I think if we had found someone, either a major company 
ourselves, or if we found someone who could benefit from this, that would have 
been a better approach strategically.  
25 
I: So, partnerships are very important? 
26 
S2: Partnerships are very important and that is one of the main reasons we did not 
succeed. 
27 
I: Do you think there are other mechanisms to find partnerships, than 
innovation labs, such as the SCWL? Could there be another way to get into 
partnerships with Singaporean stakeholders? Or is cold calling the method 
to go? 
28 
S2: To be honest, I think that the SCWL method was good. In a sense that we 
were going there and having meetings with real people. Cold calling is hard, 
especially within these boarders. Meeting people in person increases your 
chances way more. I do not have a clear picture of how it should be done. 
29 
I: But the innovation lab is a good way or start to find partnerships? 
30 
S2: Absolutely, innovation labs are a good way. 
31 
I: Jumping into the topic of smart cities, with focus on Singapore. I think you 
are an expert with your Singapore experience in this matter. Is there 
something that should be there their vision, as for example opening up 
towards companies? If you could phrase it in a kind of vision. 
32 
S2: For Singapore?  
33 
I: Yes. 
34 
S2: (repeating the question). The thing is that we have seen things from our own 
point of view. Our main argument has been, that is a matter of making it possible 
for people to live green, or sustainable. Where it is a matter of choice that you say 
==PAGE:149==

that want to save water, how do I actually do that. In our point of view, it is a matter 
of providing enough information, that you can actually make a choice and live a 
greener, more sustainable, or smart life. I am not a politician and I am not the one 
deciding for Singapore’s smart city initiatives. I would refrain from making too bold 
statements there. 
35 
I: But maybe you can tell who are the main stakeholders in Singapore? We 
heard a lot about the government already, but who do you think are the main 
stakeholders? I wrote some ideas, as for example, public, private, foreign? 
36 
S2: From our point of view, the main stakeholders have been the PUB, the public 
utility board. It is one big utility, providing water for entire Singapore. In that sense, 
if you just convince them, the PUB, then you are already good to go. In Denmark, 
there are 3.000 utilities all across. To reach everyone, you have to talk to 3.000 
companies. It is just one body. But that is of course also a challenge, because this 
body is a huge and slowly moving thing. That also made it hard. We managed to 
get a meeting with them through the SCWL and through the embassy. The 
embassy played a vital role there. They did some work for us and we were a small 
start-up and we actually got a meeting with a lot of stakeholders there. So, the 
PUB, facilitated by the SCWL and the Danish embassy, that was vital. We also 
talked to JTC. 
37 
I: What is the JTC? 
38 
S2: I am trying to remember the abbreviation. [Thinking] It is a public body, or at 
least a private company on which the public owns the majority. They are 
administering most of the business buildings and commercial buildings. They own 
the buildings and rent them out or sell them.  
39 
I: Is it similar to the EDB? 
40 
S2: It is the same, just for companies and for commercial use. They were – It was 
partially the SCL and the Danish embassy, who hooked us up with them. They 
were a huge stakeholder there. They run the buildings and run part of the university 
buildings. There is the green tech building. They had some challenges with their 
water use as well. What we talked about with them at that point was, that they had 
leakages that they would like to detect. As for example, hey there is a toilet leaking, 
can we be informed of that. The challenge there was that we sit on top of the water 
meter, and if we sit on top of a water meter with a building with 100 floors, then we 
==PAGE:150==
Transcripts of interviews 
151 
can detect as well the leaking toilet in the 15 floor. We need more measure points. 
There was a technological challenge. So, the JTC was an important stakeholder, 
and as you mentioned the PDB. We did not connect with them at that point. 
41 
I: And do you think that policy makers in Singapore could approach the laws, 
or regulatory to facilitate start-ups working there? Could it be made easier 
for start-ups to enter Singapore by the policy makers, or the government? 
42 
S2: Yes, I think partially what SCWL was trying to do was to establish a playground 
for start-ups to showcase and run smaller tests. Where they do not have to say 
“yes” to everything, meaning they say “yes” to this small project with one or two 
products or services as a demo, or showcase. I love that idea and I think that is 
also the way to go. Part of our challenge was also that we did not have any pilots. 
We have some pilots going on now in Denmark, but even with them it will still be 
hard to point to them and to say we did this in Denmark, can we do this in 
Singapore. People would response that they need to see it work, they want to 
touch and feel. A small demo lab, or something like the SCWL, that would definitely 
be beneficial.  
43 
I: Do you think that external knowledge is important for innovation in 
Singapore? Are they dependent on external knowledge? 
44 
S2: Singapore is a highly developed country, especially in terms of innovation. I 
am not sure in terms of start-ups for instance. I would definitely consider it a more 
intensive than in Denmark. On water, as we sort of have been dealing with, I would 
say it is a very competitive place and they are pretty good off. I doubt that – I would 
find it hard to go there and say that they should listen to us, because we are Danes. 
I definitely feel in many ways, that Singapore from a technological point of view is 
superior to Danish standards. 
45 
I: But even though you have seen a good product fit for Dripview? 
46 
S2: They have a problem, they have a challenge with water. And that is what we 
were trying to target. We asked us if this is where we should start and forget 
everything about what we do in Denmark and go there immediately. But then we 
ran into the issues. We did not success with the funding and the PUB had other 
political agendas that we found too hard to overcome.  
==PAGE:151==

47 
I: Maybe jumping into internationalization and speaking about your product 
fit. Maybe you can summarize the restraints or weaknesses that you had 
during your time in Singapore. I already heard that you had a very good 
product fit, and that your product was superior to other competitors, but on 
the other hand you had the weakness that maybe the government was not 
so into it. Are there may be other restraints that you see? 
48 
S2: To be honest, I think it suffers from the same challenges in Singapore, as it 
does here in Denmark. It is no secret that we had also challenges with finding the 
spot in the market for our product here (in Denmark). We have been following the 
path of trying to push it out utilities as an extra service. They have customers calling 
that they are concerned about their water consumption, or they do not understand 
their water bill, or they are afraid that they have a leak. What we were trying to do 
is to meet that problem. That is our first paying customer, and that is where we 
landed at a Danish utility. To be honest, it has been very difficult to copy and we 
have other utilities signing up to that service. I think, we have a challenge to solve 
a big enough problem and we ran into that in Singapore.  
49 
I: So, it is very similar than to Denmark?  
50 
S2: Our problem was probably similar to what we see here. Weather it applies in 
general, I am not sure. I can just say things from our perspective. This is more than 
half a year ago, almost one year. Things have changed since then. If we knew 
then, what we know now, then probably we would have made different choices. I 
think we would have been stronger focused on the JTC path. Where we actually 
had some good conversation, but it was not on a trail that we were following back 
then. We did not follow that. 
51 
I: And speaking maybe more strategically. There are two common ways to 
go international. The organic way, also called Uppsala model, where you try 
to go into similar markets, like Sweden, Germany, Norway, that are culturally 
more or less similar. And then the Born global model, that is the Silicon 
Valley approach, where you say that we have a product and we bring it 
everywhere into the world and scale it up. Why did you choose to go the 
Born global way?  
52 
S2: For two reasons. The first and obvious, it became a possibility. Through this 
SCWL and through our connection to DTU, it was specifically Jens who contacted 
==PAGE:152==
Transcripts of interviews 
153 
us. He said that we have something that may be relevant and invited us to apply. 
So, we did. If that did not had happened, we would not have gone there. Obviously, 
we cannot afford to make that choice. That is the primary reason. The secondary 
reason, and the reason why we took part is that there was actually a problem to 
be solved. There was a water scarcity and we knew – our approach has always 
been to get something up and running in Denmark and then immediately go to a 
place where it is an urgent problem. In Singapore it was an urgent problem. I think 
the opportunity came to us. Had that not come, our focus would have been on 
Silicon Valley and the US.  
53 
I: Because the product is the same as in Denmark, and the opportunity? 
54 
S2: Because there is a water issue as well. And they are technological wise on a 
very high level. You have to have things connected and it needs to be smart, and 
that is also where we have our only competitors. I think that is a challenge, but it 
is also a benefit in a sense that you can point to them and say that we are doing 
something like them, except that we do certain things different. Whereas in 
Denmark people ask us what we are actually doing. Competitors can be an 
advantage. People can relate to and you can point to. Where are not the only one 
with this idea, so maybe it is not that stupid. That is what we have been running 
into in Denmark. People are – from the beginning they say that it is a good idea, I 
like, but you will never make money on that. To be honest, maybe people are true, 
but we are still struggling.  
55 
I: Thinking about Singapore again. What do you think is the biggest barrier 
during your time in Singapore? 
56 
S2: The biggest barrier? From our point of view there has been on a technological 
side the biggest barrier has been the fact that the smart meters, or the water meters 
there do not support what we actually would like to do. That is the biggest 
challenge. On the business, or commercial side, it has been the challenge to 
establish closer partnerships with someone. Either moving there, we were not 
ready to do that. Or finding a partner there who would get an upside from us 
succeeding there. More than just a university, because then it is a research project 
and it was s my feeling that it was a way for the university to both tick off on their 
checklist that they are doing something to safe water and also fund a research to 
==PAGE:153==
Transcripts of interviews 
154 
do so. That is not how you make money. You get into the market, but it is not a 
commercial success. A partnership with someone. 
57 
I: Do you think there was a cultural barrier as well? 
58 
S2: No. I do not feel that there was a specific cultural barrier. I felt welcomed both 
as a person, but also as a company.  
59 
I: Jumping into the last block of your general perception. This is just your 
own opinion to get some ideas about topics. What do you think is the legal 
and political environment for a foreign start-up, and have you received 
funding or support? I know that you did not get the Temasek, but any other 
support from the Singaporean government? 
60 
S2: We did not receive anything. [Thinking] There has been – I have been a little 
bit – I know the legal – I guess there are certain – I said that there are no cultural 
differences, but there are. When we made the application for instance, I was 
actually more concerned about getting details correct in a sense that if I violated 
any laws. There is a certain perception that it is a strict country. You go to jail if you 
do this and that. I was from a business point of view more nervous to do something, 
than I would be here in Denmark. Not that I break laws, but I have a better feeling 
for not being run over, if I do not comply with the rules. Whereas in Singapore, I 
am outside of my own habitat, but I am also in a political area where I am not sure 
if I can go to jail if I do something that I am not supposed to. That was one of the 
challenges. As I said, when I went there, the people and all the meetings that we 
had, I felt very welcome. The fact that all statements are correct has been an issue. 
For instance, we discussed very much the statement, whether we [showing paper] 
– what we tried to do is to say that we help to safe 15 percent water. One of the 
challenges was: can we actually say that when we go to Singapore? Can they 
actually legally claim that we promised a 15 percent saving, could we end up in a 
situation where we would have to deliver that. That was one of the challenges. We 
ended up not saying it. We tried to avoid any of these terms. 
61 
I: Do you think that a report could have helped? Or an empirical finding or 
testing? For example, from the smart campus here at DTU? Do you think an 
empirical statement could have helped?  
62 
S2: Absolutely. As a company, we still suffer from the fact that we do not have 
enough data to say: this is how our device works, and this is how our product 
==PAGE:154==
Transcripts of interviews 
155 
works, and this is how much you can safe, this is how much we can predict. We 
do not have that today. I think it would have helped us there, it would also help us 
in our current situation here in Denmark. Afterwards, we met a professor at the 
NUS, the other university in Singapore. He was actually very open. In the university 
context, it had been very open. The meetings we had with public government 
bodies, and JTC, they have also been very open and informative. I think that part 
of the reason that has happened, is through the embassy. The Danish embassy in 
Singapore. They arranged a meeting with the PUB. I also think they were partially 
involved in the meeting with JTC. They have been a door opener in many aspects. 
But as soon as the door was open, people have been very open. Whether we got 
the full picture? Of course not. But then it is on our own to make as much as 
possible out of those contacts.  
63 
I: A last opinion about sustainable businesses in Singapore. Do you think 
that this is something that the government has a focus on, like funding 
sustainable businesses, or do they not care about CleanTech? 
64 
S2: I think with the Temasek call, it is a focus area. Obviously, we did not make it. 
I cannot really say that we hit it. But from what they judge, I think that is a focus. 
Also, from the university, the NTU, they have the Eco campus initiative, where they 
have to deliver 20 or 30 percent reduction in water consumption within a five years 
period. That is also a specific focus for the university. In my opinion, yes there is a 
green orientation of Singapore. From a political and a strategic point of view.  
65 
I: Asking for additional remarks. 
66 
S2: We have some commercial challenges, this is not related to the Singapore 
experience. On the contrary, I think it made us wiser. We have an issue with the 
fact that, we are data oriented and we want to analyze data in percentage and 
make it fun, entertaining and useful. But that requires that there is data, that 
someone is measuring the water flow. In a lot of place, as well in Singapore, that 
is not the case. Our solution to that has been to make our own hardware and try 
to adapt. But right now, we can only play with this water meter from a specific 
brand, but they do not sit in Singapore. They are located in a lot of places in 
Denmark. They are located in certain regions of the world, but it’s not there. Just 
as a bonus info for the status. Right now, we are doing pilots. We are testing it. But 
it has been tough to sell. The thing is that it is hand built, so it is expensive. It is 
==PAGE:155==
Transcripts of interviews 
156 
expensive to make. Of course, when we go up in quantity, things will scale and it 
will become cheaper. There is still a GSM module inside and you need to pay for 
the data transmission. We had a Wife version as well, where we just connect to 
the Wi-Fi and then sort of get it for free. The challenge with Wi-Fi is that it goes on 
and off. Then we lack data. This is why we went with the mobile version. It is more 
expensive, but it works. 
67 
I: Closing

Interviewee: I2 Quercus Group 
 [Other comments: Office] 
3 
I: Thank you very much for taking the time for the interview. Could you for 
us maybe just a describe your organization and what you do in it? 
4 
I2: I come from Quercus Group. Sorry for the washing machine, that might make 
some noise. We are a private consultancy that works with scoping and fundraising, 
defining, and running large multi-stakeholder projects, primarily around smart 
cities, cleantech, and agribusiness is our Focus. Often, we are involved in 
internationalization. Should we go and sit in the kitchen because this might take a 
while. 
5 
[Changing room, due to disturbing washing machine] 
6 
So, starting over. Quercus group is a private Consultancy that works with multi 
stakeholder projects, like I said before, defining, facilitating, scoping projects that 
involve multiple stakeholders within these three sectors and also running quite a 
few bits of project management. That was very briefly, I am partner in the company 
and I have been driving this whole Singapore project that we have been working 
on for the past two and a half years. It started off with me doing a mapping that 
was commissioned by the Danish trade Council in Singapore. The mapping was 
concerned with what are the opportunities for Danish companies coming from this 
smart nation Vision and plan that was launched by the Prime Minister in 2015. That 
is where it started; from there we identified some opportunities and common goals 
and visions. Also, the notion of using the Living lab as an innovation tool and 
methods, as well as an investment promotion tool from the Singaporean point of 
view, which is also which part of the Danish innovation, at least in Greater 
Copenhagen where we have a great focus on living Labs as a method as well. On 
the basis of that analysis we were approached by Gate 21 which you might know, 
DTU, and the embassies saying: “how can we build on the findings that we made 
on the analysis and see how we can bridge this”. We came up with using the Living 
Labs as an export model, trying to bridge Denmark and Singapore, and the 
companies through the Living lab method. We fundraised and then we've been 
working on it- since then. So, we have gone into the second phase now, with the 
funding from Climate-KIC.

have to come up with different ways. Now there is a much larger focus on

transition. There is a difference strategic priority in Singapore now compared to

I: And do you think that the innovations made in Singapore and the

have built your nation on the system that has been put in place. Basically, it has 
been extremely top-down and people have been told this is what you need to do it

control and there has been no room for failure in Singapore’s development project. 
It couldn't fail, because if we failed it would not be what it is today. But I think when 
you talk about innovation you have to give room to some mess, you have to be 
able to embrace that risk of failure and you have to fail every now and then before 
you can actually innovate. I think, Singapore is aware of this, but is still trying to

really found out that they do not have to sell power, this is just a commodity that 
they supply the customer with, but there is much more money in the services. In

model that they have done here in Denmark, is that the utilities are- you are paying 
a subscription for your power fixed, and then the power is bought and sold to the 
customer at zero margin. That means, that the utility is creditable. When they go

==PAGE:174==

Here in Denmark they are also competing on price, but people are not moving,

16 
Not getting into dept is the key element for every development project. There are

in-between is to go to a bank, or Venture Capitalist (VC) and say: I’ll give you 50

agreement made is something that is forgotten and they do what they want 
afterwards. It is- it was a stupid thing to go there. We lost years and a lot of our 
investor’s money by trying to make a difference there. Now, we are trying to 
establish ourselves in Greece. We are looking into the US as well. There we are 
able to communicate, and to speak to people. This is a good start. And in the US 
especially, and in Greece, we have a certain similarity in the legal aspect and 
banking systems and so. It is so much easier to establish companies. Lesson

Singapore?  
20 
I3: Yes, and I think it is important to them to know: what are their boundaries. It is 
one thing to be flexible, but it is another thing to not do anything more than this. 
They need to certain what they want as well.  
21 
I: Would you recommend a kind of, you said before due diligence, before you 
enter Singapore? What would that include, only the policies and resources 
needed for entering Singapore, or what should start-ups consider when they 
plan? 
22 
I3: There are two parts of this story. One is whether you see a solution being 
deployed in Singapore only. Or you are seeing your solution being deployed in 
Singapore for the purpose of using it as a platform to enter the South East Asian 
market. You need to understand where your market is. Singapore, although there 
is a high level of affordability, the market size might be small compared to South 
East Asia. It is about asking, what is the purpose of coming to Singapore. If you 
are to ask Singapore’s needs, then you have to decide if there is enough scale to 
make it worthwhile. But if you are using Singapore as a launch bed for South East 
Asia then you need to understand the nuances within South East Asia and the 
different countries.  
23 
I: The NTU, or the institution where you work in the CleanTech cluster, is 
actually very powerful in terms of building own capacities, building own 
innovations, are there actually innovations coming from foreign countries 
needed for the smart city projects in Singapore?  
24 
I3: Yes, for sure. There is a lot of- it is a question of the solution. Innovation is 
always required and I think there is a difference between impact versus speed. 
People can be working on the same thing and each thing might have a different 
level of impact and way to deploy. The speed of deployment might be different. 
Innovation is always welcome and like all good ideas, they are never unique to one 
person, the question is how well you execute it and how fast do you execute it.

I: Do you think that the Open Innovation collaborations help foreign start-

I3: Repeat please 
27 
I: Open innovation practices, just like the innovation lab, or collaboration

28 
I3: If a start-up is coming here to do collaborative innovation, then yes. They need 
to understand is how they treat their IPs. If you are getting a joint funding from the 
university, then the IPs are shared. You need to be comfortable with that. Unlikely 
the university will set up something to market, it is more a joint licensing, or 
licensing fees. That is something that you need to decide first. Unless really think 
there is a specialty within the Singapore university that you want, to procure their 
expertise to a full funded program then the IP could stay with the company, but I 
think they should allow the IP to be used for research. There are different models 
for the IP, that needs to be talked about.

companies in Singapore to partner up, or to form a joint venture, or some kind of 
agency agreement, so that they can help market the products into the regions, 
because they are experienced in the region, if there is a need for specific 
innovation.

returns. Someone who has sufficient courage, or line of sight, or vision to put that 
investment today for future return. I think, that is something that applies to different 
types of organizations. I think that the government is trying to lower the barriers to

energy efficiency program was set up it had all the different agencies sitting on the

54 
I3: The thing is, smart city is such a broad term. It is education, healthcare, 
transport and more. In every sector there is something happening. In every part of 
a smart city, someone is doing something. If there is a ministry around it, then 
someone is doing something. When we look at CleanTech, the problem with 
Singapore is that it does not have much renewable fossils, except for solar. There 
may be some way to energy and biomass. What do you do, you only have solar. 
The innovation lab around is- what they are doing on the CleanTech side is: how 
can we solve the renewable integration into the grid, for example. How do we 
maximize the production of solar systems, and you will recognize that Singapore 
is very cloudy, the radiance- looking and following forecasts- looking at different 
business models that are using blockchain, or others to see how you can enhance 
[uncompr. 26:48]. How does it interact with the grid, distribution and all that? At the 
moment Singapore has only defined how to treat energy storage from a policy 
standpoint. For example, is energy storage a generator, or something that you use 
to modulate the power. If it is only a generator, then there are certain rules and 
policies that limit the deployment of energy storage, and who can actually deploy. 
What the government also recognized is, own policies may be hurdles for the 
emerging and new technologies, they set up a panel called the pro enterprise panel 
and companies or projects can write this panel and ask for a sandbox project. This 
allows them to pilot certain solutions. What the project will do is to allow the policy 
maker to see how the policies need to be treated: deleted, or a new addition needs 
to be put in place. After that we can move faster as well, otherwise we will always 
have to come up towards the policy makers. No one can rewrite a policy, unless a 
minister rewrite it. That process becomes very difficult, so the easiest thing is to 
make an application, to make a policy sandbox, which is also headed by two 
ministers, so that they can move faster to facilitate innovation and the deployment 
of innovation.

is this huge perception that Asia is China, and China steals all your ideas and you 
might lose everything. I think the legal framework is very strong. They need to 
change their mindset and change the perception of losing their idea. The second 
challenge for Danish and Nordic companies is that they have no understanding of 
Asia and there is a bit of a fear. I think this is where the embassies can be very 
helpful to help them navigate through that.

own SMEs. The other thing is that Singapore has established a new agency, called 
Singapore Enterprise. Singapore Enterprise is set up to help companies establish 
in Singapore. You can be a local company and you can be a foreign company as 
well. 
67 
I: Is this something different than the Temasek? It is not the same? 
68 
I3: No, Temasek is the sovereign well fund. Singapore Enterprise is a government 
agency that came out from a merger between Spring and International Enterprise 
Singapore, IE. They merged this to help companies to enter the market outside 
Singapore. Singapore does not distinguish between- if you are a Danish SME and 
you open up your company in Singapore, you will be considered as a Singaporean 
company. You get the same assistance like the Singaporean companies. So, one 
of the challenges is not knowing what to do when I come to Singapore. How do I 
reach out to the region?

the smart city Singapore? 
72 
I3: There are a few agencies involved with the planning. One is the Urban 
Redevelopment Authority, that tries to look at Singapore as a complete landscape 
and then allocate land. There is a lot of smart city planning from that perspective. 
Then the different agencies- because of the government having the vision of smart 
meters, they will formulate procurement plans. There is quite a bit of planning going 
on and the details of the planning is a function of political will. If the government 
does want a smart nation, this would mean that we do not need smart meters, then 
I can just use my mechanical meters. But because they want us to be a smart 
nation, the agencies need to plan for smart meters and procure smart meters.

Maybe we can start with the background of your company and what you are 
doing there.  
6 
SME3: I was the initiator of the ShowerEcoGuide. My background is engineering 
and I have an MBA. The whole company started around my- I was a bit annoyed 
about my own energy bill, so I wanted to find out on what I am spending money. 
There I ended up in the bathroom and I discovered that, especially when you take 
a bath, it was quite costly if you calculated both the price of water, the heating of 
the water, and especially the discharge to the waste system. There I saw an 
opportunity. Then I looked into which products where on the market, and there was 
nothing that I really liked and I saw them as a little bit difficult and complicated to 
install and also design-wise, they were not really that nice. So, I thought maybe we 
can do this better. That was actually the starting point for the ShowerEcoGuide. I 
have a ten-year history within the energy sector. I know a little bit about the 
customers, and what to do and especially what not to do. When you talk about a 
communication towards the customer, you should be very careful when you are 
communicating KW/h, CO2, or even qm, because not everybody understands what 
it really is. That is why the whole ambition of the solution should be a plug & play, 
meaning very easy to install, but also on a communication level, very easy and 
very simple to follow our solution. That was sort of the whole background. What I 
did after I got the idea back in summer 2016, I spend half a year shooting the idea 
down, or at least trying to shoot it down. That should be understand, that I went 
around and spoke with a lot of potential customers and stakeholders around the 
idea, in order to hear their views on the solution that I had figured out in my mind. 
Nobody really thought it was a bad idea. Then I took the ultimate test before we 
==PAGE:208==
Transcripts of interviews 
209 
founded the company, I sat down with a potential customer and I asked him: If this 
solution were in front of you, would you then buy it? HE was an owner of seven 
hotels in Jutland. He said: I think I would. Then I asked him: What should the price 
be, if the product were here on the table? He said that it should not be more than 
a return on investment over two years. I said to him that I could achieve that goal, 
so again I asked him: If I am able to do this with a price that gives you a return on 
investment of two years, would you then buy it? Then he said, that he thinks he 
would buy it. I asked him to sign a Non-Confidential Purchase agreement. He said 
that he would do it. Then I went home and wrote a very simple one and send it to 
him and he signed. That's the way how way sold 580 units to this hotel chain, 
without having any product. That was actually the start-up of the company. Then 
we founded in March last year.

Interviewee: SME5 AquaGreen

3 
I: [Explaining the thesis] Maybe we can start with the background of you and 
your company and what you are doing there? 
4 
SME5: I don't know how much you know about AquaGreen, but you were sitting 
in the Pitch right? 
5 
I: I wasn't actually. I just read your description. 
6 
SME5: What we do is that we deal with sludge treatment, we have developed this 
solution together with the DTU, where we use superheated steam to dry the sludge 
and after that we have a pyrolysis process where the gases are released and burnt, 
which means that all the energy that is needed for the drying and the pyrolysis 
process is the energy that is within the sludge. By that you do not have to use any 
external energy source. Then that is said, that is not completely correct, because 
of course you have a motor or an engine, you have some wind turbines, so you 
need a little electricity on the side of it. But all the thermal energy that is needed is 
coming from the sludge itself. Then you consume about 20 percent of the energy 
for the heating and the pyrolysis process and 80 percent of the energy that is within 
the sludge can be sold for district heating purposes, or you can use it for local 
process heat if you need that. The end product is about char, where you have 
phosphorus, which is available. That is basically what we are doing, there is a lot 
of focus on this, because of the recirculation of resources, which is very much up 
in time. Then of course the fact that you actually utilize all the energy that you use 
from the sludge. So, the payback, if you can utilize the energy, is vast. This is what 
we do and it was originally invented for the fish farming industry in Norway, where 
they produce a lot of salmons, but what we have seen is that it is very much 
applicable for a lot of other industries and one of them is municipal wastewater, 
which is huge, because you have a lot of municipal wastewater around the world. 
If we link that to Singapore and why this is interesting for us is that the largest 
country of the world, China, where we had a lot of requests from, but we are very 
reluctant to go into China, we do not want the Chinese to copy our technology to 
be very open. I have been living in Beijing and I was working in China, so we have 
some knowledge about how they think. Often, they do not see this as being the 
wrong the to do, to take the technology and use it on the Chinese market. But at 
==PAGE:232==
Transcripts of interviews 
233 
some point, they do that and they will not be able to sell anything. Our intention 
with Singapore is to use Singapore as an Asian hub and then have a demonstration 
solution, that we can show to interested customers in Singapore and then see what 
we can find. Singaporean investors. There are a lot of Singaporean investors that 
have a lot of investments in China. A lot of the Singaporeans are actually originally 
Chinese. So, we see this as an opportunity for us to start up in Singapore, where 
the IP protection is much better than in China. Then go with Singaporeans to reach 
China, this is our idea.

smart people so you have to stay alert when you make agreements with them, but 
I think that is not a risk.

==PAGE:240==

locals, for instance the university. I would rather have a customer that is interested

3 
I: [Explaining the thesis] Maybe we can start with the background of your 
institution and what you are doing in this institution? 
4 
I4: I am the leader of our entrepreneurship activities in the Nordic countries. Our 
main activities that we are running is an accelerator program, where we have an 
active portfolio of 40 companies. 70 have gone through altogether. It is a European 
program, meaning there are six other centers as this Nordic one. This is the largest 
entrepreneurship start-up program within climate innovation in Europe. Altogether 
we have 1000 start-ups that have gone through this program and we typically have 
young companies with one or two founders maybe three. It is a three-stage 
program, when we leave them in stage three they either get a big first order, or 
they get a last investment. Last year, our Nordic start-up raised 22 million Euros. 
We were looking for start-ups that have potential, but of course that also have an 
impact potential within climate mitigation or climate adaptation. 
5 
I: And what is your connection to the government? Climate-KIC is somehow 
connected? 
6 
I4: Climate-KIC is a triple helix program. It was actually initiated by the European 
commission and the Danish, or the membership countries. In that sense the whole 
structure is all about bringing the public and the private sector together. My key 
partners in the entrepreneurship programs are, for example some of the elite 
universities and research institutions in Northern Europe. We work with the private 
sector. The whole aim is to create innovation and build it up based on the private, 
meaning the market. 
7 
I: Are you involved in the SCWL? 
8 
I4: My colleagues are involved in it. They were part in setting up the program 
originally. We are a partner in the program as Climate-KIC. My involvement has 
been to assist in [the workshop], but it's a lot about other things than just the 
support of the start-ups.

10

innovation and in entrepreneurship is to have an international dimension. The way 
how you develop is to be connected internationally. I think it is a two-ay 
collaboration, where we can learn from Singapore and they can learn from us. 
Clearly, looking at the start-ups and the partners here, we have a strong base 
around CleanTech, the whole technology experience and also the maturity of the 
markets where we are more mature than some other areas. That is a good basis 
to collaborate within that area and I think the program and the collaboration with 
Singapore is based on the mature sector that we have here, which are interested

a strong sector and are keen to collaborate. Singapore is a first mover in Asia in 
many of these areas and I am sure within Air pollution and many areas they are 
even leading the market. I think it is an example of two strong partners working

I: Thinking about Climate-KIC, what kind of OI activities and practices do you 
have going on? 
16 
I4: A lot of our partners are cities. Cities are very complex structure but they are 
also very good focus areas as there is a clear limit where it is relevant and there is 
a strong demand coming of the people who live in it. If you look on decision making, 
compared to counties or regions, cities are in a better position and are often 
stronger in making decisions than others. We have deployed OI with our city 
partners. Starting with Copenhagen, but also with Malmö, and Stockholm. We 
have done this a lot of times. This has now developed into an OI methodology that 
we deploy for cities. We also use it within our innovation program when we work 
with universities as a way of doing calls. You can name many things OI, but for us 
it is a way to focus on a certain area and bring together demand and supply within 
the area. You could imagine that we have done more to bring start-ups into 
corporates as well. This is a field where we are looking at, and three is a lot of 
potential in it, but still we are in our early days in that field. I see a movement 
outside, where a lot of corporates are- compared to five or seven years ago they 
bothered around the whole start-up sector, now most bigger corporates have some 
kind of a program to keep an eye on what is happening and an OI approach is one 
to do that. 
17 
I: You mean like an Accelerator, incubator, or innovation lab? 
18 
I4: It is done in many different ways, but within the last three or four years we see 
a lot of accelerator programs that have emerged. You can look on all the programs 
like: Rainmaking, Danske Bank, IKEA. There are quite a lot of big corporates have 
tried to enter that type of programs. This are good examples of corporates that 
want to do something in that area. Then there are also programs that are more 
research and innovation focused, like the new Bio innovation program that Novo 
Nordisk is launching. And more kind of traditional innovation and research 
collaboration, where you invite stat-ups and researchers that take part in it. 
19 
I: When you work with a city like Copenhagen, which we would consider as 
a smart city. What are the tasks or challenges that try to solve with these 
start-ups? If Copenhagen for example asks you that they have this and that 
problem, how would this look like? 
==PAGE:245==
Transcripts of interviews 
246 
20 
I4: It depends, because cities are many things. You can put smart in front of it all. 
All bigger cities have some kind of smart district program. The whole digitalization 
technology is a key part to many solutions, this is why smart becomes trend setting. 
The challenge that we work on is defined in collaboration with the demand. It is up 
to the city to define that. What is important when you define that, is that it is not so 
broad that you cannot bring all relevant stakeholders to the table, but it should also 
not be too narrow, so that you cannot be creative around it and think about it in a 
broader sense. Our role in that is to help the challenge owner, you can say the city, 
in defining the challenge level in the right way, so that it is still open enough, but 
concise enough to make a proper call around it. I can give you an example of a 
recent one that we were running was with Malmö and two other municipalities in 
southern Sweden, that was around how can you use resistential heat from 
industries in food production. That is a very narrow topic, but still we had 30 start-
ups. If you can do a European search and a call for that, there is quite a big number 
of start-ups that are working on that. Air pollution, mobility, the typical problems 
that cities are facing can all be used as an open challenge call. 
21 
I: You have already tackled a term called triple helix collaboration. Could you 
elaborate this and how you work with other stakeholders in this triple helix 
formation? 
22 
I4: I formed a center called Copenhagen Innovation and Entrepreneurship lab, 
before I came to Climate-KIC. I realized- I was invited all the time to Brussels to 
talk about our results. I realized, that was because the politicians were looking for 
evidence that it makes sense for private-public collaboration in the field of 
innovation and entrepreneurship. At that time, I got to know about the triple helix, 
because it is more a policy discourse more than something in practice. It is from 
the united states, a professor who has created the whole term. My issue with the 
whole approach around it is that is very much a top down policy-initiated approach 
coming from policy makers. Whereas, I think there are a lot of strongholds in public-
private collaboration, but again it is really important that it is built on collaboration 
that works, where you can have this win-win situation and where you have a mutual 
trust among the partners to grow the collaboration. Rather than talking about triple 
helix, I think you should talk about the elements of collaboration and what makes 
a good collaboration. Because triple helix is just a name of a form of collaboration. 
I think organizations like Climate-KIC have struggled a lot over the years to find a 
==PAGE:246==
Transcripts of interviews 
247 
way to do this in a meaning full and efficient way. Especially, with triple helix in an 
international context. That makes things much more difficult than when you do it in 
a local context. The center that I was running before, we did university 
collaboration with start-ups and with businesses and it was very easy. We had lot 
of results, it was very fast compared to when you do it in an international context, 
it is much more difficult. In Europe we are lots of small markets, we need to scale, 
so we need to work together as one market.  
23 
I: You would rather say that we should not focus on triple helix, but on 
general collaboration. 
24 
I4: Of course, things get a name when it is something that you focus on. I am 
saying that we should try to see: what are the opportunities and barriers of 
collaborating among public and private sector and work to make that efficient and 
scalable.  
25 
I: What do you think could be the barriers? How do they manifest in such a 
collaboration with smart cities?  
26 
I4: You have the normal cultural barriers. 
27 
I: Especially in Singapore, right? 
28 
I4: Then you have different languages and so on. But even if you take the equation 
of Singapore out and just look on the private-public collaboration. What we look a 
lot on is the following. Is the timeline aligned? A start-up typically has a three to six 
months’ timeline, this is how much more time and money they have, before they 
need to move to the next goal. They have a hard funding, or milestone that is hard. 
If you work with a city, the timeline is very different, it is four or three years. For 
example, with the heat, here was formed a consortium and we had a start-up that 
was working in the area. They were in the consortium and they were actually 
leading it. After three months they came back and said that they can’t do it 
anymore, even though they were paid to do this, because they had to focus on 
their start-up. They had raised money and investments to implement it, and they 
did not know about the cities, whether they would be ready to take action on this 
within the next six or twelve months. They couldn't wait for that. So, you have to 
align timelines. And there is a lot around, that the start-ups have to be ready to 
expand. If it’s for Singapore, you have to be ready to take a big order. They have 
to be ready to enter a new market and have resources to do that. On the other 
==PAGE:247==
Transcripts of interviews 
248 
hand, the city partner needs to be ready to act on the challenge, so that they have 
a call. Not only within time, but they have to set aside resources as well. We are 
working with C40. That is a network of leading cities that have a commitment to 
move to more sustainable solutions. It is a very strong network with Paris, 
Copenhagen. With them we are doing OI programs. There is a running call with 18 
cities around the globe that have formed a challenge, that start-ups and all the 
consortiums are being formed around specific sites, building sites. The cities are 
saying that they are going to build, what will win in this. There is a strong call for 
action. To be concrete, this is very important. You can get into a consortium with a 
start-up, but if you are not able to act on it immediately within the time frame, the 
start-ups will not have time to wait. If you have a university partner, just like in the 
Singapore case, you have the whole interface between researchers and the 
business. They have different virtues and cultures and work approaches. I have 
realized, in that collaboration you can use students as a very good linkage. 
Students are faster to adapt, faster to look at opportunities. To use students that 
are doing their thesis as a way to initiate the collaboration between the corporate 
and the university can be a very good bridge. When you have a knowledge about 
each other and you have built trust, then you can create a research program, or 
innovation program on top of that. You can also look on who is successful in getting 
the big innovation programs attracted. Is it the ones that already have an 
experienced collaboration, meaning they know each other? As in any other 
relationship, it is about having the trust and the knowledge of the weaknesses and 
the strengths of each other, to be able to build something. 
29 
I: if we look at the opportunities with these collaborations, what do you think 
are the biggest opportunities for start-ups, in going into such partnerships 
or collaborations with the public? 
30 
I4: In many of the smart city district projects, it is about getting test beds for your 
product. A place to have a demonstration and maybe as well to build a brand, in a 
sense that you can say that your product is already there. Sometimes are these 
smart district projects well known of. You can use it in your PR strategy as a way 
to enter a market. Often the commercial terms and possibilities are fairly limited, 
but it is a first important move into getting into a new market or get your products 
into a setting. That is when it is a for a city. If it is for a corporate, like corporate 
start-ups, it is different. There it is more about that the start-up has an innovation 
==PAGE:248==
Transcripts of interviews 
249 
around and a technology, but they often know very little about the markets, the 
prices, the customers. In a collaboration with a corporate they can get very 
important market intelligence around how this is all working. We see some of our 
start-ups entering. One is working with E-on, a big energy provider. They have 
learned so much from entering a collaboration with them. If both parties open up 
and are vulnerable and tell each other exactly about what they know and what they 
don't know, they can help each other a lot. I think there is a great potential in the 
collaboration of big corporates and start-ups in making the whole process of 
scaling your company up much faster. Even my role, it is to help start-ups scale as 
fast as possible. In many ways, we cover a lot of different areas, you can say the 
best person to do that would be someone in the corporate sitting in that market 
helping a start-up to enter. But of course, there is often a competitive situation. You 
need to find a way where there is an opportunity for both. 
31 
I: If you summarize the opportunities with the collaboration it is testing and 
bridge making, and the innovation lab is actually the connection to do that. 
Would you agree to that? 
32 
I4: I think so. Of course, you have the dimension of getting access to an 
international market in the Singapore case. That is also why the most relevant 
partners are start-up that are mature enough to be ready to enter a new market. 
You mentioned flow loop and Aquibiq and you can say that they are not mature 
enough, but then the Singapore market represents a very key market for them, 
because their water scarcity is key there. They go to Singapore to get a test bed 
there, but for many others and also from the cohort that we as part of the program, 
they are established companies that are looking for new export possibilities. 
33 
I: You have said one thing that is very interesting in terms of building trust 
between companies or start-ups and these innovation labs by using 
students. Are there other ways to use OI practices, or to get them going and 
started?  
34 
I4: You can say that these type of programs, that facilitate the collaboration is mean 
to overcome the barriers, because it is difficult for them even to meet, if it was not 
for those programs. I think, even though you have to be realistic about the key 
assets and possibilities. You have to do a SWOT on both sides, where does it 
make sense and where is a common field where we can collaborate and where it 
==PAGE:249==
Transcripts of interviews 
250 
will be a beneficial situation for both parties, or for all parties. A barrier can be 
changed into an opportunity, it is not like you see a barrier and you cannot do 
anything about it, but you need to identify and you need to work around it. For 
example, C40. My advice was- there was a three-step development program. I 
said do not ask for- the architects and the building consortium they should form 
now, don't invite the start-ups to be part of this. Wait until you have a short list of 
the ones you have already won and then when you have identified the specifics of 
how you are going to work with it, then you should invite the start-ups to come in 
with their solutions. Then you will be much readier to work with them afterwards. 
The start-ups will then not be part of the whole concept development. In most 
cases they do not have time for this anyways. An architect firm needs to be part of 
this, because they create a lot of value, but the start-up is much more focused on 
a very particular thing. They don't want to sit and work with those bigger problems, 
they would rather come up with a solution and implement it. You can say, in that 
was a timeline and an area where we could work on. When do you need to enter 
the program? Others can start to work and when they are ready, you can invite 
them in. We have not seen the result of that yet, because it is has not happened 
yet, but I think that this could be an example.  
35 
I: Talking about BMI. Start-ups that go to Singapore from here, they probably 
have to adapt their BM, they cannot just copy and paste their BM to 
Singapore. What do start-ups, especially in the CleanTech, water and waste 
management sector have to do, in order to be successful in the context of 
Singapore, or South East Asia? 
36 
I4: I probably do not know enough about those markets. In any market you enter, 
you need to know the whole situation around that market. That may influence your 
BM and how to do it. For example, with water, water prices, who is paying for what, 
are there meters in housing complexes, who is paying and what are they paying. 
It does not matter if you are in South East Asia, this is just where you deploy your 
market. If you are going to sell to another market, you have to understand and 
make changes. 
37 
I: What do you think are the most important triggers that start-up should look 
at? 
==PAGE:250==
Transcripts of interviews 
251 
38 
I4: It depends on how mature they are. If they are- if they already have a good 
understanding of their customer value and they are already dealing with 
customers, then entering- I have an example, we have Donkey Republic in our 
program. They are now in 50 cities around Europe. They know their product well, 
half a year ago it was just a lock, now it is a business around that. For them, when 
they enter a new market, it is a lot about understanding the potential of the macro 
markets, not the individual consumer, but rather how their lock look like here, 
compared to the type of users, city and ho it is going to develop, and legal issues. 
Another company that are still in their product development phase needs to 
understand their customers in the first place. I think, when you go from a setting in 
Europe to a setting in South East Asia, you should understand the customers there 
and you should check that your customer validation.  
39 
I: So be customer centric is one thing that you would give them on their way.

I4: But that is the prerequisite of doing business. Competitors are important as well 
and so on.

their products? What is their perception? 
42 
I4:  I was just part of the pitching and I was commenting on that. To me they gave 
an impression of being realistic and most of them, probably 60 percent, were 
mature companies where I thought that they were good companies to go there. I 
understand their motivation to do that and I think they were realistic about why they 
should go there. I think the general impression was, that it was for the market 
opportunity that they represented, but also some seem to be aware about the 
challenges that they were facing with that as well. There were a handful of 
companies that were ready to do it, and probably another hand full that were not, 
but they surely will learn a lot if they were successful in getting in. 
43 
I: Would you say that some companies are not aware of the consequences 
of going international? They just go for it, because the opportunity was 
there? 
44 
I4: That is probably the danger with those type of programs. It is an opportunity to 
just be a part of a program. Ideally the start-ups should invest themselves. When 
==PAGE:251==
Transcripts of interviews 
252 
someone is paying the lunch, you can get guests in that would not be willing to pay 
if it was for themselves. I am not saying that this is what I saw, but this is one risk. 
In general, you have to be concerned about that. You attract all kind of potential 
participants. The ones who are not ready, maybe they should not have been there. 
Or they should and they learn a lot and, in a few years, they will be ready to do 
something. It depends how you look on it and what’s the success factor. I am not 
there to say that. They were all very interested to be part of the program, that is 
fair enough. It is an offer and they applied to the other.  
45 
I: One last thing about internationalization, when we think about Singapore 
it is a market that is very distant from Denmark. Why do Danish or Nordic 
companies have the idea to go to such a distant market. [Explaining Uppsala 
and Born global models]. Why do Danish companies go the Born global way 
and enter Singapore?  
46 
I4: There are some specific challenges and there is a maturity of markets, it can 
be an entering to a market, not just to Singapore, but to the Asian market. I think, 
the market of China and India is huge. Especially when you talk about Greentech 
and CleanTech technologies, it is a very interesting market. It might be the 
realization of some that have entered the market, and they see that it is actually 
difficult to have a pilot running in Singapore, when you could run it next door. That 
is why you should consider the steps on how you internationalize it. Maybe you 
should not go to Asia, before you already have a strong base in your home market. 
Maybe even the Nordics and Germany, and as soon as you have that working, you 
are ready to go. The example of Donkey republic, they also opened a collaboration 
in Asia and they did in the US and they realized that the cost benefits of that was 
nit worthwhile, so focus on Europe. Right now, they are very focused on Europe. 
It has a cost to work I a far distant market, but it also represents an opportunity. 
For some it is the right decision and for some not. 
47 
I: So, start-ups should actually analyze that before they go? 
48 
I4: Of course. That refers a bit to what I said before. Probably some of the start-
ups were ready and some were not ready, because they were not strong enough 
in their home markets. There might be even an example, or two of them, that did 
not even have a product in the home market. Does it even make sense then? But 
maybe you find an investor in Singapore that believes in your idea and this is how 
==PAGE:252==
Transcripts of interviews 
253 
you become successful. If you look on the idea that we are trying to do here, they 
might not fit. There might be many other circumstances where they might be a 
success and they go anyhow. It is up them to decide. 
49 
I: One last question about government activities, that might be a little bit 
difficult, because you are not so involved in the Singapore context. If you 
can give your personal perception about how policy makers should 
approach the planning of a smart city with focus on Singapore, but we can 
leave the Singapore context for this question out as well. 
50 
I4:  As a city policy maker, it makes sense to have a district where you have a 
particular focus on trying out new things. 
51 
I: Is that in Copenhagen Nordhavn? 
52 
I4: Yes. It gives you a visibility internationally and you can attract partners and 
corporates and start-ups, that otherwise would be hard to get in as partners. It 
gives visibility about what you do to the citizens. The city is very broad, if you can 
focus on one area. There are many good examples of cities that have deployed a 
district approach and it has been very successful in creating an area, but also 
around that meaning to create attention towards the city and what they are doing 
and the whole sustainability agenda. It makes sense to do it as a test pilot area. It 
is natural that all collaborations are open. It says it in the word itself. You wouldn't 
create a district collaboration, without your city partners, because you limit 
yourselves. You should keep it as open and accessible as possible, then you can 
attract the best possible talent. I think we have learned that in Europe now from 
the US. They have always worked- they do not care where people come from as 
long as they bring talent in. Whereas here in Europe we have a tendency to say 
that this program is for these people only. There is probably an example, where 
some of the funds coming from these projects, like the Danish industry authority 
and they say that it is only for this type of people that we want to help. The more 
generations of these types of programs are happening, you realize that it you 
should not care so much about that, you should only care about getting the best. 
If you are successful in that, your program will also be successful. You can 
experience that a Finnish start-up would relocate to Denmark when they are 
successful in a program and suddenly you have attracted talent coming from 
Finland coming to Denmark. It was a win for all. 
==PAGE:253==
Transcripts of interviews 
254 
53 
I: I am actually through with my questions, but if you would like to give other 
start-ups that want to go to Singapore or that are planning to internationalize 
in Singapore and use OI practices- this is the chance to give a final remark, 
if you want? 
54 
I4: I think it is important to know that their journey is unique. An advisor or expert, 
or program can tell them what they need. They need to assess their need by 
themselves. They have their strategy and they have to fulfill that strategy, they will 
be met with all these offers. They are the rock stars of today, the start-ups. 
Everyone wants to work with them, including myself I am also having a supply offer 
here. But they are the demand and should know where they want to go and what 
their next steps will be that are relevant to them. If they are going to join a program, 
where it is about entering the market in Singapore, they need to analyze that 
beforehand. As with any other program or activity, they need to prioritize. 
55 
I: Closing

DECLARATION OF AUTHORSHIP 
 
I hereby declare that the thesis submitted is my own unaided work. All direct or indirect 
sources used are acknowledged as references.  
 
I am aware that the thesis in digital form can be examined for the use of unauthorized 
aid and in order to determine whether the thesis as a whole or parts incorporated in it 
may be deemed as plagiarism. For the comparison of my work with existing sources I 
agree that it shall be entered in a database where it shall also remain after examination, 
to enable comparison with future theses submitted. Further rights of reproduction and 
usage, however, are not granted here. 
 
This paper was not previously presented to another examination board and has not been 
published. 
 
 
Copenhagen, the 22nd of June 2018
